The 14-time major champion, who underwent two surgeries during a 15-month hiatus, trails fellow American JB Holmes by nine shots. At 40, Woods had four birdies in the first eight holes but faltered with a bogey on the ninth due to a poor drive and chip. He added another shot on the 11th and suffered double bogeys on the 16th and 18th, finishing one over par. Woods expressed satisfaction after completing his round, hosted by him to support his charitable foundation, stating he felt "pretty good" and looking forward to the upcoming days. On Sky Sports, he joked, "I hit the ball in three bushes and the water, so it could have been something really good." He noted a strong start followed by errors, particularly on par fives, and mentioned difficulties with awkward shots. Woods highlighted the advantage of driving well on this course, while emphasizing the consequences of poor drives, which can result in being caught in bushes or rocks. His first double bogey came from a pulled approach on the 16th fairway, while his second double bogey occurred after hitting his drive into a lake on the 18th. Despite leading briefly with three consecutive birdies from the sixth hole, Woods' round concluded with two double bogeys. Holmes led the 18-player field with an eight-under-par 64, featuring seven birdies and an eagle. Hideki Matsuyama and US Open winner Dustin Johnson were both one shot behind at seven under, while Open champion Henrik Stenson, American Matt Kuchar, and South African Louis Oosthuizen were tied for fourth on five under. England's Justin Rose, returning from a seven-week break, was the last in the field at two over.
The official ONA news agency stated that the three Americans were flown out of Yemen's capital, Sanaa, on Wednesday. Sanaa is controlled by the Houthi rebel group, and the US government had asked Oman for assistance in their release. The Yemeni ministry did not provide details about the Americans' identities, but a security official from Yemen informed Reuters that they had been detained by the Houthi forces. According to the official, the Americans were suspected of espionage. In early September, two other Americans who had been held hostage by the Houthis for several months were freed and transported to Oman along with a British citizen and three Saudis. The Houthis, who expelled Yemen's government from Sanaa earlier this year, are currently under attack from a multinational coalition led by Saudi Arabia, with support from the US. While not part of the coalition, Oman has actively engaged in peace negotiations. The conflict has resulted in at least 5,700 deaths, including around half who were civilians, since late March, according to the UN.

Previously, he threatened to wage "all-out war" against the RFL if found culpable. "I'm in discussions with several other chairmen to organize a meeting and present a no-confidence vote on the RFL's leadership," stated Koukash. "They are not suitable for the job. We pour millions into rugby league and aim for its best interests. "Over the past four years, I've been involved in the sport, and commercially, it has regressed during this period." For the millionaire racehorse owner Koukash to convene an emergency meeting and propose a no-confidence motion, he needs support from at least six out of the 12 Super League clubs. The RFL responded, saying, "We exist to serve the clubs and everyone interested in rugby league. Our governance provides a structured and democratic process for clubs to express their concerns, and we always listen to them. I've only spoken to those directly affected—bosses and managers at a major appliance company, and stock-market analysts. As I continue to interact with more people, my perspective on economic changes here will evolve. However, there's relief that the U.S. Federal Reserve has postponed its plan to raise interest rates, thus maintaining the era of historically low interest rates on dollar debts. This decision follows the 2008 financial crisis, and given that more than $9 trillion in dollar-denominated debt has been issued globally since then, any rate hike can significantly affect borrowing costs worldwide. While Fed Chair Janet Yellen and her colleagues did not delay the rate increase due to concerns about Chinese markets, they acknowledged that the current fragile state of markets and economies outside the U.S., particularly the slowdown in China, influenced their decision. A drop in share prices due to global economic uncertainties and the strengthening dollar (up 15% over the past year) have already tightened monetary conditions in the U.S. Essentially, China’s reduced growth rate—from 10% to around 7%, and potentially lower—has made American investors feel less wealthy and reduced sales for American exporters. Yellen argued that this indirect impact from China is roughly equivalent to a slight rate hike. If inflation remains a concern in the U.S., China's slowdown has somewhat alleviated that pressure. However, two concerns persist. First, heightened anxiety over China’s economic situation might lead to further market volatility. Second, the prolonged period of ultra-low interest rates, which have fueled a global debt bubble since 2008, raises fears that a sudden rate increase could cause a catastrophic deflation, rather than a gentle correction.

In 1987, Daniel Morgan was discovered dead in a London pub car park. Five police investigations later, no one has been convicted of his murder. His brother, Alastair Morgan, is collaborating with investigative journalist Peter Jukes to recount the story through weekly podcast episodes. Alastair stated, "The full narrative of my brother's murder hasn't been presented coherently, making it hard for people to grasp what truly occurred." He added, "This is an outrageous case, and I want the public to learn what happened." When Daniel's body was found in March 1987, he had been attacked with an axe after drinking at the Golden Lion pub in Sydenham. Persistent police failures to secure convictions and allegations of corruption and cover-ups surrounded the case. An independent panel has now been set up to reassess the circumstances of Daniel's death and the police investigation. Alastair, from Llanfrechfa near Cwmbran, was 37 years old at the time of his death. Peter Jukes, who is presenting the podcast, expressed astonishment upon learning the details during his research. "We hope to encourage witnesses to come forward and help piece together the story," Jukes said. "There's a wealth of open-source material available, allowing people to form their own conclusions." The podcast team plans to revisit the murder site with Alastair and utilize the podcast format to explore complex stories. Jukes highlighted the podcast's potential, noting, "Podcasts are fantastic for in-depth storytelling; they offer flexibility and a singular voice." The producers aim to match the success of Serial, a podcast that investigates a single true story over multiple episodes. The first series of Serial questioned the conviction of an American man who murdered his high school girlfriend. The Daniel Morgan Murder podcast will be released to subscribers before Christmas, with broader availability starting in the new year.
Elmbridge Museum announced its "museum without walls" approach would offer its services in a more accessible, relevant, and cost-effective manner. The last opportunity to visit the gallery in Weybridge was on Saturday, while the website was launched on Sunday. Elmbridge Council stated that Surrey County Council owns the museum building and will decide its future. The borough council highlighted that the new website provides a fresh, interactive online experience representing the museum comprehensively and catering to diverse audiences. It features an "object in focus" section with detailed information on selected items and a "what we're up to" section detailing projects by the museum team, now based at the Civic Centre in Esher. Actual artifacts will continue to be exhibited at temporary shows across the borough, and the museum will conduct a schools program in September. Currently, two exhibitions are taking place at Thames Ditton Library and Walton Library. The museum's collection has been transferred to a dedicated storage facility, where artifacts can be viewed by appointment.
Scarborough Hospital, grappling with financial and care quality issues, has reached out to York Hospital for a potential partnership. This move stems from the government’s mandate that all hospitals become foundation trusts by 2013. Scarborough’s board acknowledged it cannot meet the necessary standards independently, given its £4 million debt and recent criticism from NHS regulators. While they could seek assistance from various sources, they prefer York Hospital, which already holds foundation trust status. A spokesperson from the Scarborough and North East Yorkshire NHS Trust stated, "To achieve foundation trust status, hospital trusts must meet stringent clinical and financial criteria. Our likelihood of meeting these requirements by 2013 is slim, and lacking this status would hinder our ability to ensure the sustainability of our services." At their recent meeting, the board explored options to secure sustainable healthcare services for Scarborough and its surrounding areas amid a challenging financial landscape and evolving health policies. They decided to approach York Hospital Foundation Trust to investigate partnership possibilities. Patrick Crowley, CEO of York Teaching Hospital NHS Foundation Trust, noted, "Achieving foundation trust status involves meeting high standards for both clinical performance and financial management. We are collaborating with the Scarborough trust and other health partners to find a path forward." York has not yet formally responded to the approach, but both parties hope to build on existing collaborations to serve the local population.
The letter criticizes the "Dubs resettlement scheme" for its lack of planning and inadequate sharing of useful information. Approximately 200 children entered the UK under this scheme last year, and none arrived in 2017, according to the Home Office. The UK government pledged to honor its commitments to refugees. Named after campaigner Lord Dubs, the "Dubs amendment" mandated assistance to unaccompanied child refugees who arrived in Europe without family connections. The UK agreed to take up to 480 such children but plans to close the scheme, citing concerns about encouraging people trafficking. A joint letter from Scottish and Welsh equalities ministers, Angela Constance and Carl Sargeant, highlights issues with the scheme's management. They note that only a few hundred of the 480 planned placements have been fulfilled and express frustration with insufficient information from coordinators. Immigration Minister Brandon Lewis affirmed the government's commitment to the Dubs scheme and emphasized other mechanisms for aiding vulnerable children. He stated that while they aim to fulfill commitments, the most effective way to help the most vulnerable children is through direct resettlement from conflict zones, which helps prevent traffickers from exploiting them.
The Scottish Environment Protection Agency (SEPA) stated that Dawnfresh had used azamethiphos excessively at one of its Loch Etive sites. Azamethiphos is an organophosphorus pesticide used against sea lice. Dawnfresh acknowledged "a few minor oversights" and assured SEPA they are working to minimize their environmental impact. The conservation group Friends of Loch Etive reported the rule violation. Last year, Dawnfresh produced around 3,000 tonnes of rainbow trout at Loch Etive. SEPA noted that consecutive azamethiphos treatments at Etive 6 were conducted within 24 hours of the initial treatment, violating the site's CAR license. SEPA will continue to investigate. Scottish fish farms, like Dawnfresh, employ various methods, including chemical treatments, to manage sea lice. Dawnfresh’s Farming Director Stewart Hawthorn highlighted that the company achieved historically low sea lice levels last year through proactive measures. However, there were six instances where technical treatment standards were not met. Without these minor errors, SEPA said the site would have been rated 'excellent'. Hawthorn emphasized their commitment to reducing environmental impact, noting that three of their four Loch Etive sites achieved 'excellent' or 'good' ratings. They plan to introduce new treatments to lessen chemical dependency.

Staff at Claythorpe Watermill near Alford, Lincolnshire, noted that Stan, the cockerel, was known for chasing toddlers and pecking at ankles. With the busy summer season ahead, they suggested he find a new home. Owner Rebecca Agate wished for visitors to enjoy a peaceful experience. She stated, "Many small children visit our park, and while Stan can be friendly, he can also be intimidating when chasing or pecking." To avoid negative memories, she hoped someone could provide Stan with a new home. Agate described Stan as having "a lot of personality and enjoying human company," but also mentioned his grumpy side. There have been similar incidents involving aggressive birds; last year, a pheasant in Cambridgeshire terrorized vehicles and pets, and another pheasant named Phil in Shropshire prompted a family member to use a badminton racket for defense. Claythorpe Watermill and Wildfowl Gardens houses various animals such as waterfowl, wallabies, chipmunks, peacocks, and a family of otters.
Approximately 20,000 Syrians are anticipated to be resettled in the UK over the next five years. Discussions focused on concerns regarding housing, education, and healthcare, along with funding additional services. The Local Government Association has advocated for increased resources to assist member councils in accommodating refugees, rather than relying on their limited housing stock. According to BBC correspondent Daniel Sandford, the UK plans to resettle around 400 refugees each month, selected by the United Nations Refugee Agency (UNHCR). The process is set to begin in October. Sandford noted, "Local authorities are generally reluctant to use their own council housing, so efforts are being made to find private landlords willing to accommodate Syrian refugees, with costs covered by the state." Prime Minister David Cameron has stated that these individuals will come from camps near Syria, rather than those already present in Europe. Unlike other asylum seekers, they will be permitted to start working immediately and will be eligible for benefits. David Simmons from the Local Government Association emphasized the need for long-term support, saying, "It's not just about short-term finances; we need assurance that resources will be available to support families and children as they integrate into UK life, focusing on education, employment, and housing opportunities." Meanwhile, central European ministers have once again declined a mandatory quota system for distributing migrants. In recent weeks, tens of thousands of migrants have attempted to reach Europe from war-torn Syria and Libya, many traveling through Hungary to wealthier EU countries like Germany, Austria, and Sweden, which have more lenient asylum policies. European Council President Donald Tusk has indicated that he will convene an emergency summit later in the month if a resolution to the crisis is not reached soon.

The country's Purchasing Managers Index (PMI) dropped below expectations to 50.8 from 51.1 in the prior month. Any figure over 50 indicates expansion. Analysts had anticipated a slight increase in growth. Recent data revealed that China's broader economy expanded at its slowest rate in over five years—7.3% year-over-year in July to September, down from 7.5% in the preceding quarter. "Economic pressures remain, and monetary policy will likely stay accommodative," analysts at China International Capital noted after the PMI report was released on Saturday. Beijing has introduced measures to spur consumer spending, such as easing restrictions on property purchases and infusing billions into major banks. The central bank has also reduced certain inter-bank interest rates. The government targets 7.5% economic growth for the year but many expect it to fall short. Speculation suggests further steps to boost growth could be taken. Maintaining such robust growth is crucial to create sufficient employment for China's large and increasingly educated workforce. However, these growth aspirations significantly exceed the modest rates seen in more developed economies, as evidenced by the U.S. economy's 3.5% annual growth rate.
UK economist David Tinsley at UBS stated that Brexit would result in "significantly reduced growth, a substantial drop in the pound, and additional easing measures from the Bank of England." He anticipates two interest rate cuts from the central bank over the next six months, lowering rates from the current historical low of 0.5% to zero. Interest rates have remained at 0.5% since 2009, as the UK has struggled to recover from the 2008 financial crisis. UBS also predicts that the Brexit vote will delay interest rate hikes in the US. Previously, economists believed that Janet Yellen, the chair of the US Federal Reserve, would raise rates in September; however, UBS now expects this to be postponed. Tinsley forecasts that UK growth rates could fall to zero in the latter part of the year, down from approximately 2% previously. In the long term, there could be more significant damage. The outcome largely hinges on future trade negotiations and the extent of the UK's access to the European single market. According to Tinsley, "Brexit could potentially reduce long-term UK GDP by around 3.0%" in a scenario with limited access.
At 84, Gregg survived the 1958 Munich air disaster that claimed 23 lives, including eight of his teammates. Reflecting on the incident, he shared, "When news broke, I asked my wife if I could be alone in the TV room; I didn’t want anyone around." He continued, "It was extremely hard to process." In a statement to his charity's Facebook page, Gregg recounted being contacted by a grieving Brazilian journalist following the recent tragedy in Colombia. This event, which killed 71 people, including 19 Chapecoense players, resonated deeply with him. Gregg noted, "At Munich, I lost friends and teammates, but there were others on board, like one of the country’s top journalists at the time." The Brazilian journalist told Gregg, "Harry Gregg, I'm so glad you remembered everyone who died; I lost three sports journalists on that flight." Gregg emphasized, "Just like at Munich, the focus often centers on the players, but we mustn’t forget the others who perished." He assisted in rescuing people from the burning plane in Munich, including a mother and child, and remained at the scene. Following the Colombia crash, six survivors were found near Medellin, though goalkeeper Jackson Follmann lost his right leg. Gregg found returning to training post-Munich crucial for his mental well-being. A few months after the Munich disaster, he was honored as the world's best goalkeeper for his performance with Northern Ireland at the 1958 World Cup in Sweden.
Rafael Marques de Morais accused seven generals of involvement in murders, torture, and land seizures within Angola's lucrative diamond sector. Protesters were reportedly detained for opposing the trial. De Morais, a longtime critic of President Jose Eduardo dos Santos' 35-year reign, previously spent 43 days in jail, including 11 in solitary confinement, in 1999 following the publication of his article, "The Lipstick of the Dictatorship," in a private Angolan newspaper. Analyst Mary Harper from BBC Africa notes that dissent is typically suppressed in Angola, where critics may face imprisonment, bribery, or disappearance. In response to de Morais' book, "Blood Diamonds: Torture and Corruption in Angola," the generals' lawyer, Joao Manuel, stated that there was no connection between the military and the alleged crimes. Judge Adriano Cerveira announced the trial would be conducted in secret at the Luanda courthouse. Outside, clashes erupted between police and demonstrators chanting "Free Rafael" and "Imprison the Generals." De Morais was awarded a freedom of expression prize by Index on Censorship while in the UK, prompting the organization to call for the charges to be dropped. Prior to the trial, de Morais told the BBC's Focus on Africa that Angola's leaders enjoy Western luxuries but label critics as "agents of imperialism." A conviction could result in up to nine years in prison and a fine of $1.2 million. The unregulated diamond trade fueled Angola's 27-year civil war, which concluded in 2002. Post-conflict, the country, a significant African oil producer, experienced economic growth, yet critics argue the benefits have primarily accrued to a select few.
Pavel Durov, who led VKontakte, had earlier announced his departure but later retracted his resignation. The company refuted this claim. Durov had previously resisted Russian government demands to censor content on his platform. In a statement, he revealed he learned of his dismissal through media reports: "Today, I was terminated as the general director of VKontakte. It’s curious that the shareholders chose not to act directly, and it was the press that informed me of my termination." He noted that VKontakte would now be fully controlled by Igor Sechin and Alisher Usmanov. "Seven and a half years of work have come to this," Durov remarked, adding, "We accomplished much, and some of our achievements cannot be reversed." Sechin heads state-owned Rosneft, while Usmanov, Russia's wealthiest person as per Forbes, made his fortune in iron and steel and once held a stake in Facebook; he owns a significant share in VKontakte through his Mail.ru company. Durov publicly resigned on April Fool's Day but clarified it was a prank. Interfax reported that VKontakte acted on Durov's March 21 resignation because he hadn't withdrawn it within the required one-month period. In a TechCrunch interview, Durov stated he wasn't in Russia and had no plans to return, citing the current incompatibility between the country and internet business. He had previously refused to comply with Russian authorities' requests to provide user data related to anti-government protests and to shut down a pro-opposition group. Selling his shares, Durov maintained, allowed him to continue making principled decisions. Reports indicate a new leader for VKontakte will be selected at the next board meeting.

John Cleese drew inspiration for his TV sitcom Fawlty Towers from his stay at the Gleneagles Hotel in Torquay with the Monty Python team in 1973. The hotel, which has 41 bedrooms, ceased operations earlier this year and is now set to be converted into 36 retirement apartments by Churchill Retirement Living, following approval from Torbay Council. Initially, the application was rejected due to concerns about its size and suitability for the area, but a revised plan was subsequently accepted. This new plan involves demolishing the three-star hotel and constructing 21 one-bedroom and 11 two-bedroom apartments, along with a guest suite and communal lounges. The change of use from tourist accommodation to residential was approved because the hotel was deemed commercially unviable. Fawlty Towers, which aired for only 12 episodes but was later ranked number one in the British Film Institute's list of 100 Greatest Television Programmes in 2000, was based on the Gleneagles Hotel.

Each year, approximately 70 individuals lose their lives, and hundreds of thousands sustain severe injuries due to fires in appliances such as cookers, fridges, and tumble dryers. The Electrical Safety Council (ESC) advocates that manufacturers must enhance their customer tracking methods to promptly address potential safety issues. They argue that existing guidelines are inadequate. In December 2022, Catherine Paterson from Middlesbrough experienced a fire that ravaged her garage; the blaze originated in a newly acquired tumble dryer. After researching online, she discovered that her model was defective and had been recalled by the manufacturer. "Around ten minutes after turning on the dryer, we received a call from our neighbor alerting us that the garage was ablaze," she recounted to the BBC. The intense flames consumed the garage within 15 minutes, resulting in £10,000 in damages. "The company knew about the defect by late November, yet no one reached out to us until early February, two months after the incident," she noted. According to Trading Standards, the manufacturer had indeed taken steps to inform the public about the faulty dryer through media and online alerts. However, Steven Curtler, the product safety manager at the ESC, contends that these measures are insufficient. He is pushing for revised procedures to bolster consumer safety, particularly emphasizing the need for better product traceability. "A key area for improvement is making it easier to track products," Curtler stated. The ESC recommends collecting more data on buyers at the time of purchase to facilitate timely communication about product safety. Statistics from the ESC indicate that annually, 70 people die and 350,000 are seriously injured by household appliances including fridge-freezers, gas cookers, and washing machines. In 2011, a tragic incident in north London claimed the lives of a mother and her five children when a fridge freezer ignited. The London Fire Brigade highlighted that fridge freezers pose the greatest risk among household appliances due to the highly flammable foam insulation at the rear. They advised that any unusual sounds from a fridge or freezer should prompt immediate contact with the manufacturer or an electrical repair expert.

Cadets, troops, and veterans were presented with the traditional Welsh symbol, wrapped in a red thread and dipped in gold. As Colonel-in-Chief, the Queen expressed great pleasure in celebrating St David's Day with the regiment. She noted that soldiers now find her less intimidating as she has aged, suggesting she has become "less frightening." Dressed in a heather wool tweed coat by Karl Ludwig, the Queen stood on the parade square dais at Lucknow Barracks in Tidworth, Wiltshire, to take the royal salute before the national anthem. She inspected the parade seated in the back of a Range Rover and later met the regiment’s goats, Shenkin and Llywelyn. "I am also pleased to present leeks to representatives of the regiment, from cadets to veterans, and to welcome the new mascot, Llywelyn," she said. "The British Army, perhaps more than any other, has thrived through regimental bonds and the pride and camaraderie of its members, who would rather die than dishonor their corps or fail those who served before them under their colors." This sentiment is reflected in your regiment's motto, "Death Rather Than Dishonor." "I see this same pride and camaraderie today and am confident that the regiment is in capable hands. I wish you all success in the future." The Royal Welsh is one of the lead armored infantry battalions of the British Army, established on St David's Day, March 1, 2006. The Queen has long been associated with The Royal Welsh and its predecessors, The Royal Welch Fusiliers and The Royal Regiment of Wales, since her coronation.
On Wednesday, a Mumbai court sentenced Khan to five years in prison for running over Noor Ullah Khan, who was sleeping on the pavement. The Bombay High Court granted him interim bail for two days pending an appeal against the verdict. Noor Ullah Khan, 38, was one of five people hit; three were severely injured, while another sustained minor injuries when Khan's Toyota Land Cruiser struck the American Express bakery in Mumbai's Bandra area on September 28, 2002. Khan claimed his driver was at the wheel, but the judge ruled that Khan was driving and intoxicated. After receiving the bail, Khan returned home, where several Bollywood stars visited him. Reports indicate that Khan will not need to appear in court on Friday for his bail hearing. The case has captivated Bollywood and India for years. Judge DW Deshpande, in his 240-page judgment, noted that Khan "failed to visit the injured or offer assistance" and "could have reported to the police promptly if he had committed no wrongdoing."

The Queen Street Mill Museum in Burnley, along with four other Lancashire County Council-owned museums including Helmshore Mills, will close, though they will remain accessible for school visits. These museums represent the last operational example of Lancashire's cotton spinning and weaving traditions. The council aims to cut £65 million by 2018. Councillor Marcus Johnstone, responsible for environment, planning, and cultural services, stated, "While closing museums is not ideal, the scale of our financial challenges necessitates these tough choices to protect essential services for our community's most vulnerable members." Other museums set to close are the Museum of Lancashire in Preston, Fleetwood’s Maritime Museum, and the Judges' Lodgings in Lancaster. Initially planned for closure in spring, the decision was delayed to facilitate talks with potential operators. The council continues discussions, and over 10,000 signatures supported a petition to preserve the Queen Street and Helmshore Mills sites as crucial elements of Lancashire’s industrial heritage.
The Foster for Fife Choir is performing a song penned by local composer Ian Hammond Brown, titled "Help a Child Belong." This song encourages people to offer a home to a child during a vulnerable period in their life. The choir has been singing in various venues across Fife. It's estimated that around 800 additional foster families are needed in Scotland over the next year to meet the demand. Rae Ormiston from Fife Council’s children and families department noted, "We continuously seek innovative methods to recruit and raise awareness among the general public about fostering." She added, "Across Scotland, there's a decline in people stepping forward, leading us to publicize our need more frequently." Nikki Perry, a foster mum for 17 years who has cared for over 100 children, hopes the choir will highlight how rewarding fostering can be. She stated, "It's a profound experience for everyone, watching the children grow, find stability, and eventually have their own families. If I could, I'd take in many more." Brooklyn, a nine-year-old choir member, shared how finding a foster family positively impacted his life, saying, "I have a place to stay and a family who cares for me. They're always kind to me." The Foster for Fife Choir is believed to be the first of its kind in Scotland.
Knight Frank reported that the global count of ultra-high-net-worth individuals surged by 6,340 to reach 193,490 in 2016, reversing a previous decline. This recovery was attributed to robust stock market performances. The study defined these individuals as those with over $30 million in net assets. Despite political upheavals like Brexit and the U.S. election, many developed economies remained resilient, with U.S. and U.K. stock markets reaching record highs near year-end. Andrew Amoils, head of research at New World Wealth, noted that while uncertainties prevailed, many economies showed positive regulatory and policy changes fostering economic growth. Knight Frank projected a 43% increase in the number of ultra-high-net-worth individuals globally over the next decade, with significant regional disparities. They forecasted faster growth in the U.S. compared to the U.K., 12% in Europe, and a staggering 91% in Asia. By 2026, Asia is expected to nearly match the U.S., with just 7,068 fewer ultra-wealthy individuals. Asian cities like Singapore, Shanghai, and Beijing were anticipated to surpass current wealth hubs such as San Francisco. Additionally, several African nations, including Ethiopia, Rwanda, and Tanzania, were predicted to experience substantial increases in their wealthy populations.

Hunt took the lead after the first two rounds on Friday and maintained it until the second-to-last dive on Sunday. However, he over-rotated during his final routine, losing his medal chances. Steve LoBue from the United States secured the gold, followed by Michal Navratil from the Czech Republic and Alessandro de Rose from Italy. Hunt’s concluding dive—three forward somersaults with four and a half twists—carried the highest degree of difficulty score of 5.6. "I was upset about missing out on the gold medal," Hunt said to BBC Sport, "but I didn’t want to come across as invincible. This experience will drive me to improve." Hunt had previously won medals at every World Aquatics Championships featuring high diving, including silver at the sport’s debut in Barcelona 2013 and gold in Kazan 2015. Blake Aldridge, a fellow British diver, placed ninth. Despite leading the standings by 12 points before the final dive, Aldridge struggled with his entry. "It’s devastating to almost make it and then miss it on the last dive," Aldridge told BBC Sport. This year was challenging for Aldridge, who had back injuries and near-misses during competitions. He is now dealing with an abductor tear that hampers his twisting ability but remains committed to returning stronger next season.

Researchers from the University of Edinburgh discovered that Scots aged 45 to 54 typically sit for about 7.8 hours daily during weekdays. This is slightly more than the 7.4 hours spent being sedentary by those over 75. Sedentary activities include sitting for work, eating, watching TV, or using a computer. The study noted that excessive sitting poses significant health risks, such as earlier mortality, heart diseases, type 2 diabetes, and certain cancers, regardless of overall physical activity levels. Only young adults aged 16 to 24 are less sedentary than the over-75s during weekdays. Data from over 14,000 individuals in Scotland, sourced from the 2012-14 Scottish Health Survey, were used by researchers from the University of Edinburgh’s Physical Activity for Health Research Centre, which was published in the Journal of Sports Sciences. On weekends, the pattern shifts: those aged 25 to 54 are the least sedentary, spending around 5.2 to 5.7 hours sitting, while the over-75s are the most sedentary, clocking 7.3 to 7.4 hours. Younger adults spend most of their sitting time in front of screens, particularly TVs. Men decrease their screen time as they age, reaching a peak in middle age. Tessa Strain, the lead researcher, stated, "Significant portions of the population are excessively sedentary, a problem we have not fully recognized. We must address high levels of sedentary behavior in younger and middle-aged adults, as this is when habits might form. Workplace changes seem like a logical starting point due to the substantial amount of time spent sitting there."

On Monday, Chinese media outlets persisted in their criticisms, cautioning Ms. Tsai and her Democratic Progressive Party (DPP) against any moves toward independence. Beijing views Taiwan as a separated province and has stated it might use force to reclaim it if needed. Prior to her victory, Ms. Tsai had spoken about maintaining the current status quo. However, some analysts argue that her rhetoric has become more assertive since then, as evidenced by her statement that any form of suppression would harm cross-strait stability. The DPP defeated the ruling Kuomintang (KMT), which had fostered closer ties with China, on Saturday. On Monday, Ms. Tsai met with former US Deputy Secretary of State William Burns and pledged to maintain strong relations with the US, especially economically. A Reuters report mentioned that DPP Secretary General Joseph Wu planned to travel to the US. Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe also congratulated Ms. Tsai, referring to Taiwan as an "old friend." Ms. Tsai has established a taskforce to manage the power transition, marking Taiwan’s third such change in its democratic history. In response to the election result, Chinese state media criticized Taiwan for its "illusion" of independence. A Global Times article on Monday suggested that Ms. Tsai's victory was due to the unsatisfactory performance of the previous KMT-led administration rather than her pro-independence stance. Ms. Tsai stated, "Based on the existing ROC constitutional framework, and adhering to democratic principles and the majority public opinion, I will promote cross-strait policies. I will work to find a mutually acceptable way for Taiwan and mainland China to interact and ensure no provocations occur." She also emphasized that both sides of the strait share the responsibility to find reciprocal and dignified means of interaction. Ms. Tsai asserted that the election results reflect the will of the Taiwanese people and stressed the importance of respecting Taiwan’s democratic system, national identity, and international standing. Any form of suppression could jeopardize the stability of cross-strait relations.
At Ocado's automated warehouse in Andover, Hampshire, robots select crates with specific items that form customer orders. These crates are currently handed over to human teams for final selection, but plans include robots taking over this role. Additionally, a humanoid maintenance robot named SecondHands is under development, designed to assist human colleagues in maintaining the warehouse. According to Ocado spokesman Alexandru Voica, the fruit and vegetable picker is part of a five-year EU-funded research collaboration between five European universities and Disney, known as Soma (Soft Manipulation). This prototype aims to mimic the human hand using air pressure to control its fingers. The device combines computer vision for recognizing products with the precision needed for gripping. Currently, only the gripper is showcased, but it will eventually learn to differentiate between ripe fruits and other items requiring delicate handling, such as wine bottles and detergents. Voica noted that fruit and vegetables present the greatest challenge due to their fragility. The success of the robot in picking these items is crucial, as customers expect high quality regardless of who performs the task. Professor Chris Melhuish, director of the Bristol Robotics Laboratory, emphasized the complexity of creating versatile robotic devices capable of performing various tasks, highlighting the importance of understanding and replicating the flexibility and adaptability of the human hand.

Before May's general election, Miss Blackwood, now the Conservative MP for Oxford West and Abingdon, disclosed that she has Ehlers-Danlos syndrome. This genetic condition impacts joints and muscles, often causing persistent pain. She is advocating to lessen the "stigma" associated with "chronic illnesses." Diagnosed with the condition two years ago due to joint pain, migraines, and asthma, she chose to come forward during the campaign as she believed it was "right" for people to know about her illness. Her aim was to demonstrate that individuals with hidden conditions can still perform "stressful, high-profile jobs." Miss Blackwood stated, "It's crucial to clarify that while it's chronic, Ehlers-Danlos syndrome is not terminal. With the right management, like dietary adjustments and Pilates, one can lead a normal life and continue with their responsibilities." She emphasized, "In our country, there's still a significant stigma attached to disabilities and chronic illnesses, and we need to overcome it."

In October 2016, Communities Secretary Sajid Javid approved Cuadrilla's plans for shale gas extraction at its Preston New Road site in Little Plumpton. The Preston New Road Action Group (PNRAG) argued that this decision was "fundamentally flawed" and an "abuse of power." A legal challenge is scheduled for March 15 in Manchester. In 2015, Lancashire County Council had denied permission due to concerns about noise and traffic, but Cuadrilla appealed to the secretary of state, who ruled in their favor. This decision allows for horizontal fracking of UK shale rock for the first time, anticipated to produce more gas. Cuadrilla began construction work at the site in January, and the area has seen ongoing protests from anti-fracking campaigners. Claire Stephenson of PNRAG stated that Javid's decision "is not democracy in action but a top-down abuse of power to benefit a corporation over community desires." She emphasized that neither the secretary of state nor local councils supported the application. The Department for Communities and Local Government did not comment on the impending case. The hearing will take place at Manchester's Civil Justice Centre on March 15.
The Prison Officers Association (POA) reported an "ongoing disturbance" at the category B training prison, HMP Swaleside, located on the Isle of Sheppey. The Prison Service stated that specialist "Tornado" teams had been dispatched to address the unrest. POA chairman Mike Rolfe indicated that the extent of the disturbance was uncertain but suggested it was not as severe as the riot at HMP Birmingham last week. The riot in Birmingham, which lasted 12 hours and involved hundreds of inmates, was managed with the assistance of riot police. A Prison Service spokesperson noted that 60 prisoners were involved in the incident at Swaleside, which was confined to one landing on A Wing. The rest of the prison remained secure, with all prisoners in locked cells. The spokesperson later added, "The situation at the prison remains contained and is being resolved." Mr. Rolfe highlighted that Swaleside was "particularly challenging" due to staffing shortages and high violence levels. An inmate who contacted the BBC alleged that the disturbance began when staff conducted a cell raid and confiscated possessions. He attributed rising tensions between inmates and officers to the introduction of younger, less experienced staff and reduced family visitation. The BBC's home affairs correspondent, Danny Shaw, reported that although the disturbance was contained, prisoners were refusing to return to their cells. "Tornado" squads were also used to manage the riot in Birmingham last week, where inmates had set fires, stolen keys, and one prisoner suffered a serious injury. Following the Birmingham riot, up to 240 prisoners were relocated, but the Ministry of Justice did not comment on any movements to Swaleside. During a recent inspection, Eric Allison, a Guardian prison correspondent, learned from the governor that Swaleside was a "troubled jail" with high levels of violence and access to formerly legal highs. Despite these issues, Allison noted that the prison had some "innovative" education programs. A July report by HM Inspectorate of Prisons described Swaleside as "dangerous" and found violence levels to be "far too high," with many incidents classified as serious. On Thursday, former Deputy Prime Minister Nick Clegg, former Conservative Home Secretary Ken Clarke, and former Labour Home Secretary Jacqui Smith wrote to the Times calling for a reduction in the prison population of England and Wales to 45,000. They argued that jails had become "unacceptably dangerous" and cited recent violent unrest at HMP Birmingham as a "wake-up call
Rory Graham, performing under the name Rag N Bone Man, triumphed over fellow newcomers Anne-Marie and Dua Lipa to win the Critics' Choice award. This victory followed his chart entry just a week prior with the heartfelt soul ballad "Human." "I thought they had made a mistake," he admitted. "I was up against two pop giants, and now I'm absolutely thrilled." The Critics' Choice award, often a harbinger of success, has previously been awarded to Adele, Sam Smith, and Emeli Sande. Since its inception in 2008, all winners except last year’s Jack Garratt have achieved platinum status for their debut albums; Garratt’s first record is currently certified silver, signifying 60,000 album sales. Hailing from Uckfield near Brighton, Rag N Bone Man began his career as a jungle MC before transitioning to singing. Inspired by blues legends such as John Lee Hooker, his music blends soulful melodies with hip-hop beats and gospel influences. In 2012, Joan Armatrading supported him after his girlfriend submitted his early recordings, including videos of him singing in the bathroom. “I didn’t know about it,” he recalled to his local newspaper, “but I’m glad she sent them.” His current single, "Human," topped the German charts for ten consecutive weeks and entered the UK top 40 after a cover by Emily Middlemas on X Factor. His debut album is set for release in February, and he is also nominated for the BBC's Sound of 2017. He announced a headline tour for April, including two shows at London's Shepherd's Bush Empire. The Critics' Choice award is selected by a panel comprising music journalists and programmers from major TV and radio stations, considering more than 100 artists for the shortlist. Rag N Bone Man will perform on the Brit Awards launch show live on ITV on January 14. The main ceremony is scheduled for February 24 at the O2 Arena in London, hosted by Michael Buble, who is currently facing personal challenges.
The city council has announced that transforming the Robinson Centre in Castlereagh and the Brook Activity Centre in Twinbrook will cost around £35 million. Additionally, councillors approved plans to convert Windsor House, the city’s tallest office building, into the Grand Central Hotel, which will have 304 rooms, up from 200. The Belfast City Council also revealed that the Robinson Centre, previously closed in 2015 due to asbestos concerns, will be rebuilt into a modern facility with an eight-lane pool and leisure amenities at a cost of £20 million. The Brook Activity Centre, which will receive an £15 million facelift, will emphasize outdoor activities, including a new Gaelic football pitch, two football pitches, and multiple five-a-side pitches. These projects are part of a larger £105 million initiative by the city council to enhance leisure facilities across Belfast. Councillor Peter Johnston, chair of the planning committee, stated that these redevelopments represent an investment in the city's and its residents' health and well-being. Construction on these projects is expected to commence this summer, with the facilities becoming operational early in 2019.
Section 18C of Australia’s Racial Discrimination Act prohibits insulting someone based on their race. Senator David Leyonhjelam, a libertarian, argues that this section infringes on free speech and should be abolished. However, he filed a complaint under the same law to make a point. In an article, newspaper columnist Mark Kenny called Senator Leyonhjelam a "boorish, supercilious know-all with the empathy of a besser block" and referred to him as an "angry white male." Although Senator Leyonhjelam was not personally offended, he saw an opportunity to illustrate why Section 18C, in his view, should be repealed. According to him, under the Act, Kenny's article could be considered unlawful if it was reasonably likely to offend, insult, humiliate, or intimidate people based on their race. He stated that the comments were "reasonably likely to offend or insult some white males." Assuming the Human Rights Commission adheres to the law rather than being biased, he expected his complaint to succeed. If successful, he predicted that Kenny would be free to insult him without legal repercussions. Section 18C stipulates that it is illegal to publicly "offend, insult, humiliate, or intimidate" someone due to their "race, colour, national or ethnic origin." Since its use to prosecute Andrew Bolt, conservatives have advocated for its repeal. Former Prime Minister Tony Abbott initially promised to abolish Section 18C in 2013 but later abandoned the effort due to opposition. Abbott recently reignited the debate by suggesting a more modest reform that removes the terms "offend" and "insult" from the law.
Matthew Chapman, a businessman from Louth in Lincolnshire, was the sole occupant of the privately owned helicopter when it crashed on June 6. The inquest learned that Chapman, aged 48, had dropped off two passengers near the crash site in Baldwin Valley just prior to the accident. Coroner John Needham noted that the cause of death was still undetermined. The court recalled that Chapman had departed from Milton Keynes around 6:00 BST with two passengers aboard his Bell Jetranger helicopter. After reaching the Isle of Man, he dropped off the passengers at Creg Ny Baa, a well-known spot for watching the annual TT races. It was reported that Chapman planned to refuel at Ronaldsway Airport before returning to watch the races with friends. However, his helicopter crashed at approximately 09:00 BST in a field alongside the 33rd milestone on the Mountain Road. Chapman's body, found "trapped in wreckage," was pronounced dead at the scene. A paramedic's report mentioned "multiple trauma consistent with a helicopter crash." The coroner stated that investigations into the crash were being carried out by inspectors from the UK's Air Accidents Investigation Branch (AAIB). The inquest has been adjourned to an as-yet undetermined date.

Human rights organizations assert the trial is fundamentally flawed and a "sham." In the UAE, political gatherings and parties are prohibited; several neighboring Gulf states, including Bahrain, Yemen, Oman, and Saudi Arabia, experienced pro-democracy protests following the 2011 Arab Spring uprisings in Tunisia and Egypt. Activists were detained last year as part of a campaign targeting civil society figures suspected of dissent. AMs on the standards committee are investigating the need for stricter regulation of lobbyists. While Scotland and Westminster have established registers, Wales lacks one. A campaigner argues that Wales could best protect its reputation by introducing a register now. Lobbyists are individuals or firms hired to sway government decisions. During an assembly debate earlier this year, Plaid Cymru AM Neil McEvoy advocated for lobbying regulation and registration in Wales to foster transparency. However, Standards Commissioner David Elias, set to be succeeded by Sir Roderick Evans in December, informed the committee that no complaints regarding lobbying had been made to him. Elias also noted that setting up a statutory register would likely incur substantial resource costs. Tamasin Cave from the Alliance for Lobbying Transparency stated that one of the main issues with lobbying is its covert nature, making it difficult to know who meets with whom in government and what agreements are made. She suggested that a straightforward approach would be to require lobbyists to register, detailing their clients, targets within government, and objectives. Cave emphasized that maintaining Wales’s good standing requires opening up the lobbying process to scrutiny. Huw Roberts, chairman of the prominent Welsh lobbying firm Deryn, noted that his company publishes client lists and adheres to a strong code of conduct as part of the Association of Professional Political Consultants (APPC). Mark Glover, chair of the APPC, highlighted the importance of ensuring new regulations provide consistent transparency for both consultant and in-house lobbyists without being overly burdensome. In 2013, the assembly standards committee decided that while AMs should be more scrutinized, a full official register was unnecessary. First Minister Carwyn Jones has confirmed that lobbyists do not have access to Welsh ministers. The committee plans to conduct a written consultation on the matter at its upcoming meeting.
Surrounded by Rome, the Italian capital, Vatican City is home to priests and nuns from various nations. It stands in stark contrast to the former Papal States, which covered much of central Italy and were annexed by Italian unification forces in the mid-19th century. For nearly a century, the popes were confined within Vatican walls until 1929, when the Lateran Treaty established the current micro-state. Despite its diminutive size—just 0.44 square kilometers (0.17 square miles)—Vatican City is packed with significant structures like St. Peter's Basilica and the Vatican Museums, which house invaluable papal art collections. In response to international pressure, the Vatican Bank faced scrutiny in 2010 for money-laundering violations, prompting increased financial transparency. With a population of around 800, the official languages are Latin and Italian, and the predominant religion is Christianity. Life expectancy is approximately 74 years for men and 81 years for women. The currency is the euro, and the head of state is Pope Francis, who succeeded Benedict XVI in 2013. Francis, the first Latin American pope, surprised many with his election at 76. He balances conservative views on issues like homosexuality with progressive stances on poverty and inequality. Vatican Radio, known as "The Pope's Voice," broadcasts globally via powerful transmitters, though it has faced accusations of violating electromagnetic radiation limits in Rome. The Vatican also maintains a Twitter account, @pontifex.

Scott Van Zyl, a hunter, was reportedly killed last week near the Limpopo River, according to Sakkie Louwrens, director of a South African crime-fighting NGO. Louwrens stated that Van Zyl had gone missing during a hunting trip. This incident follows other fatal crocodile attacks in Zimbabwe. Louwrens mentioned that Van Zyl and a local tracker, accompanied by dogs, ventured into the wilderness on the Zimbabwe-South Africa border in search of crocodiles. When the dogs returned without their owner, a search began. Footprints led to the riverbank where Van Zyl's rucksack was found. He was married with two children and regularly took foreign clients on hunting trips. Louwrens noted that staff from the Heritage Protection Group, an organization Louwrens leads, assisted Zimbabwean authorities in the search. Three Nile crocodiles in the vicinity were reportedly shot; one contained Van Zyl's remains, confirmed by subsequent DNA testing. At least four fatal crocodile attacks have been documented in Zimbabwe this year. One Green Planet criticized Van Zyl’s death as senseless, emphasizing that animals should be treated with respect due to their instinct for survival.

In March 2015, while the tug Asterix was assisting the chemical tanker Donizetti at the Fawley marine terminal, it was suddenly pulled sideways. This caused the tug to capsize, trapping the coxswain in the wheelhouse. He was rescued by a colleague just before the vessel sank. The Marine Accident Investigation Branch (MAIB) noted that there were safety concerns regarding the crew's training. The coxswain lost control of the tug due to strong winds around 8 PM BST on March 30, 2015. The report highlighted that the coxswain did not know how to properly use a 'gog' rope, which is designed to prevent such lateral movement. It also revealed communication issues between the Donizetti and Asterix. Additionally, the report pointed out that the crew were not adequately trained in using the tug's emergency release mechanism. Safety and training recommendations were issued to Asterix's operator, ABP Southampton, and the National Workboat Association. Following the incident, a crew member from the tug received a bravery award for rescuing the coxswain. Both individuals required medical attention for shock and hypothermia.

The Council of Mortgage Lenders (CML) noted a significant decline in loan demand in April, with mortgage advances totaling £18.4 billion, a 11% decrease from March. Despite this monthly volatility, the CML indicated that the amount borrowed in April was still 4% higher than in April 2016 and exceeded the yearly average. CML's senior economist, Mohammad Jamei, observed, "The housing market seems to be stagnating, to put it simply." Other indicators suggest a slowing housing market. HM Revenue and Customs reported a 22.5% drop in property transactions in April compared to March. One reason for this could be that landlords may have accelerated their purchases to avoid recent tax changes; starting on April 1st, the tax relief available for mortgage payments was reduced. Additionally, the number of first-time buyers obtaining mortgages surpassed the number of people moving for the first time since 1996. According to CML research, while the numbers of both home movers and landlords seeking mortgages have decreased, first-time buyers have been on the rise. Jamei commented, "First-time buyers and remortgage customers are supporting the market, whereas home movers are experiencing less success. Their activity has been low, contributing to fewer properties being available for sale. This imbalance between supply and demand is likely to keep house prices stable, even if the pace of increases eases."

The Clackamas County owners of the 1,500-pound (680kg) animal, named Moses, called for help when they couldn't free him from a sinkhole. Rescuers spent hours digging to extract Moses, whose long legs were deeply mired in mud. A veterinarian confirmed that Moses was unharmed after the rescue. The owners received a call from firefighters late on Tuesday, explaining that they had attempted to pull Moses out but failed due to the depth of the mud. Concerned about the risk of breaking his legs if he tried to extricate himself, they sought assistance. The firefighters worked for several hours around Moses, who continuously cried and wailed, before securing straps under his belly to pull him out. After lying on his side for about an hour to rest, Moses eventually stood up, walked to his barn, and resumed his normal activities.
Macsen Oatway, from Neath, has LAD1, an extremely rare condition affecting his immune system; only about 400 people worldwide have it. He stayed for weeks in a specialized isolation unit called a "bubble" in Newcastle, accessible only to medical staff, nurses, and his parents. He underwent several blood transfusions, chemotherapy, and bone marrow treatment, which would otherwise have required him to take antibiotics indefinitely. His parents, Richard and Zoe, state that this treatment has significantly improved Macsen's quality of life and are advocating for more people to sign up for the bone marrow registry, ensuring that other children with the condition can enjoy full lives. Mr. Oatway remarked, "It's crucial. You might never be needed, but if the chance arises, grasp it firmly." He added, "It’s a wonderful chance to save a life, and to those who donated to Macsen, thank you very much." Medical professionals confirm that Macsen is now healthy enough to return to school, and his parents report that he loves it there.

Christopher Langley, from Pontypridd, collaborated with pub owner James Lovegrove to create these paintings. The Campaign for Real Ale (Camra) reported that two Welsh pubs close each week. Langley has completed 13 paintings thus far and aims to finish another 20 in the coming months, expressing concern about the potential loss of pub history. So far, featured pubs include The Vulcan, which was recently saved by St Fagan's Museum, the York Hotel, the Pantmawr Inn, and the Glendower. "This project has broadened my perspective," Langley noted. "I wasn't accustomed to pub culture, but working with James and learning about Cardiff's pubs has really enlightened me. These paintings serve as a means to preserve and share this history." The artworks are based on drawings or photographs, and he explains, "Before, I didn't appreciate the architectural details of pubs. Now, standing there and painting them, I can see the immense craftsmanship involved in creating these spaces."

The Elephant and Castle roundabout in south London had the highest incidence of cycling accidents between 2009 and 2013, with 80 incidents recorded. According to Aviva's analysis of DfT data, cycling accidents within the M25 were also documented. Transport for London acknowledged that every road accident is unacceptable, stating: "While we don't recognize the interpretation of these figures, we completely agree that any accident on London's roads is one too many. " The authority plans to invest nearly £1 billion in enhancing existing Cycle Superhighways with better segregation, introducing new segregated cycle routes, and improving numerous junctions on both London and borough roads. In March 2014, the London mayor's office unveiled a £4 billion plan to overhaul London's road system, including new road layouts and increased cycle lanes at Elephant and Castle roundabout. Trafalgar Square in Central London ranked second for cycling accidents, with 46 incidents from 2009 to 2013. Caroline Pidgeon, leader of the Liberal Democrat London Assembly Group, criticized Transport for London for underinvesting in critical cycling infrastructure. Four cyclist fatalities occurred in London in 2015, all involving lorries, with the latest incident resulting in the death of a mother-of-two in Victoria. Earlier in February, a tripling of fatalities prompted Scotland Yard to reintroduce Operation Safeway, deploying 600 officers at 166 junctions during rush hours for six weeks to target traffic violations.
Now, people in Scotland can dial 111 instead of the former 11-digit number to access medical advice. Previously, landline callers paid for local calls, while mobile users often incurred significantly higher charges. This change aligns Scotland with England, which adopted the 111 number in February. The NHS 24 service will continue unchanged, with nurses and healthcare professionals providing advice on non-urgent health issues around the clock. The switch to a freephone number was first announced by Scottish Health Secretary Alex Neil in January of the previous year, aiming to reduce barriers to accessing healthcare services. The move was supported by poverty campaigners, such as Caroline Mockford, who faced exorbitant charges from her mobile provider for a call to the helpline.
The Dominican Republic, a Caribbean nation, is a significant tourist destination with free-trade zones contributing to its economy. These sectors have replaced earlier reliance on sugar, coffee, and other exports as the main employers and revenue generators. Predominantly inhabited by people of mixed European and African ancestry, the country showcases Western influences through colonial architecture in the capital, Santo Domingo, and in art and literature. The African heritage is evident in its music. With a population of 10.2 million, the Dominican Republic spans 48,072 square kilometers (18,696 square miles). Spanish is the primary language, and Christianity is the predominant religion. Life expectancy stands at 71 years for men and 77 years for women. The official currency is the Dominican peso. President Danilo Medina Sanchez, a member of the Dominican Liberation Party (PLD), was re-elected in May 2016, succeeding Leonel Fernandez Reyna. Medina has faced criticism for changes to abortion laws and a naturalization policy that rendered thousands of Haitian immigrants' descendants stateless. Media ownership is concentrated among a few powerful entities, yet there are numerous terrestrial TV channels, cable TV operators, and radio stations. Press freedom is legally protected, and media carry a range of political perspectives. Key historical milestones include Christopher Columbus's 1492 arrival, the establishment of the first Spanish colony in the Western Hemisphere in Santo Domingo, and the 1697 Treaty of Ryswick dividing the island between France and Spain. Independence was declared in 1844 after Haiti's annexation, and subsequent periods saw the country alternating between Spanish rule and independence. The US intervened militarily in 1916, and a series of dictators ruled the country until Rafael Leonidas Trujillo Molina's assassination in 1961.
Last month, an inquiry was initiated after the PSNI learned that a legal firm had illegally received confidential documents from their office. A 69-year-old man, previously an investigator for the ombudsman, was arrested in England and subsequently released on bail. The PSNI has announced that an independent police service will oversee the investigation. Assistant Chief Constable Stephen Martin stated, "Since the investigation began, steps have been taken to ensure independent advice is available to the PSNI. Additionally, we are actively seeking another police service to independently lead the investigation." The new team is expected to be appointed within weeks. This move was made due to concerns about a potential conflict of interest, as the ombudsman investigates allegations of misconduct by PSNI officers and former Royal Ulster Constabulary members. At least eight former RUC officers are known to be named in the documents, believed to be part of an ongoing misconduct investigation. The PSNI and MI5 suspect the material could pose a risk to lives. The police declined to comment on the scope of the investigation, stating, "As the PSNI is conducting a criminal investigation, further comments would be inappropriate." As a precaution, the PSNI has halted the release of sensitive material related to dozens of ongoing ombudsman investigations until security protocols are reviewed. The ombudsman, Dr. Michael Maguire, has also referred the document theft and unlawful disclosure to the information commissioner. Furthermore, the Centre for the Protection of National Infrastructure has been tasked with reviewing the ombudsman’s office information handling processes. In a statement, the Police Ombudsman’s spokesman noted, "While the theft occurred about ten years ago, we are now bringing in the Centre for the Protection of National Infrastructure to conduct a review, aiming to provide independent assurance that our current systems for handling material are secure."

Remy and Paul Hoggard, renowned sand sculptors, are crafting a 4-meter-tall and 9-meter-wide sculpture of King John at Runnymede for the Festival800 anniversary event. This artwork, made from around 80 tonnes of sand, will commemorate the signing of Magna Carta in 1215. The event, which celebrates democracy and freedom, includes various activities such as performances by Billy Bragg and the Levellers, comedy, poetry, street theater, lectures, and debates. The Hoggards, who have created sand sculptures worldwide, describe this project as their most ambitious due to its scale. Remy, originally from Beverley, East Yorkshire, currently resides on a farm in Bulgaria. Magna Carta was an agreement between King John and the barons, outlining principles of freedom under the law; only three of its 63 original clauses remain in law today. One clause protects the freedom and rights of the English Church, another pertains to the City of London's privileges, and the third is believed to have established the right to trial by jury. One of the surviving copies of Magna Carta can be seen at Lincoln Castle. Festival800 takes place from August 28 to September 6 in various venues across Lincoln.

Health Secretary Vaughan Gething stated that these measures aim to ensure Wales's health and social care services are prepared for the future. A three-month consultation period will now commence. A few minutes after launching a Japanese satellite towards orbit, the Falcon-9 booster touched down on a drone ship near Florida. This marks the second successful at-sea landing by SpaceX, following one from last month. Notably, this recent feat was particularly challenging due to the increased speed the rocket carried during its return. SpaceX officials noted that retrieving boosters from missions to geostationary orbits is inherently more difficult because of the higher velocities needed. Upon confirmation of the latest landing, CEO Elon Musk jokingly tweeted about the need to expand their rocket storage facilities. The primary objective of the latest Falcon-9 mission was to deploy the JCSAT-14 satellite, which will provide telecommunications services across the Asia-Pacific region for Sky Perfect JSAT Corporation. The launch took place at 01:21 local time (05:20 GMT) from Cape Canaveral Air Force Station, with the satellite separation occurring 32 minutes later. Due to unfavorable weather conditions, the mission was initially delayed by a day. To date, SpaceX has successfully landed three boosters, including the first soft landing near the Florida launch site in December. However, the company remains focused on perfecting ocean landings on specialized barges, as these will likely become a common practice given the nature of many of their missions. Achieving a controlled descent and landing while managing the additional speed and thermal stress poses significant engineering challenges. SpaceX aims to reuse these rockets to reduce launch costs for its clients.

The European Commission examined if tech giants adhered to their commitment to eliminate hate speech within 24 hours after reports. It discovered that only 40% of hate speech reports were addressed within a day. This pledge was agreed upon in May when the firms joined a "code of conduct" facilitated by the Commission. Justice Commissioner Vera Jourova stated in a press release that recent events highlight the necessity for social media companies to fulfill their crucial roles and assume responsibility regarding issues such as online radicalization, illegal hate speech, and misinformation. A six-week study from October and November monitored 600 instances of hate speech reported to tech firms. Campaign organizations and NGOs that track racist and fascist movements in Europe utilized social media notification systems to report these incidents and tracked the firms' response times. Out of 600 reports, 270 were directed to Facebook, 163 to Twitter, 123 to YouTube, and seven to other social media groups not part of the May agreement. The Commission noted that the 600 reports were a selection of racist, sexist, and misogynistic content, not an exhaustive representation of hate speech during the study period. Approximately 20% of the messages were anti-Muslim, and 23% were anti-Semitic. Research showed that in 169 cases, tech firms removed the flagged content; YouTube removed 48.5%, Facebook 28.3%, and Twitter 19.1%. Reports were reviewed within 24 hours in 40% of cases, and in 43%, reviews occurred within 48 hours. Commissioner Jourova emphasized, "Our duty is to safeguard Europeans from online incitement to hatred and violence. While tech companies are making progress, they must do more to ensure this initiative's success."

Honey Rose, 35, was convicted of gross negligence manslaughter after Vincent Barker died in July 2012 due to fluid accumulation in his brain. She received a two-year suspended sentence in August for failing to detect the condition during a routine eye exam. Vincent’s family expressed deep distress over the appeal court's decision. The judges acknowledged a significant failure in duty but stated that such issues were for regulatory bodies rather than the courts. During her trial, Ms. Rose, a mother of three from Newham, east London, claimed she had performed all necessary tests during Vincent's eye examination at the Ipswich Boots branch on February 15, 2012, but missed the swollen optic discs, a sign of hydrocephalus. Vincent passed away in July 2012, approximately five months after the examination. In the Court of Appeal, Sir Brian Leveson and his colleagues noted, "While this decision does not excuse the high level of negligence shown in how Ms. Rose examined Vincent, resulting in his death, the serious breach of duty is a matter for her professional regulator, not the courts." The Barker family's statement reflected their disappointment, asserting, "We are devastated and believe the charge of gross negligence manslaughter should have been upheld. If Honey Rose had fulfilled her duty of care, our son would still be alive." They also highlighted that Vincent was "let down" by both his optometrist and the legal system.
The East Lindsey District Council aims to transfer the So Festival, the Embassy Theatre in Skegness, and the Meridian Leisure Centre in Louth to a charitable trust. This move, officials argue, would enable the facilities to secure new funding. Additionally, it would save the authority at least £1 million over five years with minimal impact on jobs. The council has already faced significant funding cuts, including a £1.9 million reduction this fiscal year, on top of a £1.2 million cut the previous year. Currently, the Meridian Leisure Centre, which opened in 2010, needs a £250,000 annual subsidy, and about £350,000 is allocated to its events program, which includes the So Festival. Councillor Adam Grist, responsible for culture, leisure, and tourism, emphasized that continuing with the current approach is not viable. "We must take action; standing still won't solve our problems," he stated. By being proactive and making these changes, the council hopes to safeguard the services it provides. Charitable status would also offer tax benefits like business rate and VAT relief. According to Grist, the council would pay a management fee to the trust, which would decrease over time. The authority would maintain ownership of the buildings and ensure employee pay and conditions are protected. These proposals will be discussed at the full council meeting next month.

A £282 million relief road planned for junction 6 near the National Exhibition Centre could spell the end for Pairc na hEireann, the home of the Warwickshire Gaelic Athletic Association. Built with funds raised by the Irish community in the 1950s, the site is a hub for Gaelic sports in Britain. Martin Grogan, the treasurer, expressed concern, stating, "Highways England is taking away our memories." Mark Morgan, who has played for the club for 30 years, declared, "This is the heart of Gaelic sport in Britain. Period." The facility, hosting hundreds of players weekly for hurling, camogie, and Gaelic football, is irreplaceable in size and location. Highways England is evaluating three route options; one of which would cut directly through the club's land. The upgrade aims to address congestion and prepare for an upcoming HS2 station. While a decision hasn't been made, Highways England assured the club they would be kept informed and that efforts would be made to minimize environmental and community impacts.
Wildcat Haven’s recordings, taken near Huntly in Aberdeenshire, depict a protected wildcat perched on a large branch. These images were captured as part of a surveillance operation using camera traps. Wildcat Haven claimed it was the first wildcat to achieve full points on a breeding scale with domestic cats. However, Scottish Wildcat Action contested this, noting that ten wildcats had previously been sighted in the area, including one confirmed as highly pure. Dr. Paul O'Donoghue, chief scientific adviser for Wildcat Haven, commented, "It feels like seeing a unicorn. This species is often thought to be extinct, almost mythical, but we've always believed they were still around, and now we have evidence from an unexpected location." A spokesperson for Scottish Wildcat Action stated, "This recent claim is not new to us; the wildcat in this 'new' video closely resembles one we've already identified."
Developed over 15 years for rescue or military purposes, the $14 million (€11 million) Cormorant (previously known as AirMule) can carry 500 kg (1,100 lb) at a speed of 185 km/h (115 mph). Urban Aeronautics asserts that its internal rotors enhance safety compared to helicopters. Additionally, it can take off and land vertically, navigate between buildings, and fly beneath power lines. Urban Aeronautics CEO Rafi Yoeli stated, "This paves the way for the Cormorant’s transition from prototype to near-term production and eventual commercialization for various applications and markets." Commercial drones are increasingly popular; Amazon launched its Prime Air delivery service by drone in the summer. In June, the 184, a passenger drone by Chinese company Ehang, received permission for test flights in Nevada. Dr. Mirko Kovac, director of the Aerial Robotics Lab at Imperial College London, noted, "The proposed vehicle demonstrates significant potential for personal drones, beyond just delivery and environmental monitoring, including transportation. While substantial technological advancements are needed for safety and user-friendliness, such a product could revolutionize urban mobility and provide fast aerial travel for the general public." Drone expert Ravi Vaidyanathan, from Imperial’s Department of Mechanical Engineering, suggested that the Israeli drone’s recent flight might mark a significant milestone in low-altitude navigation. Initially focusing on humanitarian rescue and military uses is a prudent approach, he argued, as establishing a niche application and building a safety record are essential. While civilian drones are expected to emerge, addressing regulatory challenges such as takeoff and landing zones, and flight altitudes, remains uncertain.
Dow Corning workers in Barry have been informed about potential site changes due to the company's restructuring. Approximately 630 employees work at the facility, and a 45-day consultation process is set to commence shortly. The company clarified that the decision is unrelated to Brexit. Dow Chemical Company plans to reduce its workforce globally by about 2,500 positions, which is roughly 4% of its total staff. In a statement, the company stated, "Leadership met with site employees to discuss the reasons behind this global workforce consolidation and its potential impact on roles in Barry." Silicone manufacturing began at the Barry site in 1952, initially by a company known as Midland Silicones. According to Dow Corning’s website, it is "one of the most advanced facilities in the world" for its kind. The site produces silicone fluids, rubbers, and specialty polymers for various applications, including cosmetics, car airbag coatings, and construction sealants.
