George Osborne stated that 98% of English and Welsh homeowners would pay less after the reforms compared to the current system. Only those purchasing properties valued over £937,000 would face higher taxes. He declared, "It's time to reform this poorly designed tax on aspiration." How will the changes work? Currently, stamp duty, which applies across the UK, imposes progressively higher rates on the entire purchase price. Critics argue this makes it a "slab tax" due to sudden rate increases when prices surpass thresholds. For instance, a buyer purchasing a £250,000 home pays £2,500, or 1%. If the price rises by just £1, the tax jumps to £5,000, as the buyer must pay 3% on the full purchase price. Effective midnight on December 3rd, the new rates will apply only to the part of the purchase price within each specific tax bracket, similar to income tax. Thus, a buyer of a £200,000 home will owe no tax on the first £125,000, then 2% on the remaining £75,000, totaling £1,500. Previously, they would have paid £2,000. Although the percentage rates seem higher in some cases, the overall charge will generally be lower. A family home buyer in England and Wales will save £4,500 in stamp duty. The new system will also smooth out the abrupt increases in existing stamp duty thresholds. Starting April 1st, 2015, Scotland will replace stamp duty with Land and Buildings Transaction Tax (LBTT), but until then, the new stamp duty rates will apply. The new system in England and Wales mirrors LBTT, taxing higher rates only on the portion of the property price within each band. Under LBTT, Scottish buyers paying less than £324,280 will benefit, while those spending more will pay more. The government has provided a stamp duty calculator, but some examples illustrate that LBTT rates in Scotland will be lower than the corresponding stamp duty rates in England and Wales. Buyers in Scotland start paying tax at £135,000, compared to £125,000 elsewhere in the UK. The new system will favor more modest buyers and disadvantage wealthier ones.

An IPC taskforce has unanimously decided to maintain the suspension on Russia, which was imposed in August. The ban followed revelations of systemic doping at the Rio Paralympics last year. A recent taskforce report highlights concerns about Russia's lack of action in addressing these issues before the 2018 Winter Olympics. IPC president Sir Philip Craven previously indicated that Russian athletes might still face bans for the upcoming Games. The suspension was based on detailed allegations by WADA investigator Richard McLaren regarding widespread drug use and cover-ups, allegedly involving government officials. However, according to the IPC taskforce report, Russian officials have not specifically addressed McLaren’s findings by either accepting them or providing adequate rebuttals. Taskforce chairman Andy Parkinson, CEO of British Rowing, stated that until the root causes of the suspension are fully understood and addressed, there can be no significant cultural change. Despite this, the report notes some positive developments, such as efforts to establish an effective short-term testing program. Additionally, Russia remains suspended from international competition, as the IAAF extended the ban earlier this month.
Abubakar Shekau rejected the Islamic State (IS) designation of Abu Musab al-Barnawi as the new leader, accusing al-Barnawi of attempting to stage a coup against him. Boko Haram continues to fight to overthrow Nigeria’s government and establish an Islamic state in the north. Over the past 18 months, the group has lost much of the territory it once controlled due to advances by Nigerian and neighboring forces. Shekau’s last recorded statement, made in an audio message in August, affirmed his continued leadership and denied any replacement; an IS video from April similarly stated this. In a recent 10-minute audio message, partly in Arabic and Hausa, Shekau distanced Boko Haram from IS while still acknowledging its leader as the caliph. He alleged that some within the group had prevented him from communicating with the caliph, claiming his messages were manipulated to serve personal interests. Shekau also described al-Barnawi and his followers as polytheists. This split marks the most significant division within Boko Haram to date, indicating deep disagreements that could lead to conflicts between Shekau’s supporters and al-Barnawi’s. The fragmentation may reflect the group’s weakening and potentially signal its impending collapse. Military officials view this split as evidence that Boko Haram is nearing its end, though some security analysts warn that internal strife might make the group more dangerous and unpredictable. This division could benefit Nigeria and regional forces, who may exploit the factionalism. Shekau took over as leader following Muhammad Yusuf’s death in police custody in July 2009. Under his guidance, Boko Haram grew more radical and pledged allegiance to IS in March 2015. Shekau frequently taunted Nigerian authorities in videos, boasting about the group’s actions, such as the abduction of over 200 Chibok schoolgirls in April 2014. Despite Nigeria’s army claiming multiple times to have killed Shekau, he has not appeared in a video since Boko Haram aligned with IS.

The St James Theatre, located on Palace Street in Victoria, stands on the grounds where the Westminster Theatre once stood, which was ravaged by fire in 2002. The theatre's owners aim to draw new audiences and promote commercial theatre beyond the West End with their £7 million project. The inaugural season kicks off with "Bully Boy," a play penned by comedian Sandi Toksvig. Originally constructed as a chapel in 1766, the building was later transformed into a cinema in 1924 and then into a theatre in 1931. For decades, it was part of the Moral Re-Armament movement's property, hosting shows with moral or spiritual themes. The new complex features a 312-seat main hall, a studio space for up to 150 people, and apartments. Additionally, the theatre will include multimedia broadcast capabilities for live-streaming performances via its own BSkyB satellite channel.
Since June 24, annuity rates have declined by 3.5%, according to Tom McPhail, head of retirement policy at Hargreaves Lansdown. Prior to the vote, a 65-year-old with savings of £100,000 could secure an annual income of £5,069; now, that pension value stands at a new record low of £4,890. Experts anticipated this outcome, but despite this, more than half of those over 60 chose to leave the European Union. "Annuity rates have plummeted to unprecedented levels," remarked Mr. McPhail. "Six months ago, a 60-year-old could obtain a better deal than what is currently available to a 65-year-old. Although rates are now historically low, there is no guarantee they will rise again." Annuity rates have been decreasing for years due to increasing life expectancy. The decline has been further intensified by reduced bond yields in the two weeks following the vote. Nonetheless, Mr. McPhail encouraged individuals considering purchasing an annuity to avoid waiting for any potential increase in rates. "In short, if you're thinking about buying an annuity, don't delay simply because current rates are lower than they were previously."

Whether the recent test was genuine remains unclear, and even if it was, it's uncertain if it will be viewed as a success by North Korea. Such a test would be significant because SLBMs are difficult to detect. This comes as North Korea prepares for an infrequent and important party congress next month. North Korea is prohibited from conducting nuclear tests and ballistic missile-related activities under UN sanctions from 2006. A South Korean defense ministry spokesperson reported, "A projectile suspected to be an SLBM was launched around 6:30 pm (0930 GMT) in the East Sea (Sea of Japan) near the northeast port of Sinpo." "We are closely monitoring the North Korean military and maintaining a full defense readiness," the spokesperson added. North Korea has not officially reported the test through its media. The secretive nation has previously claimed similar tests but faced skepticism regarding those claims. According to the U.S., photos purportedly showing a December launch were manipulated, and some believe North Korea has fired missiles from submerged platforms without confirming submarine launches. South Korea's Joint Chiefs of Staff stated that the missile traveled approximately 30 kilometers (19 miles), significantly less than the typical range of 300 kilometers (186 miles) for an SLBM. North Korea has conducted four nuclear tests, with the most recent in January this year, prompting the UN Security Council to impose its strictest sanctions to date. Last month, North Korea announced it had developed small enough nuclear warheads to fit on ballistic missiles, though experts remain skeptical. Analysts suspect North Korea might be preparing for a fifth test as a display of power ahead of the upcoming party congress, the first since 1980.
In Siyazan, police used tear gas and rubber bullets to disperse protesters, resulting in at least one hospitalization and multiple injuries. The demonstrators were upset over deteriorating economic conditions, partly due to plummeting oil prices. Azerbaijan's economy relies significantly on oil, with nearly half of its GDP in 2014 coming from the sector. Oil prices have dropped by 70% over the past 15 months, reaching $31 per barrel. This decline has led to a sharp depreciation of Azerbaijan's currency, the manat. The interior ministry alleged that the protests were organized by the opposition and religious extremists. In response to the crisis, the government reduced the price of flour starting from Friday, according to Reuters. VAT was waived on wheat imports and the sale of bread and flour. To stabilize the manat, Azerbaijan's central bank prohibited commercial banks from selling foreign exchange through bureaux de change, as reported by Reuters. The economy, heavily reliant on oil, has been hit hard by falling global oil prices. Small businesses have faced significant challenges due to bribes reportedly demanded by officials, and corruption has been highlighted as a major issue by the International Monetary Fund. At the beginning of last year, the manat was roughly equivalent to the euro, but it depreciated against the dollar in February 2015. In December 2015, the Central Bank decoupled the manat from the dollar, leading to immediate price hikes that particularly impacted low-income families and those with substantial bank loans. Given that Azerbaijan imports most basic goods and relies on imported raw materials for local production, the economic downturn affects a wide range of consumers. Despite warnings from analysts about the risks of oil dependency, the government has made limited efforts to diversify the economy. Media in Azerbaijan are tightly controlled, and critical voices face forceful responses. While discontent was minimal during the oil boom, the current currency volatility impacts almost everyone, potentially making it harder for the government to maintain public support.

Research indicated that taller individuals faced a marginally elevated risk of certain cancers, including breast and skin cancer. Specifically, the study noted that for every additional 10 cm in height, fully grown, the cancer risk rose by 18% in women and 11% in men. However, experts highlighted that the study did not consider numerous risk factors and advised tall people not to worry excessively. To lower cancer risk, the key actions are well-established: previous research has linked height to increased cancer risk, though the exact reasons remain unclear. The study, conducted by researchers from the Karolinska Institute in Stockholm over more than five decades, involved a large group of Swedish adults. It found that taller women had a 20% higher chance of breast cancer, and both taller men and women had a 30% increased risk of skin cancer. These initial findings align closely with those from earlier studies. Dr. Emelie Benyi, the lead researcher, suggested that these results could aid in identifying risk factors that may lead to treatment development, yet acknowledged the complexity of predicting individual cancer risk due to multiple contributing factors. Experts propose that adult height serves as a marker for other childhood growth-related factors, such as more growth factors, a larger number of cells, and higher food intake, all potentially increasing cancer risk. Professor Dorothy Bennett from St George's, University of London, explained that the cancer risk in taller individuals might be due to the greater number of cells, particularly noting that melanoma risk could be related to skin surface area, which scales with height. Sarah Williams from Cancer Research UK pointed out that the study did not account for factors like smoking or breast screening attendance. She concluded that regardless of height, lifestyle choices such as avoiding smoking, limiting alcohol consumption, maintaining a healthy diet, staying active, keeping a healthy weight, and protecting skin from excessive sun exposure can significantly reduce cancer risk.

A lorry and a minibus crashed near Newport Pagnell on Saturday, resulting in nine deaths and several serious injuries. Ryszard Masierak, a 31-year-old Polish national from Worcestershire, faces charges including eight counts of causing death by dangerous driving and four counts of causing serious injury by dangerous driving. He is also charged with causing death by careless driving while over the legal alcohol limit. Another lorry driver, David Wagstaff, 53, from Stoke-on-Trent, is charged with eight counts of causing death by dangerous driving and four counts of causing serious injury by dangerous driving. Wagstaff is set to appear in court on September 11. Six men and two women died in the collision, which occurred around 03:15 BST on the M1's southbound carriageway between junctions 15 and 14. A five-year-old girl, along with a man and a woman, sustained life-threatening injuries and remain hospitalized. Another individual was treated for less severe injuries. Sponsored by the Scottish Crofting Federation and the Crofting Commission, the contest aimed to capture the essence of crofter life in 2014. Heather Gray from Shetland emerged as the winner with her photograph "Hentin Totties," featuring a family of all ages at work. She explained, "My main inspiration for the photo is family. Witnessing the entire family, from grandparents to toddlers, working together brings a smile to your face." Ria Macdonald took second place with "Checking the Sheep with Grandad," a picture of Cormac Sandison and his dog Mac inspecting pregnant ewes on his grandfather's croft on the Isle of Lewis. Third place went to Fiona MacKay's "Summer Bales," showing haymaking on Lewis. John King, a judge from RoS, noted, "We received many excellent photographs from crofters of all ages across Scotland’s crofting regions. These images vividly convey crofters' passion for their work and the crucial role of family in this traditional lifestyle. 'Hentin Totties' especially highlighted the joy and camaraderie of communal labor."
The hack impacted 51 franchises in 24 states, exposing clients' names, postal addresses, email addresses, and payment card details. UPS, a company with over 4,450 franchised locations across the U.S., said the malware had been removed and its services were now secure. Last week, a major U.S. hospital chain reported that its systems had been breached, affecting around 4.5 million healthcare patients at Community Health Systems facilities. Additionally, SuperValu, a national grocery chain, disclosed a data breach. UPS, originally established as a messenger service in 1907, operates independently owned franchises that manage their own networks. The breaches, which UPS only detected through a government notification, occurred between January and August. While no fraudulent use of stolen data has been reported, UPS advised customers to closely watch their accounts for any unusual activity. "We urge you to stay vigilant by reviewing your account statements and checking your free credit reports," stated Tim Davis, president of The UPS Store. He also noted that the incident had been fully contained and that an internal review was underway to assess the breach.
Since Wednesday, Andrey Melnichenko's sailing yacht, Sailing Yacht A, has been detained in Gibraltar, a British territory. A spokesman for Melnichenko expressed optimism that the detention would be resolved soon. Built by Nobiskrug and registered in Bermuda, the 143-meter vessel, equipped with three masts and a main mast reaching 100 meters, left the Kiel shipyard in northern Germany two weeks ago. With a gross tonnage of 12,600 and estimated to cost at least €400 million, the superyacht features an underwater observation pod, hybrid diesel-electric propulsion, and state-of-the-art navigation systems. Designed by Philippe Starck, the yacht is currently anchored in British Gibraltar Territorial Waters. According to documents from Germany's NDR news, Nobiskrug is seeking €9.8 million for outstanding payments, plus €5.5 million for subcontractors and interest. Valla Yachts Ltd, based in Bermuda, holds the yacht's registration. Gibraltar's Admiralty Marshal Liam Yeats informed the BBC that the vessel is under arrest. Melnichenko’s spokesman characterized the situation as a "technical issue," stating, "We are confident the yacht will be handed over to the owner's project team in the coming days." Forbes reports that Melnichenko, a Russian industrialist with significant interests in fertilizer, coal, and energy, has a net worth of $13.2 billion. Additionally, he owns another superyacht, Motor Yacht A, which is listed for sale and was previously docked near the old British light cruiser HMS Belfast on the River Thames in London. When a ship is arrested, according to the Gibraltar Port Authority, it typically leads to a sealed-bids auction within six to eight weeks after the claim is validated. The authority provides security guards and essential supplies to maintain the vessel and crew.

Currently, students in Northern Ireland receive their maintenance loan and grant payments three times a year, typically at the start of each academic term. Student leaders argue that monthly payments would make it easier for students to manage their finances. The Department for the Economy (DEL) estimates the cost of implementing this change to range from £250,000 to £350,000. Most students in Northern Ireland rely on loans to cover living expenses, but many also receive additional means-tested support grants that they do not have to repay. In 2013/14, over 42,000 students borrowed a total of £131 million in maintenance loans. Additionally, nearly 25,000 students—around 60%—received support grants totaling just over £68 million. Grant amounts vary based on household income; students from households earning up to £19,203 qualify for the maximum grant of £3,475 annually, while those from households earning between £19,203 and £41,605 receive a partial grant. In 2013/14, 38% of students received the full grant and 22% received a partial grant. Both loans and grants are disbursed quarterly, but Scotland operates a monthly system, providing a larger initial payment in September followed by smaller monthly installments from October to June. NUS-USI President Fergal McFerran supports monthly payments, stating that they could help students cover essential expenses like accommodation deposits, household equipment, or study materials needed at the beginning of the academic year. DEL Minister Stephen Farry has initiated a consultation to explore this possibility, noting that while there is limited room to increase funding, there is potential to adjust how support is distributed. The consultation will conclude on September 27. Tuition fee loans, currently set at £3,805 per year for students at Queen's or Ulster University, would remain unaffected by any changes.
The Australian Broadcasting Corporation (ABC) faced criticism after allowing a former terror suspect, Zaky Mallah, to appear live on the Q&A program two weeks ago. Agriculture Minister Barnaby Joyce subsequently canceled his scheduled Monday night appearance, citing the prime minister's directive. The Q&A show has sparked discussions about national security and free speech in Australia. The ABC acknowledged its mistake in letting Mallah ask a question and is conducting a review. In 2005, Mallah was convicted of threatening to kill government officials but acquitted of terrorism charges. During the program, Mallah confronted Parliamentary Secretary for Foreign Affairs Steven Ciobo about plans to revoke citizenship for those supporting terrorism. Ciobo responded that the government would make such individuals leave the country. Mallah retorted that the government had just encouraged many Australian Muslims to join ISIS. The ABC permits audience members to ask questions of a panel of influential Australians. High-profile politicians frequently participate. Joyce learned of the cancellation only after initially confirming his participation. Abbott stated that no threat was made but expressed disappointment over the lack of prior notice. Abbott also criticized the ABC's loyalty and ordered a government review, which found that while producers knew of Mallah’s criminal history, they were unaware of his recent social media content.
Irvine, 19, narrowly lost to Armenia's Narek Abgaryan in the semi-finals of the European Olympic qualifying event but retains a chance to qualify for Rio on Saturday. In the same category, Irish lightweight David Oliver Joyce lost to Britain's Joe Cordina in his semi-final. Irvine, a silver medalist from the previous year's European Games, forced Abgaryan to a late standing count but ultimately lost unanimously. Irvine faces Bulgaria's Daniel Asenov in a box-off on Saturday, who previously lost to Britain's Muhammad Ali. In the first round, Irvine began with a clever left-right combination, but Abgaryan responded effectively, particularly with a powerful right hand that pushed Irvine back. By the third round, Irvine was trailing and attempted a comeback, though Abgaryan maintained distance. With ten seconds left, Irvine landed several punches, causing Abgaryan to receive a standing count. Joyce, the Irish lightweight, appeared disappointed as all three judges awarded the bout to Britain's Cordina. Despite Joyce's strong performance in the second round, Cordina held slight advantages, leading to a 29-28 score. Joyce managed the final round well but faced a significant deficit. The judge who had tied the match after two rounds then gave the final round to Cordina, resulting in a unanimous decision.
Between April 2016 and March 2017, South Wales Fire and Rescue documented 31 incidents, an increase from the 12 recorded in the preceding year. Crews faced verbal and physical abuse, with bricks being thrown at some of them. North Wales and Mid and West Wales fire services did not provide specific figures but stated they had a zero-tolerance policy toward attacks. Areas in south Wales experiencing the highest frequency of attacks on firefighters included Blaenau Gwent (13%), Caerphilly County (16%), Cardiff (19%), and Newport (23%). South Wales Fire and Rescue is collaborating with Crimestoppers and the Gwent Police and Crime Commissioner to decrease these incidents and ensure justice for offenders. Matthew Jones, head of the fire crime unit in south Wales, emphasized that firefighters' mission is to save lives and protect property, adding, "Sometimes being treated with such a lack of respect is frankly appallingly disrespectful." A spokesperson for North Wales Fire and Rescue noted that attacks on their firefighters are rare compared to the number of calls they handle annually and affirmed their stance against such abuse. They stressed that their staff are helping and improving communities and should not endure any form of abuse. Similarly, a Mid and West Wales Fire and Rescue spokesperson declared their service's zero-tolerance policy and commitment to reporting, apprehending, and addressing any attacks on their personnel.
Ronald Burton was convicted of sexually abusing a boy aged 11 to 13 between 1994 and 1996. He was also found guilty of sexually assaulting a 13-year-old boy in 2014. Burton, a 60-year-old from East Kilbride, taught math and served as an assistant principal. The High Court in Glasgow deferred his sentence until September and maintained his bail. The court learned that Burton met the first victim in 1994, when the boy was in first year, and continued to abuse him until he was about 15. The victim said, "I began to think I was in the wrong." After the first victim felt "worthless" and confronted Burton, the teacher moved to another school and began abusing a second boy. Burton was reported to the police after the first victim's confrontation. Prosecutor Paul Kearney portrayed Burton as "highly manipulative" and "systematically abusive." Although Burton faced additional charges, including accusations of rape and attempted hypnosis of the second victim, these were later dropped by the prosecution.
As the German labor market now fully opens to workers from Poland and seven other EU countries that joined in 2004, Germany has taken steps to protect its workforce. Until recently, Germany resisted admitting workers from former communist nations. Labour Minister Ursula von der Leyen assured Bild readers that stricter controls would be placed on sectors such as construction and hospitality, where unions feared wage competition. Inspectors will verify compliance with minimum wage laws and ensure proper registration of newcomers. The Finance Ministry plans to add 150 officials to monitor industries for illegal workers. Eligible countries include Poland, the Czech Republic, Hungary, Slovenia, Slovakia, Estonia, Lithuania, and Latvia. By using popular media to communicate these measures, ministers may reflect underlying political concerns among German citizens, despite employer support for foreign workers, both skilled and unskilled.

Banks declined to comment. Weir stated, "Not once did she check on me that week. Given my past achievements and that I had a difficult week, I felt her response was inadequate." He plans to compete in the upcoming London Marathon and noted that Banks accused him of deliberately underperforming in the 4x400m T53/54 relay heats, which resulted in a second-place finish for Britain. Weir denied the accusation, saying, "Why would I sabotage a race to upset her? I was there to win medals." He couldn’t confirm reports that Banks had damaged his racing wheelchair, stating, "I haven’t seen it, but others have mentioned it." Banks took up the role of lead wheelchair racing coach for British Athletics in late 2012, having coached Tanni Grey-Thompson previously. Weir criticized Banks's appointment, noting that his longtime coach, Jenny Archer, was overlooked. However, he insisted his decision to step down from track racing and miss the World Championships in London remains firm, regardless of Banks’s future. "I couldn't represent Great Britain if she were still coaching," he said. "It wouldn't be fair to the team or set a bad example. But no one can take away the significance of what I achieved in London." He concluded, "I dedicated everything to racing for Great Britain and have faced considerable pressure to win medals."
In 2020, the UK government secured a 25-year lease for 265,976 square feet (24,710 square meters) of space in Wales, which developers claim is the largest office deal ever in the region. Construction on the former Cardiff bus station site commenced in 2014, with BBC Wales planning to relocate there in 2019. HM Revenue and Customs (HMRC) will transfer from their current base in Llanishen. The site, adjacent to Cardiff Central railway station, will serve as one of 13 regional tax office centers in the UK, alongside other government departments like the Office for the Secretary of State for Wales. Currently, the area hosts businesses such as Hugh James solicitors, Hodge Bank, and Motonovo Finance. Cardiff council leader Huw Thomas views this as a crucial piece of the city's development puzzle, noting plans for a new bus interchange and redevelopment of the Brains Brewery site. He emphasized, "Cardiff is competing with major UK cities to attract jobs and investment." A UK government spokesperson stated that this move is part of efforts to reduce the number of buildings from 800 to 200 by 2022. Secretary of State for Wales Alun Cairns highlighted that it demonstrates the government's commitment to being "visible, accessible, and improving people's lives." HMRC CEO Jon Thompson confirmed they will collaborate with Cardiff council to redevelop their current Ty Glas base, which currently employs 2,800 staff members, after the relocation.
The claim is that around 6,700 mental health nurses and doctors have been cut from the NHS in England since 2010. According to Health Minister Philip Dunne, the number of nurses in mental health dropped from 45,384 in 2010 to 38,774 in July 2016, representing a reduction of 6,610. For doctors, the figures show a slight decrease from 8,699 in May 2010 to 8,676 in January 2017, a drop of 23. However, this may not account for all mental health doctors.

Michel Dussuyer's team will play Ghana on Sunday after a draw at a Malabo hotel. In Group D, Guinea and Mali ended up tied after all three matches concluded in 1-1 draws. This is the first time in tournament history that a draw determined a team's qualification, last occurring in 1988 when Algeria benefited at Ivory Coast's expense. Ivory Coast topped Group D after defeating Cameroon 1-0, the sole match not ending in a 1-1 tie. During the draw at a Malabo hotel, one representative from each country participated. Mali's football association president, Boubacar Diarra, drew the ball that placed his team in third place. Amara Dabo, Guinea's financial director, then drew the ball confirming his side in second position. Dabo remarked, "The god of football fortune has smiled upon Guinea after this long journey." Former Mali international and former Tottenham and West Ham striker Frederic Kanoute, 37, expressed his disappointment, tweeting, "This is no longer sport. But courage to our Guinean brothers." Both Dussuyer and Mali's coach, Henri Kasperczak, had previously stated that this method of determining team placement was unfair. The Confederation of African Football (CAF) noted that alternative methods for separating teams would be considered in the future. CAF media director Junior Binyam commented, "We must adhere to the regulations while also considering improvements. It's not fair to eliminate a team this way; it truly is the worst decision because everyone expected it to be resolved on the pitch." At Euro 2008, UEFA had ruled that in the event of a draw between Turkey and the Czech Republic, a penalty shootout would determine the winner, had they ended the match even.
A team from Imperial College London analyzed national health survey data from 1994 to 2011 and reported their findings in the Lancet. Their analysis indicated that more individuals were receiving treatment and management for high blood pressure, and that treatments were becoming more effective over the years. They predicted that further improvements could save even more lives. Specifically, the percentage of people with high blood pressure who received treatment increased from 32% in 1994 to 58% in 2011. Additionally, the proportion of those whose blood pressure was brought under control rose from 11% to 37%. Professor Neil Poulter, one of the researchers, estimated that treating patients as effectively in 1994 as they did in 2011 could have prevented up to 100,000 major cardiovascular events. He commented, "This progress is extraordinary; it's saving a huge number of lives." The study projects that 80% of patients could achieve better blood pressure control by 2022 if the current trend continues. Factors contributing to these improvements include advancements in medication, greater public awareness, and financial incentives for healthcare providers. Despite this progress, heart disease remains the leading cause of death in the UK, accounting for 82,000 fatalities annually.
The king's coffin was interred in Leicester Cathedral during a reinterment service on Thursday, with the tomb unveiled the following day. However, some service booklets are now being sold on eBay for up to £200. Cathedral officials suggest that those seeking a memento can purchase all three booklets for just £12.50. King Richard III's remains were discovered beneath a Leicester car park in August 2012. Despite a legal dispute, his remains were reburied in the cathedral amidst a series of events, including three main services: one for receiving the coffin, one for reinterment, and one for revealing the tomb. The reinterment ceremony featured the Countess of Wessex and actor Benedict Cumberbatch. On Saturday, the Diocese of Leicester advised against purchasing the Orders of Service, and Dean David Monteith echoed this advice. He explained, "Dealing with death inevitably involves acknowledging the complexities of individuals. We anticipated this and printed more service booklets than necessary, so they can be purchased at the cathedral for £12.50 each, ensuring no one is exploited and covering our costs while also supporting the cathedral's operations."
Cuba does not permit private ownership of broadcast media; instead, the government controls all mainstream outlets. According to Reporters Without Borders, Cuba is the only country in the Americas without an independent, non-state press. Official media primarily function to disseminate propaganda for the regime. A US blogger was denied a travel visa. Despite this, the US supports Radio-TV Martí, which claims to offer balanced, uncensored news to Cubans. As of 2014, Internetlivestats.com reported 3.1 million internet users in Cuba. Freedom House states that internet access is heavily restricted, making it challenging for most Cubans to connect from home. Bloggers who support dissidents have faced harassment and detention. Cuba is listed as one of Reporters Without Borders' "Enemies of the Internet." Since a submarine cable between Cuba and Venezuela went online, the authorities can no longer attribute internet connectivity issues solely to the US embargo.
On Easter Sunday 2017, Sam Pickering, 20, tragically lost his life while en route to the Sheffield Children's Hospital charity egg run on his Yamaha motorbike. His mother described him as a "beautiful son" and noted his loud, proud nature and constant smile. Fellow bikers honored Sam at the event by revving their engines. The egg run, organized by the Three Shires Honda Owners Club, has been an annual fundraiser for the hospital since 2003, raising over £40,000. In a statement, Sam's mother said, "Easter Sunday 2017 is a day we will never forget, as it was the day Sam lost his life while attending the charity event with his step-dad. Sam was always looking to do good in life." The statement concluded with, "I will never forget you and we will love you forever." A moment of silence followed by 30 seconds of engine revving began the event in honor of Sam. Sam's father, Stephen, expressed, "He was both my son and friend, and I will miss him." Authorities are investigating the incident and have requested any information from the public.
The West of England Local Enterprise Partnership (LEP) has committed £53 million from the City Deal funds, while Bristol City Council will provide the additional £38 million, to be recovered through operator rentals and other revenues. The 12,000-seat indoor venue will be situated within the Temple Quarter Enterprise Zone. The city council is scheduled to make a final decision on the project during their budget meeting on February 18. Mayor George Ferguson commented, "The LEP's recognition of the arena's potential to significantly enhance the local economy is highly positive." Colin Skellett from the LEP stated that the arena will contribute to job creation and regional growth, adding that it is one of several initiatives aimed at achieving these goals. Next month, the city council will initiate the search for an independent operator for the venue. This project forms part of Bristol's strategy to revitalize approximately 70 hectares around Bristol Temple Meads station. The council anticipates completion by June 2017. Under the City Deal framework, certain governance and financial responsibilities have been transferred to local authorities, enabling them to drive economic development and manage public finances more effectively.

A burglary attempt at Ryhall Post Office in Rutland startled its live-in owners and neighbors early Monday morning around 03:30 GMT. Leicestershire Police stated that while there was significant exterior damage, the criminals did not gain entry to the building. Witnesses observed two individuals fleeing in an Audi coupe. The police did not specify whether the 4x4 was stolen. No items were taken from the post office, and nobody was hurt. Miranda Amies, who has managed the post office with her husband Dave for nearly five years, recounted, "We were awakened by a loud noise and the alarms going off. We saw extensive damage; Christmas supplies had been broken on the floor." She also mentioned, "There was a large explosion outside as the car caught fire. It was extremely scary— I feared the entire building would collapse."
Sandra Weir, 41, faces a minimum of 21 years in prison before being eligible for release, following her conviction for murdering 82-year-old Mary Logie. The attack occurred on January 5, 2016, when Weir struck Logie with a rolling pin in her Leven home. Logie, who was also known as Rae, suffered multiple skull fractures and 31 injuries across her head and neck, including defensive wounds from her attempts to defend herself. Weir reportedly left her severely injured and alive for hours before delivering the fatal blows, according to prosecutors. Weir had been stealing money from Logie over time to support her drug habit. At the High Court in Edinburgh, a jury convicted her of murder. Mrs. Logie’s family member, likely her son Ronald, stated, "Justice was served, but we wished for more." Judge Michael O'Grady described the murder as "breathtakingly wicked" and noted it was "callous, cruel, and utterly heartless." Weir, who was also an unofficial caregiver to Logie, had a history of drug addiction and financial exploitation. Detective Chief Inspector Keith Hardie of Police Scotland commented that Weir's actions showed a complete disregard for Logie's wellbeing, describing the attack as "brutal and horrific."
The party criticized Labour for reneging on a 2011 commitment to provide annual GP health checks to those over 50. Tory AM Darren Millar argued that the Add To Your Life self-assessment scheme should be discontinued as a "complete folly." The Welsh Government stated that lessons from the program would inform a review of health advice. Launched in April 2014, the survey aimed to offer an overall health assessment and recommend improved lifestyle choices, accessible via various devices. Millar noted that only 12,939 out of 1.177 million over-50s in Wales had participated in the following 23 months. He asserted that many older individuals lacked the necessary digital skills to engage with the service, and poor connectivity in rural areas further hindered usage. Burns highlighted the vulnerability of those over 50 to health issues such as cancer, high blood pressure, diabetes, and arthritis, and declared the scheme a failure, advocating for funds to be redirected towards GP recruitment and retention. The Welsh Government defended the initiative as innovative, citing initial costs but noting lower ongoing expenses at £70,000 in 2015-16. They also mentioned its use in group discussions facilitated by charities and voluntary organizations. Public Health Wales was reviewing its public health information approach, incorporating lessons learned from Add To Your Life.

Footage from Sunday showed the incident, with eleven of the twelve injured individuals hospitalized. The report, focusing on women in England and Wales, found that about one in fourteen (7%) women born in 1995 had at least one child before turning twenty. The Family Planning Association noted that this decline indicates teenagers possess the skills to make informed decisions. Some, however, argue that the decrease stems from increased pressure to use contraception. According to ONS data, the rate of teenage motherhood has gradually declined and is now similar to that of women born in the early 1920s. In comparison, the peak rate for women born in 1952 was one in five (20%). This trend aligns with the ONS's earlier announcement that teenage pregnancy rates had reached their lowest point since record-keeping began. The most recent data does not include abortions, unlike teenage pregnancy statistics. Additionally, the report examined family sizes across different generations, showing that women born in 1970 had an average of 1.91 children by age forty-five, slightly fewer than their mothers, who had an average of 2.24 children by the same age. However, the ONS suggested that this difference might be due to women having children later in life rather than having fewer children overall. Paul Casey from the Family Planning Association commented that these figures reflect improved access to contraception and support services among teenagers, attributing the change to efforts over a decade. He warned against losing momentum, especially given the ongoing challenges in sex and relationships education and healthcare funding. Dr. Ellie Lee from the University of Kent, on the other hand, expressed concerns, noting that while teenage birth rates were historically low, the downward trend predated the Teenage Pregnancy Strategy. She argued that the strategy's promotion of long-term contraceptives may have had unintended consequences, emphasizing the importance of allowing teens to make informed choices. Dr. Lee also highlighted persistent negative attitudes toward teenage mothers, which she believes are unjustified.
Currently, the police have up to 18 ongoing investigations related to claims stretching back several decades. Deputy Assistant Commissioner Steve Rodhouse noted that police face challenges due to the scarcity of victims coming forward. Amidst claims that the Metropolitan Police covered up sex offenses involving high-profile individuals, the Independent Police Complaints Commission is investigating 14 referrals dating back four decades. In an effort to gather more witnesses, Rodhouse, the head of Crime and Operations at the Met, told BBC Radio 4's Today program that "we are making progress with these inquiries—witnesses are coming forward daily." However, he acknowledged the difficulty posed by the limited number of victims stepping forward. He urged anyone who might have experienced non-recent sexual abuse to come forward, saying, "If you have been a victim, I want you to come forward." Despite the serious allegations of a long-standing cover-up, Rodhouse assured the public that the Met has a solid history of cooperation with internal investigations, noting that the force initiated its own referral to the IPCC. He stated, "We follow the evidence wherever it leads, without fear or favor." The Independent Police Complaints Commission described the cover-up claims as "high-level corruption of the most serious nature." The allegations involve suppressing evidence, hindering investigations, and covering up offenses, particularly when they involved members of parliament and police officers. One recent inquiry, Operation Midland, launched in November, investigates claims of abuse by powerful men from politics, the military, and law enforcement in the 1970s and 1980s. The operation is also exploring allegations that three boys were murdered, potentially elevating the abuse case to a murder investigation. Focused on the Dolphin Square estate near the Houses of Parliament, the investigation has engaged with one young man who is considered a credible witness. While declining to comment on additional witnesses, Rodhouse emphasized that these cases can only advance if more people come forward with confidence. He reiterated, "We thoroughly test all evidence and accounts, but it is ultimately up to the courts to determine the credibility of the witnesses."
A report indicated that a "deadly combination" of errors resulted in the deaths of 11 infants and one mother over nine years at Furness General Hospital. Mr. Hunt expressed his intention to scrutinize the role of the Nursing and Midwifery Council (NMC). The Professional Standards Authority (PSA) will lead the review. The "preventable" fatalities took place between 2004 and 2013 at the Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust’s Furness General Hospital. An investigation revealed that the maternity unit was "dysfunctional," with subpar care delivered by inadequately skilled and knowledgeable staff. Relations between doctors and midwives were also found to be poor, with midwives referring to themselves as "the musketeers" and prioritizing normal childbirth "at any cost." Mr. Hunt previously suggested that the NMC should no longer oversee the statutory supervision of midwives in the UK, proposing instead a model similar to that used for other health professionals. The PSA is set to conduct the review "as soon as possible." He stated, "Considering the NMC's critical role in maintaining high standards in nursing, health visiting, and midwifery, this review will offer the public and the NMC itself independent assurance that all pertinent lessons from the Morecambe Bay incidents have been comprehensively addressed." Jackie Smith, the NMC’s chief executive, responded by saying, "As an open and transparent organization dedicated to continuous improvement, we appreciate the PSA's contribution in assisting us to learn from our management of these cases. We acknowledge that we cannot alter what has already occurred, but we must progress by identifying how we can operate differently in the future." In 2014, the NMC faced criticism from the PSA for its handling of disciplinary cases concerning the scandal-affected Mid-Staffordshire NHS Trust.

However, the president contended that the pipeline had assumed an "overinflated role" in discussions about climate change. The proposed pipeline was intended to transport 800,000 barrels of oil daily from Alberta, Canada, to Steele City, Nebraska, spanning 1,179 miles (1,897 km). According to Mr. Obama, the pipeline would not contribute significantly to the economy over the long term. Republican presidential candidates denounced the decision, with Jeb Bush labeling it an assault on the U.S. economy. The president’s choice coincides with global leaders preparing to convene in Paris to finalize a significant international climate agreement. Although the pipeline project is currently defunct, the controversy surrounding it is unlikely to dissipate anytime soon. Pipeline supporters will probably contest the decision in court, and if a Republican is elected president next year, construction could resume. The pipeline dispute also strained relations between the former Canadian Prime Minister Stephen Harper and President Obama. David Taylor, 55, a taxi driver from Pentre Gwyn, Wrexham, and Philip Burke, 44, from Whiston, Liverpool, acted as couriers. Taylor was sentenced to six years and eight months, while Burke received five years and four months. Burke was found with £45,000. Sentences of six years were given to David Arfon Jones, 31, from Ruabon, and Jason Maddocks-Jones, 35, from Chirk. Laura Roberts, 26, from Cefn Mawr, received an 18-month suspended sentence with 300 hours of unpaid work for setting up banking arrangements for her husband; she was the sole defendant to plead not guilty. Lyndsey Bradley, 26, from Liverpool, McKenzie’s partner, got a 14-month suspended sentence with the same amount of unpaid work for a similar offense. Judge Niclas Parry described the case as involving a sophisticated criminal syndicate that brought cocaine into north Wales, especially around the Wrexham area, generating significant profits and having an incalculable impact on the community.

The Airports Commission's consultation on this matter concludes this Friday, with their recommendation to the government expected shortly thereafter. Following a Supreme Court deadline for reduced air pollution plans, the commission requested additional air quality assessments. They will suggest which of the three shortlisted proposals—two for Heathrow and one for Gatwick—should proceed. Meanwhile, the Green Party, local Heathrow councils, and the Gatwick Area Conservation Campaign argue that the commission has underestimated the environmental impacts of airport expansion. Taylor emphasized that constructing major new runways would significantly worsen air quality, hinder climate change targets, and lead to unprecedented noise increases. Stewart Wingate, Gatwick’s CEO, acknowledged that air quality is now a critical factor in expansion decisions. He argued that Heathrow’s current breach of legal air quality limits makes a third runway there illogical. In contrast, Wingate noted that Gatwick has never exceeded these limits and its location ensures it won't in the future. Matt Gorman, Heathrow’s sustainability director, disagreed, asserting that the commission's analysis confirms an expanded Heathrow will exceed local air quality limits.

Vocalink, which oversees Link—the national network of ATM machines—and Bacs, the direct debit processing system, is set to be acquired by Mastercard for up to £869 million. This follows a £24.3 billion offer by SoftBank for Arm Holdings, prompting worries about increased foreign interest in British firms post-Brexit. Vocalink, currently co-owned by major banks such as Barclays, Lloyds, and HSBC, has been in discussions with Mastercard for eight months. The banks' spokesperson refuted any notion that the sale was opportunistic due to the weakening pound. New Chancellor Philip Hammond highlighted the deal as evidence of Britain’s continued appeal to international investors. Earlier, the Payment Systems Regulator suggested that the banks should divest their stakes in Vocalink to enhance competition and innovation. Hammond stated that the sale would benefit challenger banks, fintechs, and consumers. Mastercard will acquire 92.4% of Vocalink, while the banks will retain a 7.6% stake for at least three years. The acquisition price includes £700 million plus an optional £169 million contingent payment based on performance criteria. Details of these criteria were not disclosed.

From Winston Churchill’s notorious 1912 trip to Belfast to a 1963 Viking re-enactment in a County Down fishing village, a vivid tapestry of Ulster’s past is revealed through previously unseen archive footage. As part of the From Britain On Film project, hundreds of unique films have been made available for free online by the British Film Institute (BFI). The collection includes royal visits, Lord Mayor's shows, and Churchill’s address to nationalist audiences in Belfast, supporting Home Rule. It also features travelogues that showcased Northern Ireland to potential visitors from Great Britain and the United States, aiding tourism before the Troubles. Sports events like the 1930 Ulster Tourist Trophy race, which drew thousands of spectators and gained international attention, are also captured. Among the standout moments are ordinary people going about their lives—workers in spinning mills and construction sites, as well as the first World Ploughing Championships held in Northern Ireland, where a local man won the Golden Plough. Perhaps most intriguing is a 1938 film of the Royal Victoria Hospital in Belfast, offering a rare look at its facilities and a plea from the hospital’s management board to reduce patient wait times. Francis Jones of Northern Ireland Screen, which compiled much of this material, highlighted the project’s goal to preserve these films, noting the emotional impact they have on viewers. The entire archive is accessible on the BFI Player.

Their licenses have been updated to require each company to ensure voice coverage reaches at least 90% of the UK's land by the end of 2017. These firms already fulfill a commitment to cover 90% of UK premises with 3G service, but many still encounter dropped calls outdoors. In December, Ofcom released a report detailing the 2G geographic coverage of three out of the four operators, reflecting their capacity for call and text services. The report noted that Ofcom could not provide data on Three's 3G and 4G voice and call coverage. Culture Secretary Sajid Javid announced the changes, stating, "Too many parts of the UK have suffered from poor mobile coverage, making calls and sending texts difficult." He added, "This progress will finally give the UK the world-class mobile coverage it deserves." The Department for Culture, Media and Sport (DCMS) anticipates a reduction of "not-spots" by two-thirds. In November, Javid warned the operators that failing to improve coverage might lead to forced transfers to rival networks without fees. The networks initially objected, citing concerns about battery life and security. However, they ultimately agreed to the changes. This 2017 deadline aims to reduce mobile signal black spots while traveling through rural areas. Richard Lloyd of the consumer association Which? expressed support, calling for swift action from mobile companies and potential penalties for non-compliance. Additionally, EE, Three, and Vodafone indicated they would match O2's 4G coverage of 98% of UK premises by the end of 2015. EU competition authorities are yet to decide on this plan. Three may benefit from its parent company's attempt to acquire O2 from Telefonica.

The circles, ranging from 2 to 15 meters (7 to 49 feet) in diameter, feature barren ground surrounded by grass. Researchers from the Universities of Strathclyde and Princeton proposed in the journal Nature that these formations might result from plants benefiting from termite activity. Until recently, such circles were believed to be limited to the Namib Desert in southern Africa. However, in 2014, similar formations were noted in Western Australia. Scientists have long debated the origins of these patterns, with some attributing them to sand termites consuming vegetation roots and others linking them to competition among plant species. The new hypothesis aims to reconcile both perspectives, positing that the interplay between termite activity and plant growth creates fairy circles. Termites clear vegetation atop their mounds to conserve water, while plants near the mound thrive due to increased moisture, forming rings. Dr. Juan Bonachela from the University of Strathclyde's Department of Mathematics and Statistics explained, "Both theories have traditionally been seen as mutually exclusive, but our findings bring them together and offer an explanation for global vegetation patterns." This behavior not only aids termite survival in arid conditions but also enhances ecosystem resilience against droughts. The study utilized field data from four continents and computer simulations.
Sudan's government endorsed the independence of South Sudan, home to predominantly Christian and Animist communities who had long fought against Arab Muslim rule in the north. Despite this, unresolved issues, particularly regarding oil revenue sharing and border delineation, have persisted, causing tension between the two nations. Sudan has a history marked by conflict; two rounds of civil war between the north and south claimed over 1.5 million lives, while the ongoing Darfur conflict displaced two million people and resulted in over 200,000 deaths. The country has a population of 45.7 million, with Arabic and English as official languages, and Islam as the predominant religion. Life expectancy is around 60 years for men and 64 years for women. The currency is the Sudanese pound, and the president is Omar Hassan al-Bashir, who has ruled since a 1989 coup and was most recently re-elected in 2015. He faces international arrest warrants for genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity. Sudanese media is heavily regulated, with state TV and radio aligned with the government, while the private press occasionally voices opposition viewpoints. By July 2014, there were 9.3 million internet users in Sudan, with blogging subject to scrutiny. Key historical milestones include the emergence of the Nubian Kingdom of Kush in 2000–1500 BC, the spread of Islam in 651, Ottoman conquest in 1820–21, joint British-Egyptian rule from 1899 to 1955, Sudan's independence in 1956, the introduction of Sharia law in 1983, the start of the Darfur conflict in 2003, and the issuance of an arrest warrant for President Bashir in 2009. South Sudan achieved independence in 2011 after years of conflict with the Khartoum government.

Stephen McCafferty, 62, from Thetford in Norfolk, recently pleaded guilty to raping a 22-year-old woman on Wells Road in Knowle in 1981. He turned himself in and confessed on December 30, 2015, just two weeks after police had visited his home for a DNA sample. He will spend half his prison sentence in custody, according to Bristol Crown Court. In his recorded confession, McCafferty said, "I hate myself. I wish I hadn't done it. You have your family, you don't want to upset them, and you're scared of that." The victim, speaking through the police, stated, "I had completely given up hope that the person who did this to me would ever be caught, but thanks to the police, he was. Even after so many years, the effects of the rape still linger, but a conviction will provide some relief." Avon and Somerset Police noted that McCafferty was unaware that his DNA sample, collected the day before he confessed, matched DNA found on the victim's clothing from a previous case. On July 15, 1981, the victim was cycling near the junction of Wells Road and Somerset Road when she was assaulted and raped; no charges were filed at the time. Years later, DNA evidence from the victim was linked to a 1999 burglary in Long Ashton, but there was no match on the national database. Following another review, McCafferty's name was provided to detectives investigating the burglary, leading to his confession to both the rape and the burglary. Detective Constable Scott Westbrook-Smith remarked, "The impact of this crime on the victim's life has been immense, as 35 years later she still lives in fear and avoids going out alone. Her bravery is inspiring, and we appreciate her support throughout the re-investigation. It's also crucial to recognize how McCafferty's confession has devastated his family."
In 2012, a record 668 rhinos were poached for their horns, marking a nearly 50% increase from the previous year. Most of these killings occurred in the Kruger National Park, the largest wildlife reserve in the country. Rising demand for rhino horn in Asian markets is cited as the primary driver. With about 28,000 rhinos globally, South Africa hosts roughly three-quarters of them. In 2007, only 13 rhinos fell victim to poachers; however, this number has surged significantly since then. Poaching is fueled by beliefs in places like China and Vietnam that rhino horn has medicinal properties, especially for conditions like cancer. Horns can fetch around $65,000 per kilogram, attracting criminal networks equipped with advanced technology. Despite efforts by South Africa's government, including the deployment of soldiers and surveillance aircraft, poaching continues to rise. A recent TRAFFIC report indicates that South Africa's rhinos face a poaching crisis leading to population decline. "Rhinos are being illegally killed, their horns removed, and the animals left to die," TRAFFIC's advocacy director Sabri Zain stated. Since the beginning of the year, five more rhinos have been killed. India, too, is grappling with similar threats, with over 2,200 rhinos in the protected Kaziranga National Park. Despite deploying around 900 armed rangers and guards, poaching has still claimed 18 rhinos in India last year, up from 10 in 2011.

The appointment has sparked concerns among internet rights activists about potential threats to net neutrality laws. Net neutrality ensures equal treatment of all internet traffic, preventing ISPs from charging more for faster access to data-heavy services like Netflix. Advocates, including Google and Facebook, see it as crucial for fostering competition among online companies. Without net neutrality, a new video service unable to afford the "fast lane" would struggle to compete with established platforms such as YouTube or Vimeo. Historically, Republican FCC members have opposed net neutrality, and under President Trump, the commission will likely maintain a Republican majority. During his tenure, Ajit Pai, who previously voted against strengthening net neutrality laws, stated that the FCC should transition from defense to offense, removing regulations he believes hinder investment, innovation, and job creation. His appointment was welcomed by telecom giants Verizon, Comcast, and AT&T, particularly for AT&T, which supports the pending merger with Time Warner, contradicting President Trump’s earlier stance.
Shared spaces aim to enhance driver caution by eliminating traffic signs, pedestrian crossings, and curbs. Lord Holmes of Richmond conducted a survey of 600 individuals, finding that 63% considered their shared space experience poor. Lord Holmes expressed hope that the survey would alter attitudes toward these “dangerous and costly planning mistakes.” The study revealed that over a third of participants actively avoided shared spaces, and there was a notable underreporting of accidents in these areas. Lord Holmes, a blind former Paralympian, stated: “An immediate halt to all shared spaces is crucial. This survey should serve as a wake-up call for all involved in these hazardous and costly planning ventures.” Town centers are transforming into chaotic, unsafe zones akin to third-world traffic zones. Shared spaces are not safe, and overeager councils risk public safety for aesthetic purposes, leading to confusion, chaos, excessive costs, and potential disaster. Although no single definition exists, the Department for Transport (DfT) defines a shared space as a street or area designed to enhance pedestrian movement and comfort by reducing vehicle dominance and allowing all users to share the space rather than adhering to strict, conventional guidelines. Lord Holmes asserts that the survey results show shared spaces do not “improve pedestrian movement and comfort” or “enable all users to share the space.” The report recommends halting such schemes. Shared spaces are also facing legal challenges; solicitor Chris Fry from Unity Law represents five visually impaired individuals suing local authorities for removing pedestrian crossings. He argued that the idea of pedestrians making eye contact with drivers for increased safety is ineffective for the visually impaired, who cannot participate. Local authorities are alleged to be in breach of their duty under the Equality Act to provide reasonable accommodations for shared spaces. The Department for Transport spokesperson noted that local authorities decide the appropriateness of implementing shared space schemes, emphasizing the need to consider the entire community’s needs, especially those of disabled individuals.
For the January-to-March quarter, Samsung anticipates an operating profit of 8.4 trillion won ($7.9 billion; £4.8 billion), marking a 4% decrease compared to the same period last year. This comes after a 6% drop in the previous quarter. The decline reflects the challenges Samsung faces in boosting its earnings due to declining smartphone prices. Young Park, an analyst at Hyundai Securities, noted that Samsung's profits are suffering from shrinking smartphone margins and a slowing growth rate in the industry. The success of Samsung's Galaxy series has been a significant driver of its recent growth, but it now confronts increasing competition from rivals like Apple, HTC, and Chinese manufacturers such as Lenovo, ZTE, and Huawei, all vying for greater market shares. This competition has led to price reductions among vendors, compressing profit margins. Earlier this year, Samsung had foreseen an intensification of competition in the sector. Analysts suggest that Samsung might need to cut costs to maintain its earnings growth, with Greg Roh, an analyst at HMC Investment and Securities, stating, "For additional growth this year, Samsung needs to focus on cost reduction and cutting marketing expenses." Without improving internal efficiencies, Samsung appears to face an inevitable decline in earnings.

For the BBC, Rupa Jha met five diverse individuals in Ahmedabad, India's largest city, to discuss their work experiences. Ms. Parmar recounts, "People are very unfriendly when I'm out working. Gadget use, particularly selfie sticks, is now fully prohibited at theme parks. According to a spokesperson, handheld extension poles are increasingly posing safety risks to visitors and staff. The ban starts on Tuesday at Disney parks in the U.S., followed by Paris and Hong Kong on Wednesday. Selfie sticks enable better selfies but pose hazards such as accidental strikes and interference with ride mechanisms. Visitors must store their sticks in park lockers upon entry. Several venues, including football stadiums, museums, and tennis clubs, have also banned selfie sticks due to safety and disturbance concerns. Apple recently barred them from its WWDC event. St John Ambulance advises using a cold compress if hit and seeking medical attention if symptoms worsen.
