Following an outbreak in Brazil, Colombia, Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica advised delaying pregnancies until more was known about the Zika virus, which can cause microcephaly in newborns. Brazil reported nearly 4,000 cases of babies born with suspected microcephaly since October. The U.S. health authorities warned pregnant women against traveling to over 20 countries in the Americas and beyond where Zika cases have been registered. While the link between microcephaly and Zika remains unconfirmed, a few babies who died tested positive for the virus. The virus itself is not contagious and typically causes mild flu-like symptoms. In Colombia, Health Minister Alejandro Gaviria recommended delaying pregnancies for up to eight months, citing serious potential consequences. Comparable advisories were issued in Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica. Women’s rights activists criticized these recommendations, noting high rates of unplanned pregnancies and prevalence of sexual violence in the region. Brazil’s health ministry reported that 49 babies with suspected microcephaly had died, with Zika infection confirmed in five cases. The country is experiencing the largest known Zika outbreak, predominantly in the northeast, with additional cases in the southeast regions including Rio de Janeiro and São Paulo. Several other Latin American countries have seen a sharp increase in Zika cases, with over 13,500 cases reported in Colombia. The U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention initially warned pregnant women about traveling to more than 20 affected countries and expanded the warning list on Friday.
In total, recorded crime in London decreased by approximately 6%, with 45,000 fewer incidents reported between 2012-13. The Metropolitan Police noted that personal thefts rose by 17% compared to the previous year, with 70% of cases involving stolen mobile phones. Mike Pannett, a former Met officer, expressed concern about sustaining this reduction, stating, "While I'm pleased with these numbers, I'll be focusing on ensuring they can be maintained over the next couple of years." The police attributed the decline to proactive strategies, including the establishment of the Trident Gang Crime Command to address about 250 active criminal gangs in London through a mix of enforcement and prevention. Knife crime dropped by 20%, and gun crime also decreased by 20%. The unit recently lost its mandate to investigate firearm-related deaths, as the force deemed it unnecessary. Homicide rates remained stable at 106 deaths, matching the 103 reported in the previous year, marking the lowest count in over four decades. Victim Support suggested that the 16% increase in reported rapes might reflect greater confidence among victims. Jeff Gartner from the charity commented, "There has been a significant effort by the Met to improve the way rape investigations are conducted, and I believe this is leading to more reports." Mayor Boris Johnson declared, "London remains one of the safest major cities globally, and it's encouraging to see crime rates continue to decline, thanks to the dedicated efforts of our police officers who remain on patrol to combat crime."
Barbara, his wife, announced that he passed away on Tuesday at their Vermont home after battling leukemia. Known for his prolific and celebrated poetry, Kinnell authored over a dozen books throughout his five-decade career. His work, including the 1982 Selected Poems, earned him the Pulitzer Prize and a National Book Award for Poetry, shared with Charles Wright. Notable titles such as The Book of Nightmares, inspired by the Vietnam War, When One Has Lived a Long Time Alone, and Mortal Acts, Mortal Words, further solidified his reputation. One of his most famous poems, "The Bear," describes a hunter's identification with his prey after consuming animal blood and excrement. Other significant works are "After Making Love We Hear Footsteps" and "When the Towers Fell," addressing the September 11 attacks. Born in Providence, Rhode Island, on February 1, 1927, to Scottish and Irish immigrant parents, Kinnell was described by WS Merwin, his Princeton roommate and future U.S. poet laureate, as "a very silent child, almost mute." Merwin recalled Kinnell as "a very generous soul" who had been "like brothers" with him. He credited poetry as his path to happiness, sharing that he developed a profound sense of isolation and found solace in poetry. After studying at Princeton, where he roomed with Merwin, Kinnell became a Vermont resident in 2005 and served as its poet laureate from 1989 to 1993. In 2010, he received the Academy of American Poets' Wallace Stevens Award for lifetime achievement. Kinnell's first marriage to Spanish translator Ines Delgado de Torres produced two children, Fergus and Maud, before they divorced in 1985. He remarried Barbara in 1997.
Dr. Nihal Weerasena was found guilty of various failings in his care of five children and one adult patient while working for Leeds Teaching Hospitals NHS Trust. The Medical Practitioners Tribunal Service (MPTS) sanctioned him, citing a "reckless disregard for patient safety." Among the proven charges, the MPTS noted substandard practice in treating a seven-year-old patient in a complex surgery in 2012. While he was cleared of missing a key event in the operation, he was found to have failed to maintain accurate records for another patient. The tribunal did not prove an allegation regarding his care of a sixth child. The MPTS stated that Dr. Weerasena’s misconduct and lack of insight made his practice incompatible with continued registration, emphasizing the need to protect public safety and the profession’s reputation. He was referred to the General Medical Council in 2014 following a Trust review of pediatric care services, which included evaluating clinical outcomes. This review followed a two-week suspension of children’s heart operations at Leeds General Infirmary due to safety concerns, though a subsequent report found no excessive mortality rates. Dr. Weerasena did not attend the hearing but wrote to explain his absence and claimed he was a victim of a cover-up.
In 2011, British artists made up 11.7% of all albums sold in the US, an increase from 9.8% in 2010, according to the BPI. Adele's second album, 21, led sales with 5.8 million copies, followed by Mumford and Sons' Sigh No More at 1.4 million. Thirty albums by UK acts sold over 100,000 copies in the US that year. This market share was the highest since 2000 and possibly the highest since the 1980s, the BPI noted. Other top-selling British albums included Coldplay's Mylo Xyloto, Susan Boyle's Someone to Watch Over Me, and Florence and the Machine's two albums. Adele's debut album, 19, sold over 900,000 copies, and Marsha Ambrosius, born in Liverpool, was also among the year's top-selling British artists with her debut solo album, Late Nights and Early Mornings. Additionally, Jessie J, Tinie Tempah, Coldplay, Taio Cruz, and The Beatles were among the UK acts that sold more than a million single tracks in the US. BPI chief executive Geoff Taylor predicted that boybands like One Direction and The Wanted, enjoying success on both sides of the Atlantic, would continue this trend. He stated, "These results demonstrate that investments in British talent pay off, helping these artists achieve international success and contributing significantly to the UK economy."
In the past three years, residential sales within five miles of the new train stops have increased, but the rise is smaller compared to the broader local authority areas of Midlothian and the Scottish Borders. Since 2006, house prices along the route have also risen, though the increases are lower in the five-mile zones than in the wider council areas. According to RoS, residential sales in Midlothian have grown by 50.2% and in the Borders by 26.3% within five miles of the new stops. In contrast, the entire council areas saw increases of 60.6% and 30.1%, respectively, with Scotland as a whole experiencing a 28.2% rise. RoS noted that house prices in the Midlothian catchment area have increased by 6.1% to £166,639, and in the Borders by 4.5% to £143,283. Over the same period, council-wide price hikes were 13.4% in Midlothian and 9.5% in the Borders, with Scotland seeing a 16.1% increase. Rhona Mackay, RoS's head of data, stated, "While sales volumes around the new Borders railway stations have increased, they are still lower than in the broader local authority areas. Prices have risen, but more significantly in the wider areas." She added, "It's anticipated that the new line will boost property interest near the stations, and we've already seen some new developments in recent years. We'll be looking at these numbers again once the railway is fully operational." The Borders Railway opens to the public on Sunday, running from Tweedbank to Edinburgh, with seven new stops including those in Midlothian and the Borders.
Glyptodonts wandered across South America for millions of years, until the final Ice Age, with some growing as large as cars. Initially thought to be closely related to armadillos due to their impenetrable shells, recent research suggests they are actually a subfamily within the armadillo lineage. "Glyptodonts can now be classified as a subfamily of giant armadillos," stated Frederic Delsuc from CNRS and Montpellier University. Delsuc and his team used computational methods to predict ancestral armadillo DNA and created RNA 'bait' to extract glyptodont DNA from a small fragment of fossilized shell. This approach enabled them to confidently reconstruct the entire mitochondrial genome of a glyptodont, specifically Doedicurus, one of the largest members of the family. Reaching up to 4 meters in length and weighing around 1.5 tonnes, these formidable yet herbivorous creatures continued to grow larger until their extinction at the end of the Ice Age, approximately 10,000 years ago. This period also saw the presence of giant ground sloths and saber-toothed cats in South America. The researchers are certain they have accurately positioned glyptodonts in the evolutionary tree, placing them deeply within the cingulata order alongside various armadillo species. According to co-author Hendrik Poinar from McMaster University, "Glyptodonts likely originated 35 million years ago within the broader armadillo lineage." A key distinction between ancient and modern armadillos is the glyptodont's massive, domed shell, which lacked the characteristic segmented bands seen in modern armadillos. This unique feature may have evolved concurrently with the dramatic increase in glyptodont size.
In January, the Home Office granted posthumous pardons to numerous deceased men. However, individuals who are still alive must seek to have their convictions expunged. Stonewall Cymru stated that these men had been "wrongfully persecuted," and many were unaware of their ability to seek clearance. This year marks the 50th anniversary of The Sexual Offences Act of 1967, which decriminalized homosexual acts between men over 21 in England and Wales. Approximately 65,000 men were convicted under the previous law, with 15,000 still alive today. Mabli Jones, campaign, policy, and research manager at Stonewall Cymru, argued that men should not bear convictions for acts no longer deemed criminal after the 2012 law change. She noted, "The legal shift was significant progress, but many men suffered unjust persecution under these laws, and their convictions should be removed." Jones also suggested that automatic removal of such convictions from records would be welcome. A Home Office spokesperson explained that convictions are not automatically erased to ensure pardons are granted only to those convicted of consensual behavior involving individuals over 16 years old. The latest ONS data from 2015 indicate that 1.6% of people in Wales identify as LGBTQ+, compared to 1.8% in the UK. Jones pointed out that while attitudes toward LGBTQ+ individuals have improved in Wales, many, particularly young people, continue to face discrimination. Stonewall Cymru's 2017 School Report revealed a decline in anti-LGBTQ+ bullying and language in British schools since 2012, yet 45% of surveyed LGBTQ+ pupils still experienced such bullying. The charity advocates for mandatory relationship education in schools, including safe sex practices in same-sex relationships, and is collaborating with Education Secretary Kirsty Williams to address this issue. Jones expressed hope for substantial progress in making schools more inclusive.
In 2014, Grace Academy was rated "inadequate," but recent inspections have shown overall improvement, with the latest report noting that the academy "no longer requires special measures." Following January's inspection, Ofsted rated the school as "requiring improvement," praising its new leadership team. Academy principal Colin Boxall stated that this was a positive first step. The education watchdog reported that the academy needed improvement in the five key areas: leadership, teaching, student achievement, behavior, and study programs for 16 to 19-year-olds. Inspectors highlighted that many staff members cared deeply about their students' well-being and emphasized the importance of spiritual, moral, and social development. Senior leaders were credited with moving the academy out of special measures, resulting in better teaching, improved behavior, and higher achievement. Mr. Boxall added that they would continue striving for the highest standards for their students.
The 500-meter Aperture Spherical Telescope, or FAST, spans an area equivalent to about 30 football fields. With a cost of approximately $180 million (£135 million), this satellite project aims to explore space and search for signs of extraterrestrial life, according to Xinhua news agency. Beijing continues to prioritize advancing China’s space program. Scientists are set to begin testing and debugging the telescope, according to Zheng Xiaonian, deputy head of the National Astronomical Observatories of China. FAST will supersede the Arecibo Observatory in Puerto Rico, which measures roughly 300 meters in diameter. China has declared its space program to be for peaceful purposes, but the U.S. Defense Department has stated that it is conducting activities intended to prevent adversaries from using space-based assets during a conflict, as reported by Reuters. Additionally, China plans to launch the core module of its first space station in 2018.
A survey of 250 school, youth, and health staff in England indicates that many children are not getting enough to eat during the school day. The Children's Food Trust's poll revealed that 68.1% of respondents observed an increase in the number of families struggling to feed their children over the past two years. Lunchboxes are now containing less fruit and more junk food, according to the survey. Among school staff, 47.5% noted changes in the contents of children’s lunchboxes due to tightening household budgets. One respondent reported seeing "poorer quality sandwich fillings, sometimes just margarine," while another mentioned "fewer processed items—more leftovers or store-cupboard items." However, they also pointed out that some families opt for "cold cooked rice or cold chips with fish fingers." The survey also highlighted a rise in junk food, sweets, and chocolate in lunchboxes, with a decrease in fruit. Additionally, 84.6% of the professionals participating in the survey witnessed children going without sufficient food in their roles, with 84.8% stating that about a third of the children they worked with experienced this. Linda Cregan, the Children's Food Trust's chief executive-designate, commented that many individuals working with children must go beyond their duties to address issues related to hunger and poor nutrition. She emphasized that while parents bear the primary responsibility for ensuring their children eat well, the reality is that this can be extremely challenging. She suggested that local authorities should allocate funding to support children's nutrition through various initiatives such as cooking skills training for family organizations, subsidizing school meals, and providing breakfast clubs and holiday grub clubs. Respondents from Priory School in Lewes reported that their school lunches were generally good, with few instances of chips in lunchboxes. Students noted that packed lunches were cheaper and allowed them to eat the same thing daily, while school dinners offered hot food that was easier and nicer to consume.

The mummy, currently a favorite at Maidstone Museum, is set to undergo a facial reconstruction using a £78,700 grant, potentially revealing more about her past. Believed to be a 14-year-old girl who lived about 2,700 years ago, Ta-Kush was previously known as Ta-Kesh, but recent studies by an Egyptologist suggest her true name is Ta-Kush, meaning "the Kushite lady." Since the 1850s, the mummy has been part of the museum's collection, and upcoming scans by experts at Kent Institute of Medicine and Surgery (KIMS) and Liverpool John Moores University will provide detailed insights into her appearance and possibly her age and gender. Samantha Harris, the museum's collections manager, explains that previous scans have sometimes corrected gender misidentifications. Lyn Palmer, managing the project, notes that the effort aims to personalize Ta-Kush, allowing visitors to visualize her features. The analysis could also pinpoint her origin to the vicinity of Thebes and shed light on her family and name, as inscribed on her coffin. Matt Travail, KIMS' imaging manager, highlights that the scanning process will differ from standard medical procedures, with significant time allocated to analysis and digital reconstruction.

To restore order, additional police support arrived; during the unrest, inmates threw bricks and furniture at the officers, prompting them to use tear gas. An officer shot and killed one inmate who attempted to seize a weapon. Donkor reported that two inmates were killed and five officers injured. Despite the chaos, no prisoners escaped, and the situation returned to calm.

GP Energy has applied to explore beneath land near Commonwood Farm in Borras. The company maintains its intentions are purely exploratory and rules out fracking, a contentious method of extracting gas from rock. However, North East Wales Against Fracking Action expresses local concerns. Wrexham Council will evaluate GP Energy’s proposal to conduct test drilling for coal bed methane. The site is close to the River Dee, and opponents fear that initial testing could potentially pave the way for fracking. Luke Ashley, representing the newly-formed action group, warns that the drilling might signify the beginning of significant industrialization of the countryside east of Wrexham. Ashley explains that the borehole aims to assess gas levels in coal seams stretching from Point of Ayr in Flintshire through Wrexham into Cheshire and Shropshire. Should full-scale extraction occur, it could involve injecting large volumes of water and chemicals into the ground to release the gas. He emphasizes concern about potential contamination of nearby River Dee's drinking water supplies. The group intends to lobby councillors ahead of the planning committee discussion, likely this year. A public meeting took place on Tuesday evening. Fracking involves injecting water and chemicals under high pressure into shale rock to extract gas. Though temporarily banned in the UK after causing seismic activity in 2011, a recent government review deemed it safe under proper monitoring. Dart Energy, the parent company of GP Energy, stated it has no plans for fracking in the Wrexham area and plans to cement the well once exploration is complete. Wrexham Council confirmed the application will be reviewed by the planning committee.

Adam Brookman, a Muslim convert who is voluntarily returning, stated he went to Syria for humanitarian work but was compelled to assist ISIS. Justice Minister Michael Keenan noted Brookman could return but would be investigated. He will be the first Australian to come back from Syria or Iraq since new terror laws were enacted, which criminalize assisting militant groups in the Middle East. Brookman, not yet charged, previously told Fairfax Media he traveled to Syria to use his nursing skills during the overlooked civil conflict. However, after sustaining injuries from an airstrike and being treated in an ISIS-controlled hospital, he was coerced into joining the group. Keenan explained to ABC News that all returning Australians are subject to these laws, asking, "What are we going to do with them if they return to Australia legally?" An AFP spokesperson confirmed that if an Australian commits a crime while in Syria or Iraq, they will be prosecuted. Brookman’s return through Turkey was coordinated with local authorities, and he was met by Australian police who escorted him to Sydney. The new Foreign Fighters legislation suggests traveling to Syria or Iraq alone could lead to charges. It also toughens penalties for recruiting, funding, and training with terrorist organizations and simplifies prosecution for those who travel to designated areas abroad. In December 2014, Australia specifically banned travel to Raqqa province, held by ISIS. Government data indicates over 100 Australians are fighting with extremist groups in the Middle East, with another 150 supporting them in Australia. Additionally, the body of 23-year-old Reece Harding, who died fighting against IS, will be repatriated on Friday. Harding had gone to the Middle East in May to combat IS and was killed by a land mine. His funeral is scheduled for Sunday.
In 1915, around 21,000 British soldiers perished during the Battle of Loos, including about 7,000 Scots. Notably, many casualties came from the 4th Black Watch, based in Dundee. Prince Charles and Camilla attended a service with approximately 1,000 others to honor the fallen. As the Royal Colonel of the 3rd Battalion (The Black Watch), The Royal Regiment of Scotland, Prince Charles' great uncle, Captain Fergus Bowes-Lyon, died during the battle's third day, which took place near northern France. About 8,000 lives were lost within the first four hours of the battle on September 25, 1915. Centenary events commenced on Friday with the lighting of a beacon atop Dundee's war memorial. On Saturday, a parade of roughly 250 current service members and 300 veterans preceded a transformation of City Square into a "cathedral," featuring four ministers representing different churches. Charles, known as the Duke of Rothesay in Scotland, laid a wreath alongside the first minister and Dundee's Lord Provost. At the reception, he remarked, "The regiments involved at Loos represent Scotland's finest. They bravely fought for our freedom, leaving their homes to defend us. Their example is profoundly humbling." First Minister Nicola Sturgeon stated, "Given that about 30,000 Scots served at Loos, its impact was felt across all communities in Scotland." Lord Provost Bob Duncan noted, "The battle affected every Scottish regiment and was Dundee's darkest hour. The severe losses, especially among local Black Watch battalions, had a lasting impact on the city." Charles and Camilla will conclude their visit with a reception for military personnel and veterans and an exhibition showcasing the Battle of Loos created by Dundee schoolchildren.
Part of a new plan to enhance the management of type 1 diabetes in Scotland involves increased spending. About 30,000 individuals in Scotland are affected by this condition. A key focus will be on boosting the use of insulin pumps, which can eliminate the need for multiple daily injections. Currently, 3,200 insulin pumps are in use in Scotland. Along with these pumps, additional continuous glucose monitoring devices will also be provided. Karen Addington, CEO of the diabetes charity JDRF, stated, "Evidence indicates that this diabetes technology, when combined with appropriate support and training, significantly improves quality of life." She welcomed the announced funding to enhance access to this technology. Health Secretary Shona Robison noted, "Insulin pumps and continuous glucose monitoring systems greatly simplify diabetes management for some individuals. Effective control is crucial for better health outcomes and preventing complications. This new investment will expand access to this equipment, enabling more people to live healthier lives."
The Sun, the largest object in the Solar System, accounts for about 99% of its total mass. With a diameter about 109 times that of Earth, the Sun could contain more than a million Earths within it. Scientists believe the Sun, along with the planets, originated from the solar nebula roughly 4.59 billion years ago. Its surface temperature is around 5,500 degrees Celsius, while it reaches an astonishing 15.6 million degrees Celsius at its core. While the Sun's temperature varies, cooler areas known as sunspots occasionally appear. Comprising mostly hydrogen, it also contains some helium and minor amounts of heavier elements like oxygen and iron. Sunlight travels to Earth in approximately 8 minutes, but always avoid looking directly at the Sun to protect your eyesight.
On Friday, Anne Dufourmantelle jumped into the water at Pampelonne beach near St Tropez to rescue two children who were struggling in rough conditions. The children were later saved by lifeguards and were unhurt. Françoise Nyssen, the French culture minister, described Dufourmantelle as "a great philosopher who enriched our lives." According to witnesses, Dufourmantelle, 53, was 50 meters from the children when the beach warning changed from orange to red, prohibiting swimming. Despite her efforts, she was swept away by a strong current and could not be revived. Her funeral will take place in Ramatuelle, southern France, on Tuesday. Dufourmantelle authored several essays emphasizing the importance of taking risks and accepting the inherent dangers in life, as detailed in her 2011 book, "Praise of Risk." Nyssen noted on Twitter that Dufourmantelle "helped us live and think about the world today." In a 2015 interview with French daily Liberation, Dufourmantelle argued that absolute security is a myth, stating, "Life is metamorphosis; it begins with this risk." She warned that fear is often exploited politically to restrict freedom and that perceived threats can foster a state of helplessness, promoting overprotection. Dufourmantelle earned a philosophy doctorate from Paris-Sorbonne in 1994 and later practiced psychoanalysis.

Ecometrica, headquartered in Edinburgh, has created software to swiftly analyze satellite imagery, enabling detection of threats like illegal logging or agricultural expansion to tropical forests. Funded by the UK Space Agency, the company will collaborate with experts from six other countries—Brazil, Colombia, Ghana, Indonesia, Kenya, and Mexico—to protect endangered ecosystems. The "Forests 2020" initiative targets improved management and conservation of 300 million hectares of tropical forests. Ecometrica will partner with the University of Edinburgh, the University of Leicester, and Carbomap for this project. The funding stems from the UK Space Agency’s International Partnership Programme, designed to leverage space technology for the benefit of underdeveloped nations. Ray Fielding, head of the IPP, stated, "This program aims to find innovative ways that space technology can assist in forest preservation, an area highlighted by the UN as crucial for sustainable development." Dr. Richard Tipper, Ecometrica’s executive chairman, noted, "Technology is essential for ensuring that conservation efforts are both effective and well-directed. Early detection of threats through Earth Observation platforms will enhance the speed and efficiency of ground responses."

Islwyn MP Chris Evans stated that 34 different schemes since the 1970s have not addressed long-term and youth unemployment effectively. Jobcentre Plus was founded in 2002 to assist jobseekers. Despite this, the Department for Work and Pensions (DWP) reported helping 10,000 individuals into employment in Wales last year. Evans contended that both Conservative and Labour administrations have not implemented sufficiently innovative welfare reforms. He criticized prior programs like the Youth Training Scheme in the 1980s and Labour's New Deal in the 1990s as ineffective. Evans argued, "We keep repeating the same old solutions without success; we need to change this." He plans to advocate in a parliamentary debate that Jobcentre Plus should be abolished, instead recommending that the budget be allocated to contract services with charities and recruitment agencies that have demonstrated success. Evans noted, "For many, Jobcentre Plus feels like a place for sanctions, not finding work." In response, the DWP asserted that Jobcentre Plus offers an "internationally respected service" tailored to individual and local labor market needs, adding that thousands of people are helped daily, with 10,000 more in work in Wales over the past year. The DWP also highlighted that three-quarters of those leaving Jobseeker's Allowance are employed after eight months, marking a record of success. Evans called on the Labour party to adopt radical approaches, noting, "We lost the election by staying comfortable; we must now address topics that make us uneasy to remain credible opposition."
Around 360 million account credentials, including emails and passwords, were reportedly uncovered. Hold Security also discovered 1.25 billion email addresses without corresponding passwords. The credentials, identified over the past three weeks, are believed to include those from major email providers. Security expert Graham Cluley described the batch as exceptionally large, calling it "Godzilla-sized" and a "monster." He noted that despite potential duplicates, the data could be a rich resource for cybercriminals. Hold Security stated that their findings stemmed from "multiple breaches under independent investigation." In a blog post, they highlighted the "mind-boggling" numbers and emphasized the need for online security. Cluley warned that such details could enable not just spam and phishing attacks but also unauthorized access to multiple accounts due to reused login credentials. Reuters highlighted concerns that this discovery might pose a greater threat than stolen credit card data, as the compromised usernames and passwords could potentially access online bank accounts, corporate networks, and health records. Hold Security's Alex Holden confirmed that the credentials originated from unreported breaches and stressed the urgency of identifying victims. The batch also contained email addresses without passwords, useful for launching spamming and phishing campaigns.

He stated that if the tidal lagoon's lifetime exceeded 100 years, the UK government subsidy could match nuclear energy costs. Tidal Lagoon Power, the company behind the project, maintains confidence in achieving a competitive price. Raynor, then 24 and from Barnsley, pleaded guilty to four charges of causing death by dangerous driving. His passenger, Matthew Todd, 23, also from Barnsley, received a nine-year jail term for the same offenses. At Leeds Crown Court, ten others were sentenced to between 42 and 52 weeks for their role in encouraging the dangerous driving that led to the fatalities of Ryan Beal, 20, Brandon Brown, 20, Alexandra Binns, 18, and Terrie Kirby, 16. During the trial, the incident was compared to scenes from The Fast and The Furious. The families of the victims stated, "No sentence can compensate for the loss on September 27, 2015. We lost young adults just starting their lives, and the potential futures we no longer have. While the pain diminishes, it never disappears, and our families will forever carry the knowledge of our incomplete lives." Detective Inspector Richard Holmes of West Yorkshire Police described the crash as a tragedy for the victims' families and the officers involved. "That night, Raynor's car, with Todd as a passenger, was clocked at 140mph hitting the quad bike, resulting in the tragic injuries we witnessed," he said. "Driving at such a speed on a 60mph road is an act of extreme recklessness. Raynor posed a significant risk to anyone on that road, and the consequences were devastating." Stephen Hebden, 23, and Nathan Jackson, 21, both from Barnsley, received 12-month jail terms for encouraging dangerous driving.
An investigation revealed that fraudulent borrowers had checked out thousands of books. Library staff have been placed on suspension while officials determine whether these fake accounts may have led to increased state funding for the libraries. According to staff, this scheme helped preserve popular titles that would otherwise have needed to be repurchased. Officials from the Lake County public resources department, which oversees libraries in the Florida area, informed local newspapers about the existence of these fictitious readers at the East Lake County library through an anonymous source. An investigation by Lake County municipal staff uncovered that senior library staff had created multiple fake identities, including one named Chuck Finley after a former Major League Baseball player. This fake borrower had checked out over 2,600 books in nine months, with some being returned within an hour. The official report suggested that these fake readers artificially increased readership by nearly 4%, impacting the allocation of official funds. Last year, libraries in and around Lake County received approximately $1 million from local government. The head librarian at East Lake stated that the false accounts were set up to maintain popular titles, as discarding unread books would mean unnecessary repurchases. He also claimed that other libraries employed similar "dummy" reader systems. Digital activist Cory Doctorow argued that the logging system had become a restrictive framework that ignored the practical insights of the librarians. The librarians did not manipulate the system for personal gain but to improve it by reflecting real-world circulation patterns.

Patients diagnosed with the disease are typically scheduled for follow-up eye exams, but it has come to light that nearly 500 individuals were not given these tests starting in 2010. These tests are crucial for detecting retinal damage from elevated blood sugar levels. Since then, health officials have reached out to those affected, offering new appointments. Diabetic retinopathy, a frequent complication of diabetes, results from high blood sugar damaging the retina. Left untreated, it can lead to blindness. It was discovered that the referral systems to local clinics for specialized eye examinations were ineffective. The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Trust is investigating the circumstances surrounding these errors. Diabetes UK expressed concern over the lack of regular check-ups for these patients. The organization noted that diabetic retinopathy often shows no symptoms in its early stages, making it difficult for individuals to realize they have a problem until significant damage has occurred. Sheila O'Brien from Cumbria Societies for the Blind commented, "This situation is not ideal, but it’s good to see they’ve identified the issue and are looking into what went wrong. What matters now is ensuring such incidents don’t recur." Approximately 160 people in the Morecambe Bay area, 143 in North Cumbria, and nearly 200 in the Blackpool region were impacted. George Nasmyth, the deputy medical director of The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust, stated, "We are addressing an issue concerning several of our diabetes patients and their annual eye screening appointments. We are collaborating with the Diabetic Eye Screening Programme, NHS England, and other NHS Trusts to ascertain the exact cause of these issues and to ensure we learn from them to prevent future occurrences. All affected patients have been contacted and invited to reschedule their screenings. For any concerns, patients can contact 0845 0500 109."
The Prudential Regulation Authority and the Financial Conduct Authority of the Bank of England aim to discourage irresponsible risk-taking and short-term behavior. New regulations stipulate that senior managers could forfeit bonuses for up to 10 years in misconduct cases. FCA chief Martin Wheatley stated these rules are part of a broader effort to instill accountability in the City, describing them as a key step to rebuilding public trust in financial services. These rules, resulting from nearly a year of consultation, apply to banks, building societies, and certain investment firms. Key changes include: - Managers cannot receive buyouts of unpaid bonuses from new employers earlier than if they remained at their original company. - The deputy governor for prudential regulation at the Bank of England, Andrew Bailey, noted that effective financial regulation should incentivize individuals to be more responsible for their actions. - The goal is to reward behavior that promotes a robust risk management culture and enhances the safety and soundness of financial institutions.

The family had a history of violence and were known to Haringey children's services, which acknowledged that more could have been done to protect the child. A serious case review indicated that risk factors were not adequately addressed. Sir Paul Ennals, chair of the Haringey Local Safeguarding Children Board, stated: "Collectively, we sincerely regret that there were several areas where we could, and should, have improved. There were numerous instances during the first three months of D's life, and even earlier during her mother's pregnancy, when frontline staff could have acted differently. While we cannot guarantee that different actions would have prevented the abuse, we can assert that many of our agencies could and should have responded more appropriately." The report revealed that the baby's mother was vulnerable, having been placed in care due to neglect and violence, and was 17 years old at the time of her pregnancy. The father was 34. Baby D also lived with her grandfather and a 14-year-old uncle, both with histories of violence and criminal behavior. Although the family was suspected of gang involvement and drug-related activities, social workers did not consider these risks seriously enough, according to the serious case review. The review found that a culture prioritizing targets and deadlines led to insufficient critical analysis in assessments. Baby D was seen by hospital doctors on April 29, 2013, and the potential rib fractures were believed to have occurred when she was about a month old. The baby was placed in care, and the parents were arrested for suspected abuse or neglect, though the criminal case was later dropped due to issues with the key witness. Haringey social services acknowledged the findings of the serious case review and expressed satisfaction with finding the child a successful care placement. NHS England noted that more could have been done by the NHS in London to protect Baby D's health and safety and prevent the injuries. Top wheelchair tennis players, including Grand Slam winners and Paralympians, have competed in the inaugural Surbiton Wheelchair Tennis Tournament, a warm-up for Wimbledon. Thirteen of the world's elite players participated, giving them the chance to test their grass-court skills before heading to SW19, where three British players will defend their titles. In wheelchair tennis, athletes use specialized chairs and the ball can bounce twice, played on standard-sized courts. Lucy Shuker, a double Paralympic bronze medalist, took up the sport after a motorbike accident left her paralyzed from the waist down. She represents Great Britain at the Paralympics and has won doubles bronze medals at London 2012 and Rio 2016. Shuker says, "I'm the most disabled player on tour, but daily challenges have helped me develop techniques that enable me to compete." Britain's Alfie Hewett, who won the men's singles at this year's French Open, aims to defend his Wimbledon title after partnering Gordon Reid to win the men's doubles last year. Hewett, who started playing in 2005 and made his Paralympic debut in Rio, says, "Grass requires a different strategy, and this tournament is crucial for my preparation." On the first day of the tournament, reigning Australian Open champion Gustavo Fernandez defeated Hewett in a reverse match. The Tennis Foundation, organizers of the event, hope this warm-up will provide an advantage for Wimbledon, where wheelchair events begin on July 13.
Phil Everly passed away in Burbank, a Los Angeles suburb, due to complications from lung disease, his wife Patti informed the Los Angeles Times. "We are absolutely heartbroken," she stated, noting that the illness stemmed from a lifetime of smoking. Phil Everly, alongside his brother Don, formed the Everly Brothers, a prominent pop act during the 1950s and early 1960s. Don, in a statement to the Associated Press, said, "I loved my brother very much. I always thought I'd be the one to go first. 'The world may mourn an Everly Brother, but I mourn my brother Phil Everly." Their hit songs included "Wake Up Little Suzie," "Cathy's Clown," "Bye Bye Love," and "All I Have To Do Is Dream." Phil Everly's son Jason Everly told the Associated Press that he died on Friday from chronic obstructive pulmonary disease. Rolling Stone magazine hailed the Everly Brothers as "the most important vocal duo in rock." The magazine's biography revealed that Phil and his older brother Don were the offspring of Midwestern country music singers Ike and Margaret Everly and performed on their family radio show while growing up. Between 1957 and 1962, the Everly Brothers achieved 19 Top 40 hits, according to the Associated Press. They influenced artists like the Beatles and the Beach Boys. Phil Everly and Don had an onstage breakup in 1973, leading to a decade-long estrangement, but Phil later told Time magazine that their relationship endured. "Don and I are infamous for our split," Phil said, "but we're closer than most brothers." The Everly Brothers were inducted into the Rock 'n' Roll Hall of Fame in its inaugural year, 1986, and received a lifetime achievement award at the Grammys in 1997. Singer-songwriter Charlie Daniels paid tribute by tweeting, "Rest in peace Phil Everly. You guys brought us a lot of pleasure back in the day."

In September 2017, ministers aimed to reform the funding system, making future front-line health workers pay for their degrees. However, the Royal College of Nursing and Unison cautioned that this might deter potential NHS candidates and exacerbate staffing shortages. Student nurses, midwives, and professionals like physiotherapists would be impacted. Currently, they receive bursaries ranging from £4,500 to £5,500, plus a £1,000 annual grant, with no course fees. The government proposed eliminating these benefits and imposing university fees similar to those faced by other students. While ministers argued this would boost nurse enrollment by around 10,000 due to the higher application-to-place ratio, unions contended it might discourage applicants. Unison and the National Union of Students commissioned a study by London Economics projecting a 6.5% decrease in applicants, from 31,000 to 29,000 annually. They highlighted the unique nature of nursing education, where students spend half their time working in the NHS and have limited opportunities for paid work. RCN General Secretary Janet Davies expressed concern, especially regarding older students entering the field later, noting the importance of workforce diversity. With the NHS reportedly short of about 50,000 front-line staff, Davies urged a full risk assessment before proceeding. Nurses and other healthcare workers planned a rally in central London to protest against these plans. Unison's Christina McAnea warned that this move could worsen an already challenging situation, potentially compromising patient safety. The Department of Health maintained that the reforms intended to increase training numbers and ensure students received financial support through loans.

Archaeologists have discovered some of the best-preserved Bronze Age dwellings in the UK at a quarry in Cambridgeshire. These structures, dating back to around 1,000-800 BC, were preserved when they fell into a river during a fire and were covered in silt. The excavation, led by the Cambridge Archaeological Unit, is a delicate process with many amazing artefacts already uncovered. The two to three circular wooden houses, built on stilts, were part of a settlement that was partially destroyed by fire 3,000 years ago. Notably, the stilts, roof structure, and walls have been unearthed, a rare find given that most Bronze Age sites lack timber remains. The roof is believed to have fallen during the fire, potentially preserving the contents within. As of now, only a small section of one house's interior is being examined, revealing a "very small, delicate wooden box" among other items. Archaeologists are thrilled with these findings, which include an intact fineware pot and animal bones. The site, described as unique by David Gibson, could hold more uncommon household objects, tools, and furniture. The excavation, funded by Historic England and Forterra, continues for another four months, with the potential for further significant discoveries.
Mayor Boris Johnson appealed to the Supreme Court after the police were initially ordered to cover a £60 million bill under the 1886 Riot Damage Act. Insurance companies argue that the 19th-century law covers all damages from the Enfield warehouse fire. The Supreme Court has stated that a final verdict is not anticipated for several months. On August 8, 2011, the warehouse was torched during widespread riots following Mark Duggan’s death in Tottenham. Over 1.5 million CDs were ruined, constituting what lawyers deemed "the largest arson attack in Europe." Mr. Justice Flaux reported that total damages exceeded £60 million, with claims of £9.8 million for lost profits and £1.6 million for lost rent. According to the Riot Damage Act, judges explained that damage from rioting should be compensated from police funds. Initially, the High Court decided the police should bear the primary damage costs but not consequential losses like lost profits and rent. In a later Court of Appeal ruling, insurers prevailed, asserting the Metropolitan Police should cover all expenses. Following this judgment, the BBC’s legal correspondent noted the potential impact on other businesses affected by the riots. Mayor Johnson, who funds the police, sought a Supreme Court ruling to settle the dispute.
The UK athlete and campaigner aimed to meet Russian Minister of Defence Sergey Shoygu to discuss establishing a new marine reserve in the Ross Sea. Despite ongoing international tensions, Mr. Pugh believed their conversation could facilitate global agreement on the proposed protected area. The Ross Sea, claimed by New Zealand and explored by teams led by Scott, Shackleton, and Amundsen, remains one of the least disturbed ocean regions on Earth. However, there is a stalemate over granting it special protection. Speaking about his Russian encounter, Mr. Pugh told the BBC: "This is the first time Antarctica has been discussed at such a high level. Until now, it hasn't reached this point. They showed interest in dialogue and peace." He added, "When you strip away the barriers, you can connect as individuals." Mr. Pugh, who serves as the UN Environment Programme Patron of the Oceans, cautioned that the process would be lengthy, especially due to international disagreements over Ukraine. "The relationships between the US, Russia, and the UK are severely strained; this is the most significant crisis these parties have faced since the Cold War. Currently, no bilateral talks are taking place." In preparation for a series of Antarctic swims, Mr. Pugh recently broke his own record by swimming further south into the Ross Sea’s Bay of Whales, describing it as "the most terrifying place" he had ever swum. The average sea temperature was -1°C, with air temperatures reaching -37°C. The Ross Sea Marine Protected Area (MPA), discussed for the past four years, has failed four times. The last proposal was vetoed by Russia and China. If established, it would cover an area equivalent to the combined sizes of the UK, Germany, and France, requiring 24 countries and the EU to agree. Mr. Pugh expressed hope that Antarctica could serve as a bridge, offering a place to find common ground, with the potential for this to extend beyond the continent.

Analysis: Bradford's Bollywood Legacy By Shabnam Bashir Mahmood, BBC Asian Network News The arrival of a Bollywood superstar like Ranveer Singh in Bradford is significant for the city's Indian cinema enthusiasts. The city's love affair with Bollywood dates back nearly 50 years, when it became a hub for South Asian immigrants seeking cinematic entertainment. Several cinemas opened to showcase these vibrant films, providing escapism for settlers from Pakistan and India. For many, these films were the sole source of entertainment. My father and his brother were among the pioneers who showed these films in Bradford, often hosting notable actors like Waheeda Rahman, Jayanti Mala, and Helen. I attended my first Bollywood premiere at just six months old. The passion for Bollywood remains strong among Bradford's South Asian community and has now spread to other groups as well.

He claimed he intended to divert floodwaters away from his property, but prosecutors stated that his actions raised water levels in Thorney. It remains unclear if this directly led to property damage. The Parrett Internal Drainage Board initiated the private prosecution. Goddard violated both a local by-law and the Land Drainage Act by opening the sluice gate. The report advocates shifting from existing guidelines that recommend medication only when blood pressure exceeds a specific threshold, acknowledging that lifestyle factors significantly impact blood pressure control. A global expert group's analysis of over 100 large-scale trials involving around 600,000 participants from 1966 to 2015 supports treating individuals based on their unique risks, particularly those with conditions like smoking, high cholesterol, and diabetes. This approach could enhance the prevention of heart attacks and strokes. Moreover, the study indicates that once on treatment, blood pressure could be lowered beyond current targets. It also reinforces that patients might benefit from lowering their blood pressure regardless of their initial levels, through lifestyle modifications or medications. However, the reduction's effectiveness varies with starting blood pressure levels. While not advocating universal medication, the researchers stress balancing potential side effects against benefits. Professor Liam Smeeth highlighted that while the findings are crucial for high-risk individuals, not everyone can tolerate low blood pressure, necessitating a careful consideration of side effects. Dr. Tim Chico from the University of Sheffield emphasized that lifestyle changes can effectively manage blood pressure without relying solely on medication.
Six climbers, comprising three men and three women, were caught in an avalanche on Bidean Nam Bian around 14:00. An alarm was initially raised by one of the male climbers. The sixth climber, a woman, remains in critical condition at a hospital in Fort William. The group had been descending from a peak on the south side of the valley when the slope beneath them gave way. They were near the Church Door Buttress when the snow slope collapsed, and five climbers were swept down the mountain, buried in ice and snow. One female climber was found alive but suffered severe head injuries. According to John Grieve, leader of the Glencoe Mountain Rescue Team, the initial notification came from two other mountaineers who discovered one casualty on the snow. Afterward, the male survivor from the climbing party informed the police that additional individuals were missing. Grieve noted, "The first call to the police was from two other climbers who found someone lying near where they were. It turned out to be just one casualty, but it became evident that others were missing as the male survivor reported so." A nearby St Munda's Church will host prayers for the victims on Sunday. Reverend Moira Herkes stated it was the most severe accident in the area in recent years. Scotland's First Minister, Alex Salmond, expressed that it was an "appalling tragedy," adding, "Our immediate thoughts and prayers are with the families of those lost. Markus Reichel acknowledged providing over 200 documents to the CIA over four years in exchange for at least €80,000 (£63,000, $90,000), as reported by the Munich court. These documents included the real and cover names and addresses of German agents abroad. Found guilty of treason, he also passed three documents to the Russian secret service. Reichel, who had worked at the Federal Intelligence Service (BND) since 2007, initially handled mail and had access to classified and top-secret information. Between 2008 and 2014, he received between €10,000-20,000 annually for the secrets he shared at secret meeting points in Austria. He copied documents at work, took them home, scanned them, and sent them to his handler, according to AFP. After several years, seeking new experiences, he approached the Russian consulate in Munich but was caught when his email with classified documents was intercepted. During his trial, which started in November, Reichel admitted to sharing intelligence for both thrill and recognition, noting that no one trusted him at BND. In his final court statement, he apologized and sought forgiveness, as reported by Sueddeutsche Zeitung. His arrest occurred amidst tensions between the US and Germany following revelations of US surveillance activities in Germany by former CIA agent Edward Snowden.

At the Football Association's Annual General Meeting on Wednesday, new rules were ratified that will apply to players across the top eight tiers of English football. Currently, players are barred from betting on competitions involving their own clubs. The updated regulations now prohibit players from sharing insider information with others for betting purposes. Players like Tottenham’s Andros Townsend, on loan at Crystal Palace from Stoke City last season, and Newcastle’s Dan Gosling, who will join Bournemouth next season, have previously violated these betting regulations. Townsend was fined £18,000 by the FA in June 2013, Jerome received a £50,000 penalty in August 2014, and Gosling was fined £30,000 in March. The new rules, effective from August 1st, extend to betting on all matches worldwide and include "football-related matters" such as transfers, managerial changes, and team selections. These rules will apply to players in the Premier League, Championship, League One and Two, Conference, and the top Northern, Southern, and Isthmian leagues.

The State of Britain's Dormice report reveals that hazel dormice are extinct in 17 English counties. Over 25 years, more than 100,000 records were analyzed by researchers. The report indicates that these dormice are now vulnerable to extinction in Britain. Since 1998, trained volunteers have been collecting data on the hazel dormouse (Muscardinus avellanarius), a project that is among the longest-running small mammal monitoring initiatives globally. These creatures primarily inhabit hedgerows and woodlands, constructing ball-like nests from bark in summer and hibernating on or near the ground from October to May. Finding them is challenging; even a large area equivalent to two football fields may house just four dormice. A distinctive indicator of their presence is a nibbled hazelnut. Recent findings show that dormice populations have declined by nearly 40% over the past 16 years. Today, they are confined to the Welsh borders and southern England; they've never been spotted in Scotland or Northern Ireland. Ian White, dormouse officer at the People's Trust for Endangered Species, describes the decline as "dramatic" and emphasizes the urgent need for action. In 1885, dormice were found in 49 English counties, but today, they are confirmed in only 32 counties, excluding those where they have been reintroduced. White identifies several critical factors contributing to their decline: habitat loss, fragmented landscapes, and changes in land management practices. Additionally, they are susceptible to weather changes, particularly wetter springs and summers, which make foraging difficult, and warmer winters that disrupt hibernation. Despite the challenges, White notes increased public interest and enthusiasm for conservation efforts. Over 850 dormice have been reintroduced at 26 sites, with some showing positive signs like breeding or expanding into new areas. PTES monitors 400 sites and compiles a national database from public submissions, while also training woodland managers and landowners.

BAE Systems planned to conduct 17 flights using a modified Jetstream 31 aircraft capable of autonomous navigation. While human pilots would manage takeoff and landing, the plane would operate autonomously during a 300-mile journey from Lancashire to Inverness. The trial aimed to evaluate both the aircraft's piloting software and sensor systems, which monitor clouds and nearby aircraft. For autonomous flight, the Jetstream would rely on satellite data and onboard systems that track radio signals from other planes. Additionally, the aircraft features a camera system that detects other aviation users, regardless of whether they emit warning signals, and monitors the surrounding skies to identify adverse weather conditions, enabling it to reroute to avoid turbulence and other challenging conditions. During the test, the aircraft would fly through underutilized airspace at an altitude of approximately 15,000 feet, with the entire trip expected to last around 90 minutes. Maureen McCue, BAE Systems' head of research and technology, described the trials as "exciting" and noted that the company was collaborating with the UK's National Air Traffic Services to assess how well the autonomous aircraft could coexist with traditionally piloted aircraft.

Many Labour MPs perceive Momentum as a distinct entity within the party, sometimes seen as a platform for the far-left and a means to remove those opposed to Jeremy Corbyn's leadership. Who are these Momentum supporters and what do they seek? I met some at a Brixton gathering, where the image of traditional hard-left activists was dispelled. Notably, there were no Trotskyite beards present, and no attempts to sell newspapers. What unites Steve, Samira, Nadine, and Roland is their fervor driven by Corbyn’s campaign. They are his supporters, often referred to as foot soldiers. Steve, describing himself as a "middle-aged, BBC Radio 4 enthusiast," joined the Labour Party immediately after Corbyn's victory, while Samira joined after the same event, feeling for the first time she had someone to support and trust. Momentum currently boasts around 60,000 members, charges no fees, relies heavily on social media for organization, and has about 50 local groups across the UK. Their stated goal is to foster a mass movement for genuine progressive change. However, concerns over potential infiltration by the Socialist Workers Party (SWP) and other far-left organizations have emerged. Labour MP Emma Reynolds characterizes Momentum as a "parallel organization" and suggests it needs to clarify its objectives and membership. She raises doubts about the involvement of SWP or similar groups and fears that Momentum aims to depose MPs from the Labour Party. Some far-left groups, such as the Trade Unionist and Socialist Coalition (TUSC), express interest in collaborating with Momentum. TUSC chairman Dave Nellist, a former Labour MP expelled for his association with Militant, posits that Momentum was established to provide Corbyn with a left-leaning power base and to democratize the Labour Party by making the party conference the ultimate policy-making body, reinstating Clause 4, advocating for public ownership of industries, and reintroducing mandatory re-selection, viewed as a method to expel "moderate" MPs. The most significant concern among Labour MPs centers on re-selection. Nadine at Brixton assures that Momentum does not discuss de-selecting current MPs but acknowledges potential opportunities due to boundary changes or MPs stepping down. Momentum clearly aims for change, both in creating an anti-austerity movement and transforming the Labour Party into a more Momentum-like structure—essentially a Corbyn-led Labour Party. This transformation is deeply unsettling for many Labour MPs.

No one was injured in the fire that broke out at Mill Hill in the town around 04:30 GMT on Saturday. The blaze began in a rented property and quickly spread to two neighboring family homes. The rented house was completely destroyed, while the other two suffered damage. A family managed to escape from one home after being warned by a neighbor. They reportedly lost several items, including Christmas gifts for their children. Thirty-five firefighters and seven vehicles responded to extinguish the fire, which is thought to have been accidental, likely due to smoking materials. Fire service group commander Victor Spence noted, "Upon arrival, our first crew found a significant fire in a first-floor bedroom. The occupants had already evacuated, so we focused on evacuating nearby houses to avoid any casualties. We believe the fire was caused by an accident involving smoking materials." The council has cordoned off the affected properties due to potential structural instability.
Vine allowed users to share six-second looping video clips. On Thursday, Twitter announced it would close Vine, following a 9% workforce reduction due to slow social network growth. The company stated, "In the coming months, we'll discontinue the Vine mobile app." One of Vine's co-founders, Rus Yusupov, tweeted, "Don't sell your company!" Twitter bought Vine in 2012 for around $30 million, integrating its video feature into the main Twitter platform and launching Periscope. The blog assured users, "Nothing is happening to the apps, website, or your Vines today. You can continue accessing and downloading your Vines, and the website will stay up because we believe it's important to watch all the amazing Vines created." Analyst Ian Fogg commented, "Twitter aimed to constrain communication with Vine's six-second videos, but it struggled to keep pace with Snapchat and Instagram." Despite having a dedicated fan base, Vine's content is more often seen on Facebook. It remains unclear if Twitter will incorporate Vine-like features into its main application.
Cardiff Foodbank distributed 14,568 three-day food packages in 2016-17, with over a third going to children. Across Wales, the number of food parcels increased by 11%, and 43% of recipients cited difficulties with benefits as the primary reason. The Department for Work and Pensions stated that the factors leading to food bank use are complex and it is inaccurate to attribute them to a single issue. According to Trussell Trust data, which includes Cardiff, the number of emergency food parcels handed out grew from 85,656 in 2015-16 to 95,190 in 2016-17 in Wales. The most common reasons for food bank referrals were benefit delays, low income, and changes in benefits. Cardiff Foodbank highlighted concerns similar to other Trussell Trust food banks in Universal Credit rollout areas, noting that the initial six-week wait for payments can lead to debt, mental health issues, and rent arrears. The trust appreciated the coordinated approach to Universal Credit rollout in some council areas, such as Torfaen, where the local authority engaged food banks and charities in forming working groups before the new benefit was implemented. Wales led in completing the Universal Credit rollout for single jobseekers and is now rolling it out to couples and families. Tony Graham, director of devolved nations at the Trussell Trust, commented on Scottish Government plans to alleviate the impact of Universal Credit by providing fortnightly payments and directly paying housing costs to landlords. He also expressed a desire to discuss with the Welsh Government how best to support individuals facing challenges with further Universal Credit rollout. Helen Bull, Cardiff Foodbank’s partnership and fundraising manager, stated, "It is deeply troubling that we continue to see an increase in the number of three-day emergency food supplies given to people in crisis in Cardiff. Anyone might find themselves needing food bank assistance; each week, people are referred to us due to unforeseen circumstances like illness, delayed benefit payments, or unexpected bills, making food unaffordable." A DWP spokesperson said, "The causes behind food bank usage are multifaceted, so it is misleading to link them to just one factor. We collaborate closely with local authorities to assist those in need. Budgeting support, benefit advances, and direct rent payments to landlords are available to those who require them."

Parking fees at Glan Clwyd Hospital in Bodelwyddan have been eliminated following the expiration of an external contract. This aligns with the Welsh Assembly Government's commitment to offer free parking at nearly all NHS hospitals in Wales by the end of 2011. As private companies could no longer manage parking due to cost-cutting measures, Glan Clwyd has introduced a new system to simplify parking for patients and visitors. Parking remains in front of the hospital near the main public entrances, with extra spots available on the right side of the access road. Designated areas for renal, cancer, and maternity services, along with no-time-limit accessible parking for the disabled, will persist. New signage is being installed to guide people to these specific areas. The remaining parking spaces will be reserved for staff. A spokesperson from the hospital stated, "We understand that the new system may need some time to settle in, but our top priority is to minimize any inconvenience for patients and visitors during this transition."

CEO Mark Shorrock stated that the costs could now be distributed over 90 years instead of 35 years. The UK government declined to comment on the letter. The Swansea lagoon, the first of potentially six along Britain's coast, is expected to generate 8% of the UK's electricity with a lifespan of 120 years. The company plans to develop lagoons at five additional sites in the UK, including three in Wales. Shorrock told BBC Wales that they have a "compelling" case and believe the review will demonstrate that tidal energy is sensible for the UK. On the day the announcement was made, he received a letter from Energy Secretary Amber Rudd, indicating the UK government’s willingness to consider long-term funding for the project and future lagoons. He noted that substantial information provided to the UK government would be independently assessed, with negotiations for the electricity strike price underway. "We welcome the review because it will confirm the availability of affordable power from our local resources—the tides in the Severn and Liverpool Bay," Shorrock said. The review is scheduled to conclude in the autumn. Construction delays were caused by discussions on the required public funding, which had been postponed by a year. Tidal Lagoon Power had hoped for a decision on the guaranteed price for its energy by the following month, but the review, beginning in spring, is not expected to yield results until later in 2016. Energy Minister Lord Bourne emphasized that the review aims to clarify whether tidal lagoons can be cost-effective and assess their impact on electricity bills both now and in the long term.

If it didn't rain for another nine days, residents in Eglwyswrw, near Cardigan, could surpass the 89-day record set in Scotland in 1923. Farmer John Davies, a Pembrokeshire councillor, hoped this wouldn't happen, noting that the constant rain had become "grueling." He added, "We're still in the running, but hopefully, it will be the last day." The local shopkeeper Brian Llewelyn observed that sales of raincoats and boots were brisk, saying, "An ill wind does indeed bring some good, as you can't survive without a good pair of Wellington boots in the past two months." The prolonged rain had affected other businesses, including builders and farmers who had to bring their livestock inside. While the Met Office doesn't maintain official records for consecutive rainy days, its weather stations indicate daily rain in Eglwyswrw since October 26th, though some readings are as low as 0.2mm. Davies acknowledged that the village's proximity to the Preseli Hills contributed to the high rainfall, which, combined with the mild, wet winter, made Eglwyswrw especially soggy. In Wales, December's rainfall record for a single month had only been broken once before, in November 1929. Despite their resilience, Davies admitted that the constant rain had been taxing, even if humorously.

A recent report indicates that increasing prices by 20% or more leads to reduced consumption and improved nutrition. While the global health organization had previously recommended reducing sugar intake, it did not support tax measures. Countries like Mexico and Hungary have already implemented taxes on sugary products, and South Africa will introduce a sugar tax next year, making it the sole African country to do so. The World Health Organization (WHO) aims to reduce the consumption of "free sugars," defined as all sugars in the diet except those naturally present in fruit and milk, to decrease the incidence of obesity, diabetes, and tooth decay. Dr. Francesco Branca, the WHO's nutrition director, suggested limiting sugar intake to less than 10% of total calories, ideally under 5%. The organization, however, stated that such measures should be applied only when healthier alternatives exist. These findings were derived from a meeting of global experts and an examination of existing evidence. The report further noted that subsidizing fruits and vegetables can boost their consumption due to lower prices. Additionally, similar tax measures effective against sugary drinks appear to be beneficial for other sugary foods, as well as items high in saturated fats, trans fats, and salt.
A lavishly adorned gourd once held a handkerchief allegedly soaked in King Louis XVI's blood after his execution by guillotine during the French Revolution in 1793. However, recent genetic analysis published in Scientific Reports debunks this claim. Scientists argue the item was likely fabricated by an 18th-century con artist seeking to capitalize on revolutionary memorabilia. Professor Carles Lalueza-Fox from the Institute of Evolutionary Biology in Barcelona explained, "During the French Revolution, the guillotine was operational daily, making it easier to obtain blood from less prominent victims." Forensic tests indicated the handkerchief could have belonged to the king, but genetic sequencing revealed discrepancies. Eye color analysis showed a much higher likelihood of the individual having brown eyes rather than blue, as depicted in portraits of Louis XVI. Additionally, the genetic markers did not indicate extreme height, contradicting historical accounts of the king’s tall stature. Genetic ancestry analysis further indicated the individual's origins were primarily French and Italian, differing from Louis XVI’s known German and Polish ancestry. Consequently, the researchers conclude the blood in the gourd almost certainly does not belong to the king, aligning with a previous study that also doubted its authenticity.

William Thomas (WT) Cosgrave, who served as the first President of the Executive Council—effectively Ireland’s first prime minister—from 1922 to 1932, led the young state through its early, turbulent years following the Irish War of Independence against Britain. His grave, along with 12 others dating back to Victorian and earlier times, was damaged at Goldenbridge Cemetery in Inchicore; this incident is reminiscent of a previous 2014 vandalism. Cosgrave ascended to power amid the Irish Civil War, a bitter struggle between supporters of the 1921 Anglo-Irish Treaty and the Free State, and those advocating for an independent republic. Although he fought for independence in the 1916 Easter Rising, he later backed the treaty and staunchly defended the Free State. Under his leadership, 77 republican prisoners were executed during the Civil War, surpassing the 16 rebels executed by the British post-1916 rebellion. Cosgrave's legacy remains contentious, seen by some as ruthless and by others as a decisive leader instrumental in Irish independence. He passed away in 1965 and was buried at Goldenbridge Cemetery following a state funeral. His son, Liam Cosgrave, who also served as prime minister (taoiseach) from 1973 to 1977, visited the site to assess the damage, stating the incident was regrettable for everyone involved. The mayor lacks the authority to implement such measures, so he is pursuing government legislation to ban pedicabs. He also aims to decrease the number of minicabs, a proposal that the Licensed Taxi Drivers Association has supported. The association notes that there were around 13,000 new private hire drivers in London in 2014, partly due to the increased use of cheaper ride-hailing apps like Uber. Mayor Johnson has advocated for banning pedicabs since December 2012, citing concerns about road congestion and passenger safety. In the West End, it's hard to miss the distinctive bells of pedicabs, which many tourists and business travelers use. However, authorities have long sought to eliminate them due to safety concerns and fare issues. While some operators are legitimate, others raise worries about safety and excessive pricing. Attempts to ban pedicabs in 2002 failed in court. Since pedicabs operate under a loophole in the Metropolitan Public Carriage Act of 1869, classified as stage carriages rather than hackneys, the mayor must revise this legislation to achieve a ban. This process would require time and potentially face legal challenges.
Lindsay Newell, from Burton-upon-Trent, neglected the welfare of several animals that became emaciated. She received a two-year conditional discharge, was banned from keeping certain animals, and was ordered to pay £2,000 in costs. The judge noted that the neglect was not intentional and dismissed 25 charges of failing to feed the animals. It was found that Newell did not monitor the weight loss of six animals at Burton Wildlife Rescue and Animal Centre in Etwall, Derbyshire. One animal, a pig named Babe, gained national attention after dying before RSPCA officers' eyes during a raid by police in November 2012. Judge David Taylor stated that Newell's actions resulted from taking on too many animals and being under financial strain. She spent around £300 weekly on food and straw from her earnings as a veterinary nurse and has since lost her job. Her defense lawyer indicated that Newell now aims to run the sanctuary full-time. Newell was barred for five years from keeping sheep, goats, pigs, and equine animals. However, the rescue center continues to operate, housing hundreds of animals including cats, pigeons, chickens, rabbits, and crows. The RSPCA reported that investigating the case and bringing Newell to court cost them £20,000.

The inquiry's spokesperson declined to comment on these specifics, and Fisher himself chose not to provide further details. Fisher had previously served as the first junior counsel, the second most senior barrister on the inquiry, and had worked on two high-profile investigations, including those into Lord Janner and alleged abuse in Westminster. He informed the inquiry of his resignation over two months ago. Fisher's departure occurred shortly after the third chair, Dame Lowell Goddard, left the inquiry and just days after Newsnight revealed that the inquiry had been informed of an alleged sexual assault by lead counsel, Ben Emmerson QC. The alleged victim did not wish to pursue the matter, but the inquiry faced criticism for not investigating broader concerns about Emmerson's behavior. The inquiry allowed him to continue working for two months at £1,700 per day while an external investigation by Matrix Chambers was initiated. Labour MP Lisa Nandy questioned Home Secretary Amber Rudd about the inquiry's handling of the alleged assault, to which Rudd responded that the issue should be addressed with the inquiry's head. The inquiry, which was established by Theresa May, has already seen three chairs and its most senior and second-ranking counsel depart. In response to Fisher's departure, the inquiry's spokesman noted that it has a large legal team and that individuals join and leave based on their professional obligations. Six of these charges pertain to the abuse of two pupils at a Worcestershire school in the 1980s and 1990s. Frost is scheduled to be sentenced on Thursday. Known formerly as Andrew Tracey, Frost was apprehended following an international probe involving the UK's National Crime Agency (NCA) and law enforcement agencies in Thailand, Spain, and the Netherlands. Frost exploited impoverished Asian boys by grooming them with money, sweets, and gifts like computer games and pool time, encouraging them to engage in sexual activities. After skipping bail to evade Thai prosecution, he was extradited from Spain last year upon Dutch police discovering his activities via a computer in the Netherlands. The court learned that Frost coerced boys, aged between 10 and 14, into making hand gestures while being recorded on webcam during sexual acts. Details emerged about Frost's abuse of two pupils at a Worcestershire school during the late 1980s and 1990s, including incidents in a storeroom, at breaks, his home, and a car park in Surrey. One victim passed away after coming forward, while the other stated he lived in constant fear afterward. Frost admitted to multiple rapes, sexual assaults, encouraging children to engage in sexual activity, and producing indecent images. Prosecutors revealed that 15 boys were involved in seized material, with nine identified and interviewed. The boys' mothers expressed sorrow at learning he was the "kind and generous foreigner" they knew. One victim was too ashamed to tell his mother, transforming from a "bubbly, happy boy" to someone withdrawn and sometimes aggressive. Frost had been a member of the defunct Paedophile Information Exchange (PIE). In 1986, he attempted to adopt a son through the Catholic Church but was unsuccessful, succeeding later with a local authority. He was banned from teaching by the Department for Education in 1995. The NCA suspects he may have abused numerous other children he came into contact with over his 25-year career in education and scouting. They urge any additional victims who attended schools in east London, Worcestershire, and Hertfordshire to come forward.
