In February 2013, Estyn, the education watchdog, placed Torfaen council's education department under supervision due to unsatisfactory ratings and serious failings identified in a 2011 inspection. Following this, the council took necessary actions, and measures were lifted on Friday. Torfaen was the sixth Welsh council to enter special measures, following Anglesey, Blaenau Gwent, Pembrokeshire, Monmouthshire, and Merthyr Tydfil. Education Minister Huw Lewis stated, "I am encouraged by the positive progress we're seeing with local authorities exiting special measures, and I hope this will set the stage for continued success." Clive Phillips, an assistant director at Estyn, reported that inspectors reviewed seven recommendations from inspections in 2011 and 2013, focusing on raising secondary school standards and supporting school leavers. Inspectors also noted issues with GCSE performance, with over half of schools in the bottom quartile for core subjects and no school achieving average results for five GCSEs at grades A* to C. The Welsh government established a recovery board to assist the council in addressing these challenges. Phillips's letter highlighted the effective collaboration between the council, elected members, and the recovery board, leading to improved areas. As a result, the authority has been deemed no longer requiring special measures and will no longer face follow-up activities. Torfaen council's education executive member David Yeowell expressed delight at this news, acknowledging the collective efforts of school staff and the education service.

NFU Scotland conducted inspections at Tesco, Asda, Sainsbury's, Morrisons, and Marks and Spencer stores. Nigel Miller, the union's president, emphasized the need for a "confidence boost" for sheep farmers whose products are "valued." He suggested that retailers' commitments to Scots lamb could help improve the industry's situation. The union noted that it has been monitoring supermarket support for home-produced lamb over the past six months, finding Morrisons to consistently stock only Scottish or British lamb. They also highlighted the branding efforts of Marks and Spencer and Sainsbury's. However, they criticized Tesco and Asda for still carrying significant amounts of New Zealand and Australian lamb despite the peak marketing season in Scotland. Miller stated, "Now is the time for supermarkets to give sheep farmers a boost by clearly signaling that their products are desired and appreciated." Morrisons demonstrated success with 100% commitment to Scottish or British produce, selling packs at competitive prices. In contrast, Tesco and Asda maintained substantial stocks of non-Scottish lamb, even during the lamb's freshest period. Miller argued that increasing stocks of Scots lamb would be beneficial for both farmers and the market. "With lambing just a few months away, this could send a positive message and alleviate some of the challenges faced during one of the most difficult seasons," he added. A Tesco spokesperson defended the company’s approach, stating, "We are dedicated to providing our customers with the finest local and regional produce, including 100% Scotch lamb in our Finest range in our Scottish stores from June to December, and Scotch lamb for our core Tesco-branded products year-round." Asda also confirmed its commitment, noting that around 60% of its current lamb range comes from Scottish farms.

The memo described how the attackers reportedly entered from Mali, used vehicles registered in Niger, and concealed weapons and grenades in the spare tire compartment, well-padded to reduce noise. AQIM was previously responsible for similar attacks in West African former French colonies within the past five months, targeting hotels in Mali and Burkina Faso.
The Syrian Observatory for Human Rights stated that at least five individuals died as multiple areas came under attack. The government and its ally, Russia, ceased air strikes in mid-October to permit civilians and rebels to evacuate. While Russia rejected resuming attacks on Aleppo, it claimed to have struck jihadist groups elsewhere. Russia's sole aircraft carrier, the Admiral Kuznetsov, saw its first combat use, with Su-33 fighters operating from the eastern Mediterranean. However, videos from Russian media showed these planes carrying air-to-air missiles instead of bombs. Government forces initiated a significant offensive on rebel-held eastern Aleppo on September 22, following a two-week siege. With support from Iranian-backed Shia militias and Russian air raids, troops advanced into several peripheral regions. In response, rebels attempted to breach the siege in late October but their momentum waned after initial successes. According to the UN, over 700 civilians have perished due to weeks of aerial bombings and artillery fire in eastern Aleppo, while rocket strikes have caused numerous fatalities in government-controlled areas. On Tuesday, reports indicated that the air strike pause announced by Russia had seemingly concluded. Aircraft and helicopters reportedly targeted districts such as Haidaria, Masakin Hanano, Sakhour, Sheikh Faris, Bab al-Nairab, Qadi Askar, and Qaterji. "The tremors from the explosions are felt in our homes. Planes fly overhead, and the bombardment surrounds us," resident Modar Shekho recounted to Reuters. During a briefing with President Vladimir Putin, Russian Defence Minister Sergei Shoigu did not mention Aleppo. Instead, he discussed a "major operation" against so-called Islamic State and Jabhat Fateh al-Sham. Shoigu reported that a frigate, the Admiral Grigorovich, had fired cruise missiles at targets in Idlib and Homs provinces, while Su-33s from the Admiral Kuznetsov and the Bastion mobile missile system participated. Targets included "terrorist" training centers, ammunition depots, and facilities producing weapons with substantial destructive potential. Last Tuesday, Al-Madinah School closed, citing "health and safety concerns" despite having been inspected for just one day. According to sources, Ofsted had rated the school as inadequate across all areas but had yet to file a final report. The school maintained it was closed due to unspecified health and safety issues, not the inspection. Sources noted that Ofsted found incomplete records of child protection checks for all staff. Reports suggested that female teachers, regardless of their religious affiliation, were compelled to wear hijabs, and girls were seated at the back of classrooms while boys sat at the front. As a state-funded free school, Al-Madinah operates independently of local authorities. NUT spokesman Nick Raine questioned the seriousness of the health and safety concerns that led to the closure, asking whether they had been ongoing. Labour politicians pressured Education Secretary Michael Gove to clarify the situation promptly to alleviate public concern. Parents expressed mixed feelings; some were relieved the school was closing to address potential hazards, while others were satisfied with its performance. Al-Madinah, which opened in September 2012, positioned itself as the first free school in England with a Muslim ethos, serving all reception, primary, and secondary students. However, concerns about enforcing strict Islamic practices prompted an early comprehensive inspection. The school's website statement clarified that the closure was due to a short-term health and safety issue that had been resolved and was unlikely to recur.

On Saturday, the Lord Mayor of Bradford unveiled a stone near the village of Serre, which overlooks trenches used by soldiers in the battle. This stone is a replica of one in Bradford's Memorial Gardens. Pals battalions, formed by friends and colleagues enlisting together, were established in northern towns. The stone bears the inscription: "To the memory of the soldiers of the Bradford Pals and other servicemen of West Yorkshire who served in the Great War 1914-18. 'And lo a mighty army came out of the North.'" The stone was quarried in Bradford and transported to France. The first Bradford Pals battalion was raised at the Bradford Mechanic's Institute in 1914, and a century-old union jack flown there was used in the unveiling. Local dignitaries, representatives of veteran groups, and the Commonwealth War Graves Commission attended. Councillor Geoff Reid, the lord mayor of Bradford, stated, "We owe an immense debt of gratitude to these brave men from Bradford who gave their lives during the First World War."

With deep sorrow, we announce that Jaki passed away this morning from sudden pneumonia, as stated on Can's Facebook page. "He fell asleep peacefully, surrounded by his loved ones," the message continued. "We will miss him greatly." Can, known as pioneers of the Krautrock movement blending rock, electronic, and world music, were revered for their innovative approach. Liebezeit, who co-founded the band in 1968 with Irmin Schmidt, Holger Czukay, and Michael Karoli, was credited with suggesting the band's name, standing for "communism, anarchism, nihilism." Despite several lineup changes, Liebezeit remained a constant, appearing on all of Can's albums, including Tago Mago, Ege Bamyasi, and Future Days. While they released up to three albums annually in the 1970s, Can remained a cult band, influencing acts like Roxy Music, Julian Cope, The Flaming Lips, and The Horrors. They achieved an unexpected UK hit with "I Want More," peaking at number 26 in 1976. Liebezeit described Can and fellow Krautrock bands like Neu! and Kraftwerk as seeking their unique path, akin to an artist finding originality. Born in Dresden, Liebezeit was a student of the free jazz scene and worked with Depeche Mode, the Eurythmics, Jah Wobble, and Brian Eno alongside his role in Can. His drumming style, dubbed "motorik," provided the precise, hypnotic pulse that tethered their music. In memory of Jaki, Sonic Youth’s Thurston Moore planned to perform with Can members Schmidt and Mooney at London's Barbican in April as part of The Can Project. Wrote Jah Wobble, "Absolutely gutted to hear my dear friend Jaki Liebezeit has passed. Wonderful person and best European drummer. King of Saxony lebewohl!!!" Brian Eno shared a series of links to Can’s music and interviews with Liebezeit.

Meanwhile, Leave campaigners argued that Mr. Javid had shifted his stance on the EU. Vote Leave contended that the government's figures were dubious and highlighted the detrimental impact of EU regulations on businesses. Linney added that small businesses were more inclined towards the Leave campaign due to perceived regulatory constraints from Brussels. Waymo, the new venture owned by Alphabet, aims to commercialize the autonomous technology Google has been developing for over a decade. At a recent media event, the company highlighted a groundbreaking trip made by a legally blind man named Steve Mahan in a prototype self-driving car in 2015. Google's John Krafcik noted, "Steve Mahan rode alone in one of our prototype vehicles, navigating through Austin’s suburbs, with our sensors and software acting as his driver." Speaking about the establishment of Waymo, Krafcik stated, "We believe this technology can transform transportation, making it safer, more efficient, and more accessible for billions of people." Krafcik will lead Waymo as its CEO. The company plans to explore various applications for the self-driving technology beyond personal vehicles, indicating a heightened focus on immediate commercial opportunities. Unlike some competitors, such as Tesla, which have already deployed certain self-driving features on their vehicles, Google has taken a more cautious approach. The Waymo announcement might signal a shift towards offering more partial autonomy solutions. Krafcik explained, "Our technology could be beneficial in personal vehicles, ride-sharing services, logistics, and addressing 'last mile' challenges for public transit." The company did not confirm reports suggesting it would abandon manufacturing its own cars in favor of partnerships with established automakers, potentially to reduce costs as Alphabet seeks to control expenses in non-core businesses.

In November 2015, while a documentary was being filmed in Belfast, Hugh McGrattan, from Whiterock Drive, lost control of his vehicle and collided with a group of 15 people on a footpath. This resulted in life-altering injuries for several individuals. McGrattan, aged 25, admitted to five charges of causing grievous bodily harm through dangerous driving. The crash occurred on Apollo Road, near Boucher Road, where the speed limit is 30mph. CCTV footage revealed McGrattan was traveling at an "excessive" speed of between 52mph and 69mph before the accident. His car struck the pedestrians, one of whom suffered a spinal cord injury leading to partial paralysis and requiring a wheelchair. McGrattan confessed to speeding and told a police officer he felt the car might tip over. The court heard that some victims were trapped under his car and the fence it had knocked down. A witness reported seeing McGrattan's car unable to navigate a curve safely due to its high speed. Another witness described the impact as the car "bowling" into the group, while yet another compared it to a "snowplow" sweeping people away. The prosecutor noted that some victims' injuries were severe enough to necessitate ongoing medical care. McGrattan's defense argued he was not showing off but had made a "significant misjudgment." Despite admitting his guilt, McGrattan expressed deep regret and struggled with the incident's enormity. He remained at the scene, offered help to the injured, and provided a roadside confession. Forensic evidence indicated McGrattan's speed upon hitting the kerb was around 25mph. The judge reserved sentencing, stating she needed time to consider the case, and McGrattan was released on continuing bail.

A novel system mandates motorists to obtain a check code from the DVLA website to enable companies to verify if a driver has penalty points. This measure followed the digital conversion of the paper license that previously contained such details. Initially, the code was valid for 72 hours, but it is now extended to 21 days. In response to complaints from car rental firms about long queues and difficulties for drivers without internet access abroad, the DVLA extended the validity period. For those without internet access, a phone number is provided, though it operates outside of 24/7 hours. Gerry Keaney, CEO of the British Vehicle Rental and Leasing Association, expressed satisfaction with the DVLA's response, stating that this sensible adjustment will alleviate queues at rental desks and provide more planning time for drivers. Both motoring organizations and car rental companies had voiced concerns about the initial complexity and lack of awareness regarding the new system. The DVLA maintains that the process is straightforward: "Simply visit gov.uk and search for 'view driving licence' to easily access and share your driving record." With the discontinuation of paper licenses, all driving endorsements and convictions will be stored online exclusively. Meanwhile, the roughly eight million existing paper licenses with endorsements remain valid. This system does not affect drivers in Northern Ireland.

Food, alcohol, and tobacco prices grew at a slightly slower pace in December compared to November, and the decline in energy prices was also less pronounced. This was below the 0.3% increase that economists had anticipated. These figures may exert additional pressure on the European Central Bank to take further action to support the struggling European economy. The bank's recent efforts to stimulate the economy were deemed less effective than expected by analysts. The inflation data provided by Eurostat is an initial estimate and does not break down figures by individual member states, but it offers a general indication of price movements across different product categories. Specifically, food, alcohol, and tobacco prices increased by 1.2% year-over-year in December, down from 1.5% in November. Energy prices decreased by 5.9% annually in December, after falling by 7.3% in November. Service prices rose by 1.1% in December, slightly lower than the 1.2% increase seen in November.
A 3,100-meter runway will be constructed 1.6 km north of the current main runway and is anticipated to be operational by 2020. Planning approval for the new runway was granted in 2007 but was suspended during the economic downturn and a decline in passenger numbers. Recent increases in passenger traffic, reaching 25 million in the previous year compared to 23 million in 2008, have spurred renewed interest in the project. There are indications that passenger fees might rise to fund the new runway. Kevin Toland, the airport’s CEO, believes the expansion will boost business and tourism. "The north runway at Dublin Airport will greatly enhance Ireland's connectivity, supporting trade, foreign direct investment, and tourism," he stated. "We are mindful of balancing national and business interests with community concerns, and we will maintain close communication with our neighbors regarding this initiative." Minister for Transport, Tourism, and Sport, Paschal Donohoe, emphasized that the new runway is crucial for Ireland’s economic recovery, noting it could generate thousands of jobs both directly and indirectly.
Its Falcon 9 rocket was ready to lift off from the Cape Canaveral pad when onboard computers initiated an abort sequence due to a low thrust alarm. This was the third attempt in five days for SpaceX to launch a satellite for SES, a Luxembourg-based operator. A fourth attempt might occur later this week, but engineers need time to analyze the data to determine the cause of the abort, and the US Air Force requires time to prepare the Florida launch range. SpaceX CEO Elon Musk tweeted, "Launch aborted on low thrust alarm. Rising oxygen temperatures due to hold for a boat and helium bubble triggered the alarm." Earlier in the evening, the countdown had to pause and restart because a vessel entered the restricted zone under the rocket’s flight path. SpaceX recently resumed regular launches following a failure in the previous year when a Falcon 9 broke apart just over two and a half minutes into a mission. In December, they successfully deployed a multi-satellite payload for Orbcomm, marking the first time an orbital rocket stage was landed and recovered safely. In January, SpaceX launched the Jason ocean altimeter mission for US and European space agencies. Currently, SpaceX uses an upgraded, more powerful version of its Falcon 9 rocket, which employs super-chilled liquid oxygen alongside kerosene as propellants. Recent countdowns have highlighted additional handling challenges associated with the new liquid oxygen.

Walter Newall's contributions from the 19th century can still be admired throughout Dumfries and Galloway. Architectural historian Margaret Elliot will present a lecture on Newall's life and work at Moat Brae in Dumfries, a property Newall designed in 1823. Elliot is documenting all the structures Newall worked on in the area. Born in Doubledyke, New Abbey, on April 3, 1780, to a farmer, Newall initially worked as a cabinet-maker before transitioning to architecture. His buildings reflect his understanding of contemporary styles like Greek Revival and Picturesque Gothic. Newall's portfolio included various projects such as churches, country houses, villas, and farmsteads, many situated on Buccleuch estates. He also played a role in the development of the New Town of Dumfries and transformed a windmill in Maxwelltown into an observatory. A lifelong bachelor, Newall passed away on Christmas Day in 1863 and is interred in St Michael's Churchyard, Dumfries. Source: Dictionary of Scottish Architects. Elliot noted, "I've always lived in Dumfries, but it wasn't until now that I truly appreciated the architectural beauty of these buildings. This project has taught me to recognize Newall's distinctive style. The historical context adds a layer of intrigue, and meeting those passionate about these structures has been enlightening." Her presentation is scheduled for Thursday, August 25, at 6:30 PM at Moat Brae in Dumfries.

The bionic legs, micro-processed prosthetic limbs, will be provided to around 160 injured service personnel from Iraq or Afghanistan, enhancing their stability and mobility. Defense Secretary Philip Hammond highlighted this as a top priority for providing the best care and support to troops. Chancellor George Osborne, allocating funds from the Treasury's special reserve, stated, "Our troops are heroes who have and continue to sacrifice everything for their country, and it is right we do all we can to assist them, particularly when they are injured." Experts note these bionic legs, similar to those used by Paralympian Derek Derenalagi, will significantly enhance the quality of life and rehabilitation process for amputees. These limbs offer better stability, greater mobility, and improved ability to navigate obstacles, climb stairs, and walk backward safely. They will be fitted at the Headley Court military rehabilitation center in Surrey when clinically appropriate. Paralympic rower Captain Nick Beighton, who lost both legs in Afghanistan in 2009, views these advancements positively, saying, "Accessing the latest technology provides us with more freedom and independence, allowing us to do more." Surgeon General Air Marshal Paul Evans emphasized, "This next-generation micro-processed knee offers substantial benefits for certain amputees, improving their quality of life and rehabilitation." Bryn Parry, CEO of Help for Heroes, affirmed, "Our injured service members deserve the best, and this initiative will help transform lives. Each individual must receive the most suitable prosthetic solution."

The rest were placed under police custody, according to the police statement. Border Force was notified of the discovery. Warwickshire Police referred to the vehicle as an "overseas lorry." The operation temporarily closed the A45 Westbound between the M45 and the A4071 but later reopened. I observed about 100 square boxes on the pavement and a team of around ten people—dressed in suits and police uniforms, including forensic investigators—going through each box before reinserting them into the lorry. The lorry appeared to be foreign, possibly from Romania. "This has deeply affected me and I've been reflecting on how I'm perceived," Mr. Rees stated. Sainsbury's apologized and offered them a £10 voucher. Mr. Rees, who is 32, explained that they were holding hands while purchasing groceries on a Monday evening, and he might have placed his arm around his partner's waist. "We weren't celebrating anything; we weren't overly affectionate—just holding my boyfriend's hand as I usually do. I am deeply in love, and that's how I express it," he said. After paying, a security guard told them about the complaint, which made the situation worse. "There's nothing to be embarrassed about. I shouldn't have been removed from the store," Mr. Rees argued. "This experience reinforces that loving someone, regardless of their gender, is simply love, and expressing that love however one wishes is acceptable." Mr. Rees tweeted Sainsbury's, receiving an apology and a voucher, but he requested a phone call to discuss staff training on handling diversity. He empathized with the security guard, noting he was likely unprepared to manage such situations. A Sainsbury's spokesperson said, "We sincerely apologize to Thomas and Josh. The reform retains the tax status but restricts eligibility for those who have resided in the UK for 15 out of the last 20 years. The non-domicile rule permits certain wealthy UK residents to reduce tax on foreign income. Chancellor Osborne stated that modifications to these rules would generate an extra £1.5 billion annually. From April 2017, individuals born to UK-domiciled parents who leave the UK but return later would no longer qualify for non-dom status. Additionally, new rules effective from April 2017 will ensure that homeowners with UK residential property, who might otherwise evade inheritance tax through offshore companies, will now be taxed equally with others. Osborne noted that while non-dom status is not being abolished entirely due to the contributions of those who benefit from it, it is unfair for long-term UK residents to enjoy different tax rules. The chancellor also allocated an extra £750 million to HMRC to combat tax evasion, aiming to collect an additional £7.2 billion by 2020.

This follows a report indicating Mexican drug cartels have acquired weapons from the US, with around 70% of firearms seized in Mexican crimes and submitted for tracing coming from the US in 2009 and 2010. Agents from the Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives (ATF) noted that the weapons they tracked were used in several killings in Mexico. Lawmakers on the House Oversight Committee called for answers from the Obama administration regarding why no major traffickers were arrested during the operation. ATF agent John Dodson recounted, "We monitored as they bought handguns, AK-47 variants, and .50 caliber rifles almost daily." He mentioned that despite wanting to intervene, his superiors forbade making arrests. Senator Chuck Grassley of Iowa highlighted that hundreds of weapons intended for Mexican cartels were bought in Arizona gun stores. Operation Fast and Furious sought to trace weapons as they moved from minor buyers to major traffickers, who often escaped prosecution. In December, two US assault rifles found at the site where Customs and Border Protection agent Brian Terry was killed raised concerns. Agent Terry's cousin, Robert Heyer, urged officials to acknowledge mistakes and take responsibility. Earlier this year, plans to introduce a non-native species for the first time were announced. Public feedback showed that some wildlife experts believed it could have a "devastating impact," while others supported the idea. The government stated there were "no conservation grounds" to move forward. Environment Minister Richard Ronan concluded, after considering all opinions, that proceeding would be "detrimental," and thus decided not to move ahead. Over 100 people responded to the consultation. A representative from the Manx Wildlife Trust argued that introducing the species could harm habitats already under pressure. Experts noted that red squirrels are not endangered in the UK and do not see a scientific basis for creating a refuge for them. Grey squirrels, introduced from North America in the 19th century, have posed a threat to red squirrels mainly through habitat competition and the spread of the parapoxvirus. Despite red squirrels not being classified as endangered, the Red Squirrel Survival Trust predicts they could vanish within a decade without conservation efforts.

"Wussies and pussies complain about that stuff," the seasoned musician said in an interview with Rolling Stone. "Quotation is a vibrant tradition in folk and jazz, but it doesn't apply to me. There are different rules for me." Bob Dylan's 35th studio album, Tempest, was released this week. Earlier this year, at age 71, he received the Medal of Freedom, America's highest civilian honor, from President Obama. Dylan was accused of borrowing from 19th-century poet Henry Timrod for his 2006 album Modern Times, and his 2001 album Love and Theft was said to contain passages similar to lines from Junichi Saga's Confessions of a Yakuza. "Have you even heard of Henry Timrod?" he asked Rolling Stone. "Who's been reading him lately? And who's pushing him to the front? If you think quoting him can help your work, try doing it yourself and see how far you can go." "The same people tried to label me as Judas, the most hated name in history!" he exclaimed, referring to the controversy over his 1965 album Bringing It All Back Home. "If you think you've been called a bad name, figure out how to rise above it. And for what? For playing an electric guitar?" Born Robert Allen Zimmerman in 1941, Dylan began his musical journey in 1959, performing in coffeehouses in Minnesota. He adopted the stage name Dylan from the poet Dylan Thomas. Much of his celebrated work dates from the 1960s, including tracks like "Blowin' In The Wind" and "The Times They Are A-Changin'." His shift to electric guitar in the mid-1960s was met with controversy among folk purists. "It's about songwriting," he stated. "It involves melody and rhythm, and then anything goes. You claim everything as your own."
Crop yields must increase by 40-60%, according to predictions for 2050 from an online tool created by the government to explore ways to reduce carbon emissions. The Global Calculator, using data reviewed by international experts, examines scenarios for meeting the 2C target, deemed necessary to avoid dangerous climate change. Led by the Department of Energy and Climate Change (DECC), the model suggests that maintaining living standards is possible but requires significant changes in agriculture, transport, food, and fuel systems. By 2050, hundreds of millions of electric vehicles would be needed, with a substantial decrease in CO2 emissions per unit of electricity—by at least 90%. Consumers would also need to consider diets rich in vegetables or meat from intensively farmed animals. Energy and Climate Change Secretary Ed Davey stated, "For the first time, the Global Calculator demonstrates that everyone in the world can thrive while limiting global temperature increases to 2C, thereby preventing the most severe effects of climate change." However, the calculator also emphasizes that immediate action is required to transform energy use and production and land management practices to achieve this sustainable growth. Dr. Mike Cherrett of Climate-KIC, which co-led the project, noted, "The calculator clearly shows that we can meet our 2C target while preserving quality of life—but we must set ambitious goals across all areas and leverage innovation to combat climate change." Building on DECC's UK calculator from 2010, the Global Calculator is now available for other governments to use in advance of critical climate talks in Paris later this year.
The real hourly pay for individuals aged 22 to 39 dropped by 11% since its peak, contrasting with a 5% decline for those in their 50s and a 2% decrease for those in their 60s, according to the data. Additionally, average weekly earnings fell by 0.4% in the first quarter of 2017. The only area where typical earnings had rebounded to pre-2008 levels was part-time workers' pay. The Resolution Foundation, established in 2005 as a non-partisan organization aiming to enhance living standards for 15 million low and middle-income Britons, noted that the pay squeeze reappeared at the beginning of 2017 and seems likely to persist throughout the year. Economic analyst Stephen Clarke from the foundation expressed concern, stating, "What's most concerning is that people’s paychecks have yet to recover from the previous squeeze, even as the current one persists." However, the foundation highlighted that the National Living Wage has had a positive impact since its introduction two years ago. "The National Living Wage has brought about a 12% increase in earnings over the past two years, providing a silver lining in this otherwise challenging landscape of stagnant pay," it noted. In response to the report, a spokesperson from HM Treasury commented, "More people are currently employed than ever before, and we are assisting them by reducing tax burdens for millions of workers. The National Living Wage has granted annual pay raises of £1,400 to those earning the least, while also supporting families by freezing fuel duties and increasing the amount of free childcare available." Source: Office for National Statistics.

The high court in Chennai (Madras) dismissed a series of petitions seeking to prosecute Perumal Murugan. The judges encouraged him to return to writing, stating, "Let the author be resurrected to what he is best at: Write." Murugan ceased writing last year following protests over his novel, Madhorubagan. His supporters view the court's decision as a victory for free speech. There was no immediate response from Murugan. The court also ruled that a settlement between the novelist and a "peace council" in Tamil Nadu, where Murugan agreed to apologize and withdraw unsold copies of his book, was not legally binding. Madhorubagan (One Part Woman) is set around a century ago in Tiruchengode, near the author's hometown, and revolves around a childless woman who participates in a temple festival sex ritual to conceive. Local groups protested the book, alleging that its fictional extramarital sex ritual insulted the town, its temple, and its women. The novel's copies were burnt, shops were closed, and a petition called for the author's arrest. In reaction, Murugan wrote a dramatic Facebook post declaring, "Perumal Murugan, the writer is dead. He has no faith in rebirth and will live as P Murugan, an ordinary teacher. Leave him alone." He also directed his publishers to halt printing and selling his works, promising to compensate them for unsold copies, and urged his readers to burn his books, vowing to cease attending literary festivals. Many critics consider Murugan one of the finest Tamil language writers.

Shifts in behaviors like starting smoking earlier and increasing cigarette consumption have contributed to significantly heightened lung cancer risks, according to research published in the New England Journal of Medicine. The study analyzed data from over two million American women, revealing that death rates due to lung cancer in women now match those in men. Women who began smoking in the 1950s and 1960s faced nearly three times the risk of dying from lung cancer compared to non-smokers. By 2000-2010, this risk had risen to 25 times higher for women who smoked compared to non-smokers. A similar pattern was observed in men, reaching comparable levels in the 1980s. Dr. Michael Thun, the lead researcher, noted, "The substantial rise in lung cancer risk among female smokers persisted for decades despite the widespread belief that smoking 'Light' and 'Mild' cigarettes was safer. These cigarettes may have even worsened the risk of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease in male smokers by encouraging deeper inhalation to maintain nicotine intake." Last year's research indicated that lifelong female smokers lived about a decade less than non-smokers. However, those who quit by age 30 largely escaped the risks of premature death from smoking-related diseases, while those who stopped by 40 still died about a year earlier. Prof. Sir Richard Peto commented, "Women who smoke like men end up dying like men."

MB Aerospace has acquired Centrax Turbine Components in Newton Abbot, with the Gas Turbines division remaining under the Centrax Group. This Devon-based components manufacturer, which employs 430 people, has been supplying engine parts for nearly seven decades. Known for its expertise in rotating components for compressor and turbine sections of aero-engines, it will continue to produce components requiring automated machining, grinding, and robotic polishing. Centrax provides parts to Rolls-Royce, Snecma, and Solar engine manufacturers, who then supply Airbus, Boeing, and Embraer. MB Aerospace, headquartered in Motherwell, North Lanarkshire, operates facilities in Connecticut, Michigan, Poland, and the UK. Since acquiring a majority stake from Blackstone private equity in 2013, this is the first significant acquisition. Robert Barr, Centrax's CEO, stated, "We have found in MB Aerospace a partner that aligns with our values and commitment to the region, and that respects the high-tech manufacturing expertise we've built over time."

Glasgow has also claimed 106 millionaires since the lottery's inception in 1994, placing it second in the UK behind Birmingham. However, Paisley (PA) was deemed the luckiest area in Scotland based on the highest number of winners per capita for prizes of £50,000 or more. Andy Carter, a senior winners' advisor for The National Lottery, noted: "Recent years have brought significant lottery success to Scotland, particularly in Paisley, with the most substantial victories celebrated there. Glasgow retains its status as the UK's lottery millionaire capital, producing nearly two millionaires monthly over the past couple of years. Currently, there are over 4,250 National Lottery millionaires in the country, with more than six new millionaires emerging each week, suggesting an upcoming local winner." In terms of lottery millionaires in Scotland from 1994 onwards, Glasgow ranks second with 62, followed by Motherwell with 43 and Aberdeen with 41. Over the past two years, the PA postcode area has garnered 30 top-tier prizes of at least £50,000, resulting in four new Lottery millionaires. Since the lottery's start, 182 major prizes of £50,000 or more have been awarded in this area.

Analysis of pollen found on pottery buried alongside a young woman over 4,100 years ago reveals plants used for medicinal purposes. Discovered at Achavanich in Caithness three decades ago, the woman, named "Ava," is the focus of a long-term research project led by archaeologist Maya Hoole. Hoole noted the pollen's significance, stating, "Its presence raises intriguing questions." Last year, Hew Morrison produced a facial reconstruction of Ava. Recent research now provides additional insights. Pollen extracted from a decorated beaker with Ava included birch, pine (likely Scots pine), hazel, and alder. Heather, grasses, meadowsweet, and St John's wort were also identified. Notably, meadowsweet has appeared in other Bronze Age burials in Scotland, while the presence of both meadowsweet and St John's wort might indicate intentional inclusion of medicinal herbs in the burial. Hoole commented, "The inclusion of multiple plants with known medicinal properties prompts questions about whether this was intentional and possibly connected to the cause of Ava's death." Additional findings include Ava's remains and artifacts being cared for by the Caithness Horizons museum in Thurso. Ava was uniquely buried in a rock-filled pit, and her skull exhibits an unusual shape that some attribute to deliberate binding. She is believed to have belonged to the broader European Beaker people. Further studies aim to provide more clarity about Ava and her burial context.

The company reported evidence of "limited, targeted attacks" and advised users to update their software immediately. Flash is commonly used on the web to deliver multimedia content. A vulnerability allowing hackers to gain control over computers affects Windows, Mac, and Linux systems. To check if your Flash installation is current, visit the Adobe website; the latest version is 18.0.0.194. Those using the latest versions of Google's Chrome or Microsoft's Internet Explorer have Flash updated automatically. Adobe's Flash software frequently requires security patches and is viewed by some security experts as a weak point on many sites. Along with Java, Flash is often targeted by hackers exploiting unknown security flaws. Mark James, a security specialist from ESET, noted, "Given Flash's widespread use, it’s a prime target for vulnerabilities. If you want to impact many users, you target a widely-used application like Flash." Security blogger Brian Krebs, in an experiment, completely disabled Flash on his machine and suggested considering whether to uninstall it altogether.

Chief Inspector of Prisons Nick Hardwick stated that a sense of direction had been lost since a positive assessment in 2010. The National Offender Management Service confirmed that the facility would receive assistance to enhance its operations. While the previous inspection in 2010 highlighted progress and praised the prison's leadership and clear goals, these gains were not sustained. The current inspection revealed that staff at the Category B prison, accommodating up to 600 inmates, showed insufficient regard for prisoners. Inspectors observed staff behaving inappropriately, using disrespectful language, and failing to treat inmates with respect compared to other prisons. Cells were found to be unsanitary, with damaged furnishings, offensive graffiti, and cockroach infestations. The report also noted shortages of clean clothing and bedding. Hardwick emphasized that prisoners' experiences were subpar and outlined key areas for improvement, including creating a better environment, changing staff attitudes, and providing inmates with constructive activities. Despite these challenges, some positives were noted, such as effective security measures and successful management of resettlement issues. In the past five years, data from the Press Association shows that 272 organ donors in the UK had a history of cancer, leading to 675 transplants. Eye donations, in particular, have been crucial for these donors. NHS Blood and Transplant reports that 1,033 individuals who had certain types of cancer were able to donate their eyes, though not other organs. There is a common belief that cancer patients cannot be organ donors, but under specific conditions, it is feasible. The Advisory Committee on the Safety of Blood, Tissues and Organs emphasizes balancing the risks of cancer transmission against the risks of remaining without a transplant. They state that organs from deceased donors with certain cancers can be safely transplanted. The risk of cancer transmission through organ donation in the UK is currently estimated at 0.06%. Professor John Forsythe, associate medical director for organ donation and transplantation at NHS Blood and Transplant, encourages everyone, regardless of their health status, to register as a donor and inform their families of their wishes. The organization works to minimize recipient risks through careful evaluation of potential donors. Approximately 70 cornea donations are required each week to meet the need for sight-saving transplants. However, only about 90% of people on the NHS Organ Donor Register agree to eye donations. Alison Cooney, an aspiring midwife who passed away at 28 due to bowel and liver cancer in 2010, allowed her mother, Ann Cooney, to donate Alison's corneas, which restored sight to two individuals. Ann noted that while it was initially challenging to accept, focusing on the positive outcome helped.
Shawn Tyson was convicted of murdering friends James Cooper, 25, and James Kouzaris, 24, in Sarasota, Florida, on April 16, 2011. Although an appeal court upheld his conviction, his sentence was overturned due to his age at the time of the crime, as he was 16. The case was sent back to a lower court for a new sentencing hearing. In the judgment, Associate Senior Judge James R. Case affirmed Tyson's guilty verdicts without elaboration but noted that Tyson's status as a juvenile homicide offender necessitated a reassessment of his sentence. The judge referenced a Supreme Court ruling that deemed mandatory life sentences without parole for juveniles unconstitutional and cruel. Cooper was from Hampton Lucy, Warwickshire, and Kouzaris was from Northampton. Both victims had stumbled into the Newtown area while intoxicated when they were shot. Tyson, who faced adult charges, was found guilty of first-degree murder in March 2012.

Around 800 officers have been trained to administer on-the-spot punishments, aimed at deterring first-time offenders from entering the criminal justice system. Since early 2011, restorative justice has been employed in the county, with 190 cases handled through this approach. This method is only applied if the victim consents. Instead of traditional arrest and punishment, restorative justice operates on three key principles, as explained by Insp Dominic Human: acknowledging wrongdoing, making amends, and reintegrating into society. This process enables quick resolution, recognizing the unique chance to avoid a criminal record. In many instances, the primary goal is for the offender to apologize, which restorative justice facilitates. Trained officers seek input from victims regarding suitable punishments, ensuring they are deemed fair. Insisting that they are not judges, Insp Human states, "Our role is merely to provide a platform for immediate acknowledgment of wrongdoing and an opportunity to make amends." For example, juvenile offenders responsible for minor criminal damage agreed to clean all windows of a property. A 16-year-old shoplifter who stole goods valued at £3 had to clean 100 shopping baskets, while a 12-year-old who caused £75 worth of damage to a car sent a written apology, with the parents compensating for the loss. Insp Human notes that restorative justice saves considerable police time compared to court proceedings. "We achieve the same results efficiently, right at the scene," he says. The initiative's motivation is to prioritize the victim's needs. So far, over 1,000 operational policing hours have been saved, allowing for greater focus on community issues. Cambridgeshire Constabulary plans to train all officers in these restorative justice methods moving forward.

Between 2010 and 2012, over 400 complete skeletons were unearthed during construction at St John's College. These remains, dating from the 13th to 15th centuries, were burials from the Hospital of St John the Evangelist. A four-year £1.2 million study aims to gain insights into the health of urban poor during the epidemic. Additionally, about 1,000 body parts were discovered, with a St John's College spokesperson describing this as one of the largest medieval hospital burial grounds ever found in Britain. Most of the people buried there were poor townspeople rather than clergy, and the group included men, women, and some children. The epidemic claimed millions of lives across Europe. Researchers from various disciplines, including archaeology, history, and genetics, will collaborate to "directly test the impact of the Black Death on a medieval town population before, during, and after the epidemic." The Hospital of St John the Evangelist was an Augustinian charitable institution that cared for the public from around 1200 to 1500. This research is supported by the Wellcome Trust and St John's College.

A pilot study involving 261 teenage girls from three secondary schools found that a six-lesson course on body image significantly improved their body image and self-esteem compared to the standard curriculum, according to psychologists at King's College London. About one in three teenagers express dissatisfaction with their bodies. Poor body image is linked to issues such as depression, eating disorders, excessive use of cosmetic surgery, obesity, and unhealthy weight-loss behaviors. Dr. Helen Sharpe and her colleagues at the Institute of Psychiatry created a positive body image course tailored for teenage girls. The six-lesson program addressed societal beauty standards, negative peer interactions like "fat talking," and strategies to enhance mood and self-esteem. Delivered by teachers in three state-funded girls' schools, the course demonstrated effectiveness in improving body esteem. Dr. Sharpe stated, "Our research, published in The British Journal of Psychiatry, indicates potential in addressing issues stemming from low body esteem, such as eating disorders." She expressed optimism that continued research could refine the program to reduce disordered eating behaviors, including binge eating and unhealthy weight loss practices. The researchers noted that teacher-led interventions offer broad reach and low cost, and some schools were using non-evidence-based programs that might be harmful. Last year, MPs recommended mandatory body image and self-esteem lessons for all schoolchildren. An inquiry by the All-Party Parliamentary Group on Body Image revealed that over half of the public has a negative body image, with concerns about appearance evident even among young girls, and cosmetic surgery rates having risen by almost 20% since 2008.
Skiplagged identifies cheaper fares by searching for flights with a stopover in a major city en route to the traveler's final destination. The two travel firms claim the site engages in "unfair competition" and aims to recover lost revenue. Skiplagged's developer asserts that he is merely exposing inefficiencies in airline ticketing. Legal action has been initiated in Illinois. The site operates by finding longer flights with a stop in a large city before reaching the traveler's intended destination. For instance, a trip from New York to Lake Tahoe with a stop in San Francisco could allow travelers to save significantly on the fare. In some cases, savings of up to 40% on ticket prices are possible. This strategy only works with one-way flights, and passengers cannot check baggage since it would continue to the final destination. The site's 22-year-old creator, Aktarer Zaman, told CNNMoney that he did not profit from Skiplagged and declined to comment further. Zaman has started a crowdfunding campaign to fund his legal defense against United and Orbitz, raising $10,538 out of the required $15,000. In their legal document, United and Orbitz stated that Skiplagged is "intentionally and maliciously" disrupting their business and breaching contracts with their partners. They also mentioned that "hidden city" ticketing is prohibited due to logistical and public safety concerns, and therefore, using Skiplagged violates these rules. The firms are seeking at least $75,000 in revenue they believe they have lost because of Skiplagged's operations.
The Langstone memorial marks the conclusion of a four-year project initiated to uncover the stories of six World War Two servicemen. Langstone council's research revealed details of an additional 10 men from the earlier conflict. On a recent Saturday, Bishop of Monmouth, Right Reverend Richard Pain, dedicated the memorial, which was erected in May. The project started when two Langstone council members aimed to learn more about six men named on a World War Two memorial plaque at the village hall. Edward Jeffery, the community council chairman, discovered not only the stories of the original six men but also those of 10 more who perished in World War One from Langstone, Llandevaud, Llanmartin, and nearby areas. Constructed of granite to honor the area's quarrying heritage, the memorial includes a time capsule filled by children from Langstone Primary School with items reflecting their lives. Additionally, it features a QR code that provides visitors with information about the men whose names are inscribed on it.
This discovery will aid researchers in gaining deeper insights into the "strong force," which binds atomic nuclei together. Theoretical predictions existed for the new particle, but its identification was previously unconfirmed. Details of the Xi-cc++ were shared at a high-energy physics conference in Venice. Led by Dr. Patrick Spradlin from Glasgow University, the study was conducted at the LHCb experiment. Spradlin stated that the finding would "illuminate a long-standing mystery and open up a thrilling new area of inquiry." Professor Paul Soler, his colleague from Glasgow University, referred to the breakthrough as "a new horizon in our comprehension of the strong force." Nearly all visible matter consists of neutrons and protons, which form atomic nuclei. These particles are composed of three smaller units called quarks, which can be light or heavy. Six distinct types of quarks exist, combining in various configurations to create different particles. Up until now, only those containing at most one heavy quark had been detected. This marks the first confirmation of a particle with two heavy quarks. Professor Guy Wilkinson from Oxford University noted an interesting distinction: "Unlike other particles of this kind, where the three quarks engage in a complex dance, a particle with two heavy quarks is anticipated to behave more like a planetary system, with the heavy quarks acting as two stars orbiting each other, while the lighter quark orbits around this duo." The research team plans to measure the properties of the Xi-cc++ to understand how this novel quark configuration functions and how the strong force maintains the system's stability. They also anticipate discovering additional double heavy quark particles. Another unique feature of the particle is its double positive charge, equivalent to twice that of a proton, and its mass, which is four times greater. The scientists have submitted their findings to Physical Review Letters.

Resources will be distributed over the next three years to approximately 70,000 non-profits and 900 university research projects. Cloud computing involves storing data on the internet instead of a local device, making it more accessible. For non-profits, this means gaining access to substantial computing power typically unavailable to them. In a blog post detailing the initiative, Microsoft's chief legal officer, Brad Smith, stated, "Cloud services can unlock data's secrets, leading to new insights and breakthroughs across various fields, including addressing economic and social challenges and enhancing human services." The initiative aims to process "big data," providing non-profits tackling social issues with the necessary computational power. Unlike tangible products or financial aid, Microsoft's contribution includes server and service access that would otherwise require significant fees. Additionally, funds will support "last-mile" internet connectivity improvements, aiming to bring broadband access to under-connected regions. Other tech giants, like Facebook, have similar goals, investing in connectivity technologies such as drones to expand internet access. However, these efforts have faced criticism; Facebook's Free Basics scheme was met with opposition in India due to concerns about unfair competition. Microsoft's approach may face similar accusations, but CEO Satya Nadella has emphasized using the company's resources to benefit developing countries, including his home country, India. His recent activities, such as attending the World Economic Forum in Davos, underscore Microsoft's ambition to harness cloud computing for broader societal benefits.

Appointed as chief executive in November 2012, Dr. McNeil stepped down on Monday alongside Mr. James. The hospital faced criticism for implementing the £200 million e-hospital system last year, which stores electronic patient records. However, Dr. McNeil argued that the system was the right move, saying, "It was absolutely the right thing to do. We now have an integrated electronic health record that is starting to demonstrate its value as familiarity improves and customization continues. The entire e-hospital program may be costly, but it's significantly less than what we would have needed to maintain a failing system for the next decade."

A fire engulfed a nursing home within a hospital in Tainan City, affecting elderly and largely immobile patients. According to a hospital official, the victims succumbed to smoke inhalation. Injured residents were being treated at multiple hospitals. Police have apprehended a patient from the nursing home, suspecting him of starting the fire. The nursing home accommodated over 100 individuals, many of whom relied on respirators and were bedridden. "The darkness was complete, and the thick smoke was intolerable; it was truly terrifying," an escapee recounted to Taiwan’s Central News Agency. A 94-year-old woman used her wheelchair to escape, hiding on the second floor until rescue workers located her. Thirty fire engines were dispatched to the scene, which was extinguished at 04:16 local time (20:16 GMT), about 45 minutes after the fire was reported. Tainan Mayor Lai Ching-te noted that the remote location of the facility and the immobility of many patients exacerbated the situation. While firefighters arrived ten minutes after the fire began, nearby residents assisted in moving some patients to other hospitals due to the large number present. The fire is considered one of the deadliest at a Taiwanese hospital in recent years, as reported by local media. Premier Sean Chen expressed condolences to the victims' families in a statement.
A tender has been issued to train Whitehall staff on trade policy, aiming to equip them with a deep understanding of the UK's trade priorities and those of its key partners. This training is essential as the UK seeks to secure numerous free trade agreements post-Brexit, amid concerns over a shortage of expertise. According to the former UK ambassador to the EU, Sir Ivan Rogers, multilateral negotiating experience is notably lacking in the UK. Historically, the EU has managed the UK’s trade negotiations, with many specialists based in Brussels. Now, the Foreign Office and the Department for International Trade are looking for a provider to offer short-term civil service training in international trade policy and negotiations. The goal is to ensure officials can support and implement the UK's international trade policies both domestically and abroad. KPMG is overseeing this process, managing the civil service's core curriculum on behalf of the Cabinet Office. Until the UK leaves the EU, it can only explore potential future trade deals, but ministers anticipate a strong interest from countries such as Australia, New Zealand, and Canada. US President Donald Trump has expressed interest in an early trade agreement with the UK post-Brexit, a topic he plans to discuss with Prime Minister Theresa May. The Treasury has allocated additional funds to enhance the UK's trade promotion efforts. Recently, a high-level official indicated that while the civil service is preparing current staff for these new roles, it might also recruit external talent. As of two months ago, the UK had 45 trade policy experts, a number now increased by 100 since the June referendum.
In February 1957, 11-year-old Moira Anderson went missing after leaving her grandmother's house in Coatbridge to run some errands, presumably boarding a Baxter's bus driven by convicted pedophile Alexander Gartshore. Gartshore, who died in 2006, is suspected of her murder. Police are currently investigating an area along the Monkland Canal in hopes of locating her remains. Moira was last seen on February 23, 1957, during a heavy snowstorm, and later that year, Gartshore was imprisoned for raping a 17-year-old babysitter. In 1999, convicted child abuser James Gallogley identified Gartshore as Moira's killer. Gartshore's daughter, Sandra Brown, strongly believed he was responsible and lobbied for charges. In 2014, prosecutors stated that Gartshore would have been prosecuted for Moira's murder had he lived. A witness claimed to have seen a tall man carrying a heavy sack near the canal the day after Moira went missing, though this sighting was not pursued. In 2018, a grave in Old Monkland Cemetery was exhumed as part of the investigation, but no evidence linking Moira to the site was found.
Charlie Naysmith discovered a 600g piece of ambergris in seaweed near Hengistbury Head in Bournemouth. At first, he mistook it for an ordinary stone, but upon researching with his father, Alex, they realized it might be vomit from a sperm whale—a rare and valuable substance. Ambergris forms inside a sperm whale and is occasionally expelled, eventually floating in the ocean for years before washing up on shores. Mr. Naysmith, from Christchurch, estimates the ambergris to be about 20 years old and believes it could be worth up to £40,000. He noted, "It's very rare; you don't often hear of it and it's usually found in small quantities." In the past, it was used in perfumes and some medicines. While he’s unsure of its current value, he hopes for the best. Charlie has already planned how to spend any proceeds from selling the ambergris, stating, "I want to create a nature reserve for animals in this country." Known as "floating gold" by whale hunters and researchers, ambergris is a hardened excretion believed to aid in digestion, often containing the hard beaks of giant squids. Initially soft and smelly, it transforms into a smooth, waxy lump over time. Despite being used in some perfumes today, many manufacturers opt for synthetic alternatives due to its historical connection with whaling and the endangered status of sperm whales.
Edinburgh Airport experienced the most significant growth, followed closely by Aberdeen and Glasgow. In February, Edinburgh Airport processed 607,723 passengers, marking a 7.8% increase from the previous year. International passenger numbers surged 11.6% to 269,235, boosted by new easyJet routes and an additional daily Lufthansa flight to Frankfurt. Domestic travel grew by 4.9%, with 338,488 passengers. Gordon Dewar, CEO of Edinburgh Airport, commented, "February was a robust month for us, with a healthy rise in passenger numbers. We saw excellent performance from Lufthansa and Turkish Airlines, especially since their daily flights on larger aircraft contributed to a 97.7% jump in passengers compared to last year. We look forward to launching 10 new routes from March to June, including services to Chicago, Philadelphia, and Doha in May." Aberdeen Airport reported a 6.5% rise in passenger numbers, reaching 255,416 in February, with growth in both fixed-wing and helicopter operations. Carol Benzie, managing director, described the results positively and anticipated an increase in holiday traffic with new charter destinations like Corfu. Scheduled operations also expanded with a new service to Riga and another to Oslo. Glasgow Airport saw a 6% increase in passengers, totaling 448,465 in February. International traffic climbed 12.9% to 174,236 due to demand for long-haul services and additional capacity from airlines such as Jet2.com, Icelandair, and KLM. Domestic travel rose by 2% to 274,229, with strong demand for London and regional services, and CityJet’s new Cardiff route proving popular. Amanda McMillan, managing director of Glasgow Airport, expressed satisfaction with the positive start to 2014 and highlighted upcoming developments, including the launch of Citywing’s new Isle of Man service and the arrival of the A380 in April. McMillan added that Glasgow Airport is preparing for major events like the Commonwealth Games and the Ryder Cup, ensuring readiness to support these occasions.

The ATR 42-500 plane, intended to combat the Afghan drug trade as part of a DEA and Pentagon-led program, cost ten times less initially but required additional millions for modifications and maintenance. According to a report by the US Justice Department’s Inspector General’s Office, the aircraft remains unused in storage in Delaware to this day. The program concluded in 2015, and despite the plane being purchased over seven years ago, it still hasn’t flown in Afghanistan due to high operational costs and a lack of necessary infrastructure. The report indicates that the DEA failed to account for the extensive support staff and spare parts needed to operate the aircraft. When ready, the plane was supposed to be used in the Caribbean and Latin America, but this wasn't the intended purpose of the funding. This revelation follows similar criticisms of wasted funds on ill-conceived reconstruction projects in Afghanistan, including one that imported expensive Italian goats and another that overspent significantly on a fuel station.

In February, the film was shot on land leased for grouse shooting in the Derbyshire Peak District. A conservation group, Raptor Persecution, released the footage on Tuesday, calling for action. Despite Derbyshire Police investigating, they did not pursue further action. However, the National Trust deemed the incident "very serious" and initiated its own investigation. Birdwatchers captured the footage using a digiscope about a kilometer away, releasing it after the police had looked into it. A representative from Raptor Persecution noted that while no laws were broken, it seemed a decoy bird resembling a male hen harrier might have been used to lure another bird, potentially distracting it long enough for a gamekeeper to shoot. The RSPB expressed concern over the possibility of an "illegal attempt" to target hen harriers or other birds of prey. Sarah Fowler, Chief Executive of the Peak District National Park, described the video as "alarming and suspicious" and supported the investigation. The Moorland Association condemned all acts of wildlife crime but stated no crime occurred in this case. Alan Charles, Derbyshire's Police and Crime Commissioner, highlighted the ongoing need to protect wildlife, noting the distance between observers and the shooter prevented obtaining sufficient evidence for a prosecution. According to the RSPB, hen harriers are the most "intensively persecuted" birds of prey in the UK, with their predation of grouse causing conflicts on shooting moors. In 2015, five male hen harriers vanished from sites across England, leading to nest collapses, though their fate remains unclear.
In August 2014 and April 2015, Bronze Age artifacts were unearthed in Wenvoe, Vale of Glamorgan. An early medieval item, likely part of a gilt bronze or silver brooch, was discovered in Llanarmon-yn-Iâl, Denbighshire, also in August 2014. A silver ingot from the early medieval period was found in Trelawnyd and Gwaenysgor, Flintshire. Gwyn Rees discovered the Bronze Age axe and spearheads, which are believed to have been buried around 3,500 years ago as part of a ritual. National Museum Wales is interested in acquiring these items. The brooch, found by Karina Kucharski, features a gold baseplate adorned with a spherical blue glass bead set in a gold collar. Dr. Mark Redknap of National Museum Wales noted that such early medieval gold work is rarely found in Wales, and this discovery significantly enhances our understanding of early medieval jewelry, its distribution, and the goldworking techniques employed at the time. The silver ingot, located by Francis Hodge, is sought by Flintshire museums.

The contract Swiss prosecutors deemed "unfavorable" to FIFA likely refers to a 2005 television rights agreement with Jack Warner, former Concacaf president. Warnings were issued at the Royal College of Nursing (RCN) and British Medical Association (BMA) conferences, highlighting severe pressures on the National Health Service (NHS). At these events, delegates detailed how escalating stressors jeopardize patient safety. However, English ministers countered these claims, asserting that investments have positioned the NHS as the world's safest healthcare system. Both conferences convened amid dire warnings of mounting pressures, with the RCN presenting a dossier illustrating nighttime transfers of frail elderly patients and treatment in non-clinical areas. Doctors at the BMA conference in Belfast emphasized a critical shortage of beds, describing the NHS as "bursting at the seams." They cited OECD data indicating that countries like Belgium, France, Austria, Germany, and Romania have more than twice the number of beds per capita compared to the UK. Over the past decade, the number of hospital beds in England has decreased by more than one-fifth to around 103,000, leading BMA leader Dr. Mark Porter to warn of increased risks of infection and reduced chances of patients being placed on appropriate wards. Surgeon Michael Hardingham from Cheltenham went further, stating that patients are harmed due to lack of available beds. Doctors overwhelmingly endorsed a motion asserting that bed reductions have exceeded acceptable limits. Nurses at the RCN conference in Glasgow highlighted extreme measures taken by some hospitals, including transferring patients to inappropriate areas. RCN general secretary Janet Davies described the NHS as trapped in an "endless winter" causing chaos. Emergency care nurse Janet Youd from West Yorkshire affirmed that she cannot guarantee safe care in every emergency department. Nevertheless, a Department of Health spokesperson insisted that the NHS is performing well under additional pressures, maintaining that patient safety remains a top priority and that efforts are underway to make the NHS the safest and most transparent healthcare system globally.
Lionsgate is reviving the 1987 film starring Jennifer Grey and Patrick Swayze, with Kenny Ortega, known for directing the Michael Jackson documentary This Is It and the High School Musical series, taking the helm. "Directing Dirty Dancing feels like coming home to me," Ortega shared with Variety. "I'm excited about building a talented team and an impressive cast to introduce this timeless story to a new audience." Patrick Swayze established the standard for male dancers in films, following in the footsteps of Gene Kelly and Fred Astaire. Set in the 1960s, the original romantic drama follows a young woman who falls in love with her dance instructor during a family vacation. The film's soundtrack achieved significant success, with hits like "Time of My Life" and "Hungry Eyes." The movie later transformed into a successful stage production, with its West End run concluding in July, and it will embark on a UK tour in September. Jennifer Grey, who portrayed Baby in the original, recently won US television dance competition Dancing with the Stars. Sadly, Patrick Swayze passed away from pancreatic cancer in September 2009 at the age of 57.
Airport data showed a year-over-year decline of 8.1% in traffic to just over 300,000 passengers in May, with helicopter traffic decreasing by 13.1% and fixed-wing flights by 7.2%. Managing director Carol Benzie noted a reduction in passenger numbers across all sectors—helicopters, international, and domestic—and attributed the decline to cost-efficiency measures in the oil and gas industry. In contrast, Glasgow Airport reported its busiest May in seven years, with 785,063 passengers, marking a 13.3% increase from the previous year. International traffic grew by 17%, and domestic traffic rose by 9.1%. Glasgow Airport's managing director, Amanda McMillan, highlighted the addition of 10 new services, including several firsts for the airport, city, and country, and noted recognition as one of Europe's fastest-growing airports by ACI Europe. Edinburgh Airport saw over one million passengers in May, surpassing its previous record for the same month, largely due to recent route launches.
During the Speaker's annual St Patrick's event at the parliament buildings, Mr McLaughlin advocated for changes, noting that the building's art and symbolism do not represent all cultural identities. While he stated he would be the first to oppose any removal of existing symbols, he suggested adding more representations. McLaughlin acknowledged the complexity of the task and emphasized addressing the issue in a consensual manner to avoid controversy. He stressed that achieving a respectful balance without provocation is essential and should not threaten anyone. On St Patrick's Day, the building will be illuminated green, reflecting a mature agreement to mark four annual events with their respective colors, including orange to commemorate the Battle of the Boyne and recognizing Remembrance Day. McLaughlin expressed satisfaction with the green illumination and indicated that he hopes the building will also have an orange glow on July 12th. His approach, influenced by visits to commemorate World War I and his role in the Commonwealth Parliamentary Association, aims to foster mutual respect. In his St Patrick's Day eve speech, McLaughlin highlighted that while the building's current art and symbolism may not encompass all cultural identities, removing them would be inappropriate given the building’s historical significance. He underscored the importance of inclusivity, stating that enhancing the representation of diverse cultures can be done through consensus and democratic means, without causing division.
