On Saturday, PCSO Mike Ober, in his mid-20s and engaged, was on foot patrol in Bradford-on-Avon when he noticed an open door at a social club. Deciding to investigate, he entered and was met with a group of women who were ecstatically excited, thinking he was the male stripper they had ordered. He recounted, "They thought I was early; the party was just about to begin." He continued, "I had to quickly leave." The social club was usually unused on weekends, prompting Ober to check inside. "There were no cars in the parking lot, and the door was slightly ajar," he explained. As he stepped in, he heard loud cheers and someone shout, "Oh, are you the stripogram?" When informed he was too early and instructed to return later, Ober left, passing the actual male stripper on his way out. "Uniforms aside, it’s a matter of personal taste, but I suppose being mistaken for a stripper is a compliment," he added.
Murray, 30, withdrew from an exhibition match in London on Friday, just three days before he starts his Wimbledon defense. According to BBC Sport's David Ornstein, Murray grunted occasionally during practice but was comfortable in his last session. His coach, Ivan Lendl, stated that practice had gone well. As a two-time Wimbledon champion, Murray will play against Kazakhstan’s 134th-ranked player, Alexander Bublik, on Centre Court at 13:00 BST on Monday. This will be Murray’s second competitive match on grass this summer, following his first-round exit in the Aegon Championships. Prior to Friday, Murray hadn’t practiced since Tuesday. Ornstein noted that it was unusual for Murray to practice three times in one day so close to a tournament. At around 9:30, Lendl reported that Murray was doing well, and the duo then hit balls with Belgian Steve Darcis. Despite looking reasonably comfortable, Murray limped and grimaced frequently. He continued with some volleying and serving drills, reaching near full intensity. Between serves, he joked and smiled with Lendl, who also conversed with Wimbledon CEO Richard Lewis. Murray left without answering media questions and then practiced with 17-year-old Scottish junior Aidan McHugh, showing a relaxed demeanor.
The European Commission stated that the plan seeks to increase investment in assets such as drones and helicopters, alongside innovative technologies. This initiative aims to counter a decade of defense spending reductions by EU member states, which amounted to nearly 12% in real terms. The plan emerges against the backdrop of US President-elect Donald Trump's potential reduction of US security commitments to NATO. Trump had suggested during his campaign that NATO allies not contributing their fair share should not be defended by the United States. There are worries among some EU and NATO members regarding Russian aggression and Trump's proximity to Moscow. Additionally, Brexit has influenced the development of this plan, given the UK's historical opposition to Franco-German efforts to enhance the EU's security role. European Commission President Jean-Claude Juncker emphasized the need for increased investment and cooperation among member states. He stated, "To ensure our collective security, we must invest in the joint development of crucial technologies and equipment—from ground, air, sea, and space capabilities to cybersecurity." Juncker further noted, "If Europe does not secure its own defense, no one else will." The plan includes expanding the research portion of the defense fund from €25 million to €90 million by 2020, to encompass fields like robotics and secure software. Simultaneously, a capability window will seek to mobilize €5 billion annually from member states for purchasing shared equipment like drones and helicopters. The Commission will function as a treasury managing these procurement processes. Despite declining defense spending across EU countries, the Commission pointed out that military collaboration among these nations has not significantly improved, resulting in an estimated annual loss of between €25 billion and €100 billion for the bloc. According to the BBC’s Europe editor Katya Adler, while the EU maintains rapid response forces, there is limited evidence of the unity required among the 28 nations to establish a European defense union. The European Council, comprising EU heads of state and government, is anticipated to review the defense plan later this month.
The statement provided no additional details about the reasons for Mr. Mackenzie's departure, though an AA spokesperson mentioned it was related to "personal conduct." Mr. Mackenzie's son stated, "My father tendered his resignation today due to acute ill health." He added, "This is an extremely distressing mental health issue. My father has been experiencing these symptoms for some time, and a clinical psychologist advised him to take at least six months off. He has been admitted to the hospital." Following the news, AA's shares fell by 14%, and the company reported that trading had been impacted by "erratic workload patterns." Mr. Mackenzie, who served as executive chairman since June 2014, will be replaced by non-executive chairman John Leach, with Simon Breakwell, former founder of Expedia, stepping in as acting CEO. The AA noted that the half-year results, due at the end of September, would reflect the "erratic workload" issues, particularly in June and July, when there weren't enough patrol vehicles to meet demand. Additionally, the company has adjusted its accounting methods for certain products, such as paying commissions upfront for breakdown cover sales, with benefits recognized later. Despite these challenges, the AA remains optimistic about its long-term prospects.
The 28-year-old five-time Grand Slam champion disclosed on Monday that she tested positive for meldonium in January. "I woke up yesterday with my inbox filled with love and compassion," Sharapova, the Russian player, wrote. "Your heartfelt words brought a smile to my face." Facing a potential ban of up to four years, Sharapova stated, "While I wish I didn't have to go through this, I do, and I will." She mentioned spending her time reading supportive messages from friends, who had compiled a collage of well-wishes and hashtags. "My dog couldn’t quite grasp why this was more important than our scheduled walk," she added. Developed to treat diabetes and heart conditions, meldonium can enhance stamina and endurance by increasing oxygen flow to muscles. It was included on the World Anti-Doping Agency’s (WADA) list of banned substances as of January 1, 2016. Sharapova, who has taken meldonium for 10 years for health reasons, has seen some sponsorship agreements end or be delayed. The International Tennis Federation announced she will be provisionally suspended from March 12.
On Wednesday morning, a pigeon was noticed by staff at a car wash in Lower Hollin Bank Street, Blackburn. It is unclear if the pigeon had "fallen into something" or was deliberately painted, according to an RSPCA spokesperson. "In my 15 years, I've never seen anything like it," Insp Nina Small remarked. "Only his eyes were visible." "Given he was in a car wash, maybe he was trying to clean himself?" she suggested. After a wash, his feathers remained stained pink, with paint continuing to flake off. If someone intentionally painted the pigeon, there could be concerns for other local birds and animals. Insp Small expressed hope that the paint could be removed and the pigeon released back into the wild. However, if its feathers are permanently damaged, its flying ability might be compromised. There's also a risk the pigeon could ingest potentially toxic paint while cleaning itself.
The program aims to assist over 600 women in entering politics over the next five years. Jo Cox, the MP for Batley and Spen, was tragically killed in Birstall, West Yorkshire, in June. Labour's general secretary, Iain McNicol, stated that this initiative is a fitting tribute to the mother-of-two who championed international feminism. The effort is a collaboration between the Labour Party and the Labour Women's Network. It will provide training and mentoring for women aspiring to become MPs, councillors, elected mayors, and other party roles. Labour MP Rachel Reeves shared, "Jo was deeply committed to women in leadership and politics, and she was the first chair of the Labour women's network." Reeves added, "Alison McGovern, my colleague from the Wirral, says Jo supported her as if she had one arm around her shoulder and the other pushing her forward." Cox encouraged women to seek parliamentary nominations. This initiative follows a call from several female Labour MPs to party leader Jeremy Corbyn to address personal abuse during the leadership campaign. Owen Smith, Cox's leadership rival, also criticized Corbyn for not sufficiently addressing "intolerance and misogyny." Currently, the Conservative Party, SNP, DUP, Plaid Cymru, UKIP, and Green Party are all led by women. In response, Labour selected Tracy Brabin, a former soap actress, as its candidate for the by-election triggered by Cox's death, which will be held on October 20.
Approximately 65% of five-year-old children from disadvantaged backgrounds scored below average on vocabulary tests, compared to 38% of more affluent children, according to the 'Ready to Read' Northern Ireland report. This report, produced by the Read On Get On coalition, surveyed 1,955 children from 63 council wards across Northern Ireland. It advocates for increased investment in early childhood education and additional parental support. Save the Children, along with partners such as Libraries NI, Parenting NI, and Early Years, released this research. Fergus Cooper, head of the Northern Ireland charity, emphasized the importance of parental involvement in a child's education. "Every parent wishes their child to succeed, yet many lack the knowledge of what they can do to support that learning," he stated. "Early language development is essential for good reading, so we must invest and engage much earlier." Black Mountain Primary School in west Belfast, with nearly 150 students, has been collaborating with the charity for several years. Parents can participate in reading sessions with their children during school hours. Stacey Brittan, whose son AJ attends the school, noted that these sessions have significantly boosted her son's reading skills. "I used to avoid reading, but now my son eagerly looks forward to buying new books," she shared. "Quality time with him has made reading more enjoyable." Elaine Crossman, another parent, appreciates the opportunity to read with her son Riley during school hours. "I often miss this chance at home due to work commitments," she explained. "Reading with Riley in school helps us bond and see his progress in reading." Principal Jill Black highlighted that parental engagement has greatly improved reading skills. "Our encouragement for parents to join us in school began three years ago, and it has notably enhanced reading enthusiasm, especially among younger students," she said. This initiative has also strengthened the relationship between teachers and parents, leading to better reading test results. The 'Ready to Read' report includes new analysis from University College London based on a Northern Ireland sample from the Millennium Cohort Study. The UK-wide Read On Get On organization aims to ensure every child reads proficiently by age 11 by 2025.
The High Court dismissed Southern's parent company, Govia Thameslink Railway (GTR), argument that the strikes would violate customers' rights. This decision allowed the first of three strike days to commence on Tuesday. Southern predicted that the strikes would result in no services on most routes. The RMT union, which has been conducting strikes since the summer due to disputes about conductor roles, plans more actions before and after Christmas. Aslef, which hailed the court ruling as a victory, stated that GTR's actions were a waste of "shareholders', passengers', and taxpayers' money." General secretary Mick Whelan emphasized, "Now, the company should negotiate properly, sensibly, and in good faith to benefit passengers, staff, and the company alike, just like ScotRail did with us earlier this year." GTR responded, expressing disappointment with the decision and noting that the judge acknowledged the potential for significant disruption but upheld the legality of the strikes. A government representative suggested the need to explore alternative measures to address what he termed "unjustifiable" strike actions. Strikes are scheduled as follows: - RMT: December 6 to December 8 - Aslef: December 13 to December 14 - Aslef: December 16 to December 16 - RMT: December 19 to December 20 - RMT: December 31 to January 2 - Aslef: January 9 to January 14
Katrice Lee disappeared 31 years ago from a shop at a German military base where her Teesside father was stationed. The issue has been discussed in the House of Commons, with MPs commenting on the handling of the investigation. Iain Wright, a Labour MP from Hartlepool, stated that the family has waited "far too long" and called for action. Conservative MP Caroline Dinenage from Gosport criticized David Cameron for declining to meet the family. Katrice’s mother, Sharon Lee, her sister Natasha from Gosport, and her father Richard Lee from Hartlepool believe she is still alive and was abducted. Defence Minister Mark Francois said he would attempt to meet with them before Christmas. He explained, "While we don't have a time machine, the Royal Military Police are using all available modern investigative techniques and consulting with leading international experts to find out what happened to Katrice." Katrice vanished on her second birthday when her family was shopping for party supplies; her mother briefly left her with her aunt at the checkout while she went to buy crisps, but Katrice was missing when she returned.

Issues include pension reform, tax changes, labor market deregulation, and rehiring 4,000 former civil servants. The Army's announcement of the charges took place at Fort Bragg, North Carolina, on Wednesday. Colonel Daniel King stated that the Army Forces Command had reviewed their investigation into Sergeant Bergdahl’s disappearance before filing the charges. Sergeant Bergdahl is accused of desertion with intent to shirk important or hazardous duty, and misbehaving before the enemy by endangering a command, unit, or place. Conviction on the desertion charge could result in up to five years in prison, while the misbehaviour charge carries a maximum sentence of life imprisonment. The case will proceed to a preliminary hearing to determine if a court-martial is warranted. Col. King compared this hearing to a civilian grand jury process. The timing of the hearing has not been disclosed. Fellow soldiers claim Sergeant Bergdahl intentionally strayed from his unit in Afghanistan in June 2009. After being captured by the Taliban and held for five years, he was rescued by U.S. special forces in May. In exchange for his release, five high-ranking Taliban members were swapped from Guantanamo Bay to Qatar. This swap angered both Republicans and some Democrats, who believed it endangered American lives. Top Republican lawmakers contended the deal violated U.S. law and involved negotiations with terrorists, claims refuted by the Obama administration. Additionally, they criticized the lack of notification to Congress. Bergdahl’s family has faced death threats, and a welcome home event in his Idaho hometown was canceled due to safety concerns.

Across Scotland, schools and homes faced disruptions as power was cut off and train, flight, and ferry services were canceled. The Scottish Environment Protection Agency (Sepa) issued over 40 flood warnings. High winds hit Shetland hardest, causing the cancellation of 11 flights. Loganair advised passengers to check their websites for rebooking options. In Edinburgh, a man in his 30s suffered head injuries from flying debris and was in stable condition at Edinburgh Royal Infirmary. Trains between Edinburgh and London were delayed, with some services north of Newcastle completely canceled due to overhead power line damage. ScotRail announced numerous cancellations and reduced services for the day. Network Rail reported that the line between Perth and Inverness was closed due to flooding at Dalguise viaduct. Bridges across Scotland were closed, including the Forth Road, Tay, Kessock, Skye, and Erskine, though some have since reopened. Various incidents involved overturned lorries and trampolines blown onto roads. A landslide closed the A82, requiring a 154-mile detour. Strong winds and lightning left thousands without power, with Scottish Hydro restoring service to 11,000 customers but leaving 2,800 still without electricity. All schools on the Western Isles, Orkney, and Shetland were closed, along with several in the Highlands.

Following a tip-off about illegal oily waste discharge by Princess Cruise Lines off the coast of England in 2013, an investigation revealed the company had attempted to conceal its actions. The cruise line has agreed to plead guilty to seven charges related to incidents at ports across nine U.S. states and two territories. These charges stem from the Caribbean Princess cruise ship, which the U.S. department claimed had been illegally discharging pollutants since 2005, shortly after its launch. The ship used devices like a "magic pipe" to circumvent onboard pollution control systems designed to separate oil and monitor oil levels. The U.S. began investigating after an engineer reported the illegal dumping off England in August 2013 and resigned upon the ship's arrival in Southampton. The ports involved in the charges included Florida, Maine, Massachusetts, New Jersey, New York, Rhode Island, South Carolina, Texas, and Virginia, along with the U.S. Virgin Islands and Puerto Rico. Jeremy Smart, head of enforcement at the Maritime and Coastguard Agency, stated that this case sends a strong message to the maritime industry that such pollution practices will not be tolerated worldwide. As a subsidiary of Carnival Corporation, the world's largest cruise line operator, Princess Cruise Lines faces a record-breaking fine for deliberate vessel pollution.
Its current deepest-diving vehicle, recently tested in the Bahamas, can reach a depth of 1 km (0.6 miles), but it serves as a prototype for a future vessel capable of descending the full 11 km (7 miles) to the bottom of the Mariana Trench. At the heart of this design is a 15 cm (6 inch) thick glass sphere being crafted by Rayotek Scientific in San Diego, ensuring the safety of the three-person crew while offering panoramic views throughout the descent. With a commercial goal in mind, Triton aims to sell tickets for this unique experience. Bruce Jones, the company's CEO, notes, "While thousands climb Everest and Richard Branson secures deposits for space flights, we believe we can attract adventurous individuals willing to pay up to $250,000 to join an exclusive group of ocean explorers." The company anticipates having the craft ready within the next two years.
Around 200,000 people attended Lumiere, which featured 29 illuminated artworks across Durham over four nights in November. A report for the county council found that the event generated nearly £4 million more than the 2013 edition. Next week, council members will consider approving a commissioning fee of £600,000 to revive the festival in 2017. The remaining costs, estimated at £1.8 million, are expected to be covered by an Arts Council England grant, sponsorships, and donations. Simon Henig, the council leader, noted that the 2015 event was chosen due to its "positive economic impact, showing a strong return on our investment for the county, businesses, and residents." He also mentioned that part of the growth was attributed to a more thorough examination of visitor spending patterns. Adam Deathe, from Durham Business District, stated that hotels, restaurants, and shops all benefited as visitors came from across the North East and beyond. He emphasized that Lumiere attracts national attention, leading to booked hotels months in advance, with visitors often staying a couple of days and exploring the wider area as well. The festival, first held in 2009, has occurred biennially since then, with the inaugural Lumiere London taking place in January 2016.
Rev. Tom Gordon, the Dean of Leighlin in County Carlow, was the first Anglican clergyman in Ireland to enter into a civil partnership. He told Radio Ulster's Sunday Sequence that the Republic of Ireland's vote to legalize same-sex marriage demonstrates the need for church leaders to shift their focus. "We need to move towards discussing other issues," he said. "We should be addressing societal inequalities and broader global concerns." In the Church of Ireland, and within the Anglican communion, he noted, there has been an almost exclusive focus on the issue of human sexuality in the public eye. Last month, the Republic of Ireland overwhelmingly voted to legalize same-sex marriage in a historic referendum, with over 62% supporting constitutional amendments to allow gay and lesbian couples to marry. Ireland thus became the first country in the world to legalize same-sex marriage through a popular vote.

The first comprehensive review of its kind was carried out by Queen's University and the NSPCC. Dr. John Devaney, who led the research, stated that it demonstrated the effectiveness of Northern Ireland’s child protection system. "We have a robust child protection system," he noted. The report, titled "Translating Learning into Action," will be officially released later today. Researchers analyzed 24 cases involving children who died or suffered severe injuries. According to Dr. Devaney, in the vast majority of these cases, no deaths could have been anticipated. However, he highlighted the importance of early intervention support for families and extending professional involvement to sustain improvements. The report indicated an increase in the number of children on protection registers compared to five years ago, attributing this to enhanced protective measures. Key findings included: - The rate of non-accidental child deaths in Northern Ireland has continued to decline due to a strong child protection system. - Most case reviewers praised the dedication and professionalism of staff working with families. - The children reviewed were among many in similar situations known to professionals, and the studies suggested it was improbable to identify those at higher risk. - Services need to engage with families earlier to prevent problems from becoming entrenched. - Longer-term involvement with families is necessary to consolidate improvements in parenting. The authors recommended providing therapeutic services for children affected by poor parenting and encouraged more frequent meetings among professionals to coordinate assessments and interventions. They also emphasized that senior managers must ensure professionals' workloads are manageable and proportional to their experience.
Irish Water announced that it expects to restore water supply to 50,000 homes and businesses by Thursday. The rupture occurred on Friday morning. Storage levels are now critically low, Irish Water stated. Repair work has proven more challenging than initially thought, attributed to the age and material of the pipe, along with the complexity of the repair. The water main serving the Staleen Water Treatment Plant is buried over four meters underground. The 50-year-old pipe has warped and operates under high pressure, making the repair intricate. A customized solution is being developed. The plant provides drinking water to Drogheda and surrounding areas, including parts of south Louth and east Meath. To manage remaining resources, Irish Water has reintroduced water rationing in some Drogheda areas starting Monday at 08:00 local time. The council and Irish Water have used large tankers to bring treated water from neighboring regions, mobilizing containers from across the Republic of Ireland and Northern Ireland. This water is being directly injected into the water network to support the planned rationing schedule, details of which will be posted online and updated regularly. Temporary water stations have been established in east Meath and Louth, and consumers have been advised to bring clean containers and boil water from these stations before use. NI Water has committed to ongoing support alongside Irish Water until the situation is resolved.

Archaeologists uncovered human remains and the outlines of at least nine coffins during excavations for new classrooms at St Mary's Primary School in Leith. This discovery, according to experts, highlights Leith's "rich archaeological history." City of Edinburgh Council's culture convener, Richard Lewis, stated, "The information from the site is proving to be more significant than initially anticipated." He noted that the school community and children have shown great interest in the ongoing excavation and its findings. John Lawson, the council's archaeologist, explained that the area likely contained a cemetery from the 15th to the 17th centuries, with at least nine graves, including those of both adults and children. While such discoveries were possible, they still came as a surprise. Further excavations will provide more detailed information for accurate dating and understanding the history of Leith. The excavation site is currently fenced off, though the school remains open. Construction of the new classrooms will proceed but will be delayed until the end of June due to the additional archaeological work.

Chris Martin, the son of Michael Martin, was a healthy 19-year-old university student at University College Cork who played rugby when he suddenly passed away on December 26, 2015. Two days before Christmas, he awoke struggling to breathe and died despite efforts to seek medical assistance. His father implored individuals with mild asthma to manage their condition carefully. "I never imagined asthma would take my son's life," Mr. Martin stated. "I never expected asthma to claim any life." Yet, asthma-related deaths occur weekly in the Republic of Ireland, as reported by the Asthma Society of Ireland. This charity aims to address the misconception that only severe asthma sufferers are at risk. Mr. Martin participated in the society’s latest awareness campaign, which began airing on Irish television and social media on Saturday. Diagnosed with asthma around age four, Chris had never been hospitalized for it. An active teenager, he engaged in various sports like surfing and mountaineering. "Chris always carried his reliever inhaler and used it during rugby games," Mr. Martin recalled. "But he often forgot to take his preventive medication. He might remember it on Mondays or Tuesdays but would neglect it on Wednesdays or Thursdays. It wasn't until he experienced some chest discomfort that he would resume taking it." Mr. Martin noted that his son was unaware his asthma was not fully controlled and did not realize he was at risk of a severe attack. On the night of December 26, 2015, Chris went to bed normally but woke up at 6:00 AM unable to breathe. Despite attempts to reach a doctor and use a nebulizer, he collapsed and died in front of his family. Averil Power, CEO of the Asthma Society of Ireland, highlighted that such cases are not uncommon. "Families of young people who died of asthma in the past 18 months range in age from seven to 21," she said. "These individuals were all fit and active, unaware of their risk of a fatal asthma attack." She explained, "There's a common belief that only severe asthma sufferers are at risk, which is incorrect. A 2014 UK study found that the majority of those who died had mild or moderate asthma, and underuse of preventive inhalers was a significant factor." Ms. Power advised patients to adhere to their prescribed preventive medication even when feeling well and to inform their GP of any issues. "Proper care and

North Wales Police serves an area covering the entirety of north Wales, encompassing Anglesey, Gwynedd, Conwy, Denbighshire, Flintshire, and Wrexham. This region, with a population of 675,000, is predominantly rural and includes the Snowdonia National Park, home to Snowdon, Wales’s highest peak. The A55 road connects this area to major cities such as Manchester, Liverpool, and Birmingham, and the ferry port at Holyhead on Anglesey. Since 2009, the current chief constable, Mark Polin, has led the force. North Wales Police employs 1,454 officers, 888 staff, and 232 community support officers. For the 2016/17 fiscal year, the force had a budget of £141 million. It aims to save £4.54 million between 2017/18 and 2019/20. The police precept for Band D properties is £240.12, making it the highest in Wales, compared to £220.06 in Gwent, £207.85 in South Wales, and £200.07 in Dyfed-Powys. In the year up to September 2015, there were 36,800 recorded crimes, a 1% increase from the previous year, marking the smallest rise in recorded crime in Wales. Violence against the person rose by 17%, though all but one force experienced increases in this category. Sexual offenses increased by 11%, while criminal damage and arson rose by 3%. Robbery decreased by 12%, and burglary dropped by 10%. A recent HMIC report praised the force for its crime prevention and anti-social behavior measures and tackling serious and organized crime. However, it noted that other areas needed improvement, citing issues with how crime allocation policies were understood and implemented by frontline officers.

The health board is now tasked with consulting on alternative care options for these children. Health Minister Edwin Poots has stated that all possibilities, including an all-Ireland service, should be considered. If this is not feasible, children will need to travel to England. With rising standards for complex pediatric cardiac services across the UK, Northern Ireland must demonstrate its ability to provide the necessary surgical coverage. Although the review did not identify any immediate safety issues, it concluded that the current service within the Belfast Health Trust was unsustainable. The Children's Heartbeat Trust has termed the situation "disappointing and worrying." Sarah Quinlan, speaking for around 1,000 families reliant on the service, argued that if the review confirmed safety, the service should continue. At a lecture to the David Hume Institute, Tricia Marwick advocated for a comprehensive review of how parliamentary committees function. She also proposed that committee conveners should be elected by their peers. Currently, the Scottish Parliament consists of 17 committees, with 132 positions shared among 80 Members of the Scottish Parliament (MSPs). Marwick suggests reducing the number of committees to around 10 or 11. "Changing our culture is no longer sufficient; we must also consider structural changes," she stated. "Does our current committee setup effectively serve us? Why haven't committees proposed any legislative measures since 2002, except for procedural ones? Why have we conducted minimal post-legislative scrutiny?" She argued that larger committees might enhance policy coherence by integrating distinct areas under one body, focusing on what is crucial for the parliament. Marwick believes the Scottish Parliament should clearly define its policy priorities and tailor its committees accordingly. Larger committees could form smaller subgroups, allowing for more flexible and efficient discussions. "Not every committee member needs to be involved in every discussion simultaneously," she noted. "We must adopt more innovative approaches in our operations." Additionally, Marwick supports the election of committee conveners through a secret ballot of all MSPs, promoting a more accountable system. While parties would retain proportional representation, conveners would wield greater influence, independent of the government's legislative agenda. Marwick highlighted past criticisms of Holyrood's committees for insufficient oversight of the Scottish government, particularly from opposition MSPs who accused the SNP administration of stifling dissent.

At 52, The Venerable Rachel Treweek, currently the Archdeacon of Hackney in the Diocese of London, will become the new Bishop of Gloucester. Although two women bishops have already been appointed, Treweek will be the first to lead a diocese and will hold the position just below that of an archbishop. She will succeed The Right Reverend Michael Perham, who retired last year. Clergy and representatives from churches throughout Gloucestershire are anticipated to gather to welcome the new bishop. It is believed she will also become the first female bishop to sit in the House of Lords. In an open letter, security and foreign policy experts state that the agreement "fails to meet the administration's criteria for a 'good' deal." They advocate for fewer concessions regarding international nuclear inspections and research and development activities. The letter’s release coincides with the approaching deadline for a final accord. US Secretary of State John Kerry and his Iranian counterpart, Mohammad Javad Zarif, are scheduled to travel to Vienna on Friday to participate in the negotiations, which might extend past June 30th. The P5+1 nations aim to restrict Iran's sensitive nuclear activities to prevent rapid development of a nuclear weapon. Iran, seeking the lifting of sanctions in exchange, asserts its nuclear program is peaceful. The open letter, published by the Washington Institute for Near East Policy on Thursday, is signed by a bipartisan group of US diplomats, legislators, policymakers, and experts who have convened regularly over the past three years to discuss Iran. The letter states, "Most of us would have preferred a more robust agreement." It notes that while the deal does not prevent Iran from acquiring a nuclear weapons capability or necessitate dismantling its nuclear infrastructure, it will reduce this infrastructure for the next 10 to 15 years. Additionally, it will implement a stringent transparency, inspection, and consequence framework aimed at discouraging Iran from developing a nuclear weapon. The signatories urge President Obama to demand that Iran disclose information about suspected nuclear weapons research and grant International Atomic Energy Agency inspectors access to military sites. They also recommend imposing strict limits on centrifuge R&D to avoid rapid upgrades and expansions in Iran's enrichment capabilities post-deal. Enriched uranium can serve both civil nuclear power and weapon production. An official from the Obama administration stated that they received the letter and found it largely aligned with their negotiating stance within the room.
William Matchett, a former RUC Special Branch officer, has experience advising on policing in Iraq and Afghanistan. He authored a book detailing Special Branch's role during the Northern Ireland Troubles. Matchett noted that the current terrorism threats in the UK differ significantly from those during the Troubles. "The new threat manifests itself through vehicles, cars, and knives used to kill quickly and dynamically, often within seconds," he explained. "This contrasts sharply with the more immediate and transient attacks I witnessed in Northern Ireland, where terrorists sought to escape swiftly." He further stated, "In recent attacks in England, the assailants lingered, aiming to maximize casualties. Thus, rapid intervention is crucial, which means all police, from neighborhood beats to plainclothes officers, should carry sidearms." In the London terror attack on Saturday, seven people died and 48 were injured. The attackers, two of whom were shot by armed response unit police officers after crashing into pedestrians on London Bridge and stabbing people in Borough Market, were confronted by an off-duty policeman who was later critically injured. A British Transport Police officer, armed with a baton, also faced the attackers and sustained serious injuries but is now stable. All police in Northern Ireland are armed both on and off duty. However, Metropolitan Police Commissioner Cressida Dick expressed reservations about routinely arming all UK police officers. "While I understand the need to address this threat, I personally oppose routine arming of all officers," she said. "These terrorists seek to alter our society, and I believe the public and my officers are uncomfortable with the idea of every officer carrying a weapon. Additionally, such widespread arming may not offer greater protection to the public or officers, as evidenced by tragic incidents in other countries." Dick advocated for specialized teams capable of handling complex and terrifying threats.
IAEA Director-General Yukiya Amano stated that Iran provided environmental samples from the Parchin military site, which he visited on Sunday. However, he warned that significant work remains to complete the investigation by the December deadline. Western nations suspect that Parchin was used for nuclear weapons research, but Iran denies these claims, insisting its nuclear program is peaceful. Completion of the IAEA's investigation is vital for implementing the July agreement between six world powers and Iran, which limits Iran's nuclear activities in exchange for sanctions relief. The Parchin facility, located south of Tehran, focuses on ammunition, rocket, and explosive development. Concerns about its potential involvement in Iran’s nuclear program began in 2004 after reports of a large explosives containment vessel constructed for hydrodynamic experiments. Satellite images showed no unusual activities in 2005, and samples did not indicate nuclear materials, yet doubts lingered. Until Sunday, Iran had consistently refused IAEA requests to revisit Parchin, and extensive landscaping, demolition, and new construction were noted. During his visit, Amano toured Parchin with Tero Varjoranta, head of the IAEA’s Department of Safeguards. They inspected a building previously seen only in satellite photos and found no equipment inside, though they observed signs of recent renovations. Environmental samples were collected from “places of interest” last week, with Iran’s cooperation in the process. Amano confirmed the integrity and authenticity of the samples, stating that the collection was conducted under IAEA responsibility and monitoring.

The survey indicates general election support at 29% (up 6 percentage points from July), with Labour at 35% (+1), Plaid Cymru at 13% (-3), UKIP at 14% (-2), and the Lib Dems at 7% (-1). Conducted by YouGov for ITV Cymru Wales and Cardiff University, this is the first Welsh poll since Theresa May became Prime Minister. Professor Roger Scully, an analyst from Cardiff University’s Wales Governance Centre, attributes the results to May’s “honeymoon period” as a new party leader. According to Scully, projecting these findings across the current Westminster constituencies would result in Labour losing Ynys Mon to Plaid Cymru, leaving Labour with 24 out of 40 Welsh seats, the Conservatives with 11, Plaid Cymru with four, and the Liberal Democrats with one. With the Boundary Commission proposing a new parliamentary map that would reduce the number of Welsh MPs to 29, Scully’s projections suggest Labour would narrowly win the majority of Welsh seats in a general election under these conditions, with 15 MPs, compared to 10 for the Conservatives, 3 for Plaid Cymru, and 1 for the Liberal Democrats. The assembly election voting patterns also align with these trends. Scully’s projections show Labour losing three seats, ending up with 26 AMs, while the Conservatives gain three seats, becoming the largest opposition party in Cardiff Bay with 14 members. Other parties retain their previous numbers: Plaid Cymru with 12, UKIP with seven, and the Liberal Democrats with one. Scully notes that the significant improvement in Conservative fortunes as indicated by the poll confirms that the broader national trends observed in recent UK-wide polls also apply in Wales. He adds that it is common for new prime ministers or party leaders to experience a honeymoon period, citing the short-lived “Corbyn Bounce” seen in the previous year’s polls. The survey, conducted from 18-21 September 2016, involved a sample of 1,001 adults polled online by YouGov.

She expressed skepticism, noting that such a sum was unusual for a penny machine. McKee sought to assist her children financially, but her hopes were dashed. The casino argued that the on-screen rules stated "malfunction voids all pays and plays." During the dispute, the Iowa Racing and Gaming Commission (IRGC) secured the machine, conducted an investigation, and sent the hardware and software to an independent lab. Investigators found the software allowed a bonus up to $10,000 but could not explain the multi-million bonus message. Machine manufacturer Aristocrat Technologies suggested component degradation over time might cause such errors and advised casinos to disable the bonus feature as a precaution. The IRGC concluded the bonus display was invalid due to machine malfunction. McKee sued in 2012 for breach of contract and consumer fraud, but the court ruled against her in 2013. The case lingered after her attorneys argued the IRGC lacked jurisdiction and disputed the machine's malfunction and the existence of a contract. Similar cases in the U.S., such as a 2009 Mississippi case where a player claimed a $1 million bonus, also resulted in court dismissals. McKee's final appeal was rejected by the Iowa Supreme Court on April 24.
Gordon McLeod's schemes were thwarted when a 70-year-old homeowner and his 69-year-old friend intervened. At the High Court in Glasgow, the 32-year-old heroin addict, McLeod, pleaded guilty to assault. Judge Lady Scott commended the bravery of the elderly men. The court learned that in May of the previous year, McLeod, posing as an East Lothian council worker, attempted to enter Ronald Cormack’s home in Lauriston Place in Edinburgh. Pretending there was an emergency and needing to shut off the water, McLeod, wearing a high-visibility vest and showing a fake ID, managed to push past Cormack. Inside, McLeod fought with Cormack and produced a knife. Cormack and his companion Jack Cairns then tackled the intruder; one pushed him to the door while the other called the police. Lady Scott stated: "The elderly men were enjoying a peaceful evening when you tried to enter their home, falsely claiming there was an emergency and you needed to turn off the water. When that didn’t work, you forcibly entered the house with the intention of robbing them." She noted that when faced with resistance from the two older men, McLeod gave up and was escorted out of the house. She added, "Your two victims were both 70 and 69 years old, fortunately without any injuries." The court also revealed McLeod’s extensive criminal history, including an assault and robbery conviction from 2006, for which he served 42 months in prison. Defense attorney Matt Jackson remarked, "He cannot sufficiently apologize to the victims for forcing his way into their home."
Why are some children gaining better access to educational opportunities? Our report, alongside analyses by Alan Milburn and others in the Social Mobility and Child Poverty Commission, reflects Ofsted's annual concerns. These findings align closely with the government's belief that converting schools into academies is crucial for success, though evidence suggests a more complex picture due to varied circumstances during conversions. Milburn previously introduced Foundation Trust hospitals, akin to academy status. The report, however, highlights a discrepancy regarding teachers' pay, noting that starting salaries for new teachers are lagging behind those of other graduates and their counterparts in similar developed economies. The median starting salary for top graduates is £30,000, compared to £27,800 in London and about £5,000 less elsewhere. The Commission urges higher wages for new teachers, as the body advising the government on teachers' pay reviews applications. Teaching unions advocate for raises beyond the 1% cap set by George Osborne’s spending review, citing recruitment challenges and anecdotal evidence from head teachers about difficulties in hiring qualified staff. The government plans to recruit 1,500 elite teachers for struggling areas but may need to deploy them in clusters to be effective. Ministers aim to emphasize long-term benefits like job satisfaction and future pay. International research indicates that teachers taking on more responsibilities are often better compensated over time. Despite these efforts, it remains uncertain whether the pay review body will heed the unions’ call to make exceptions to public pay constraints for teachers.
A study from Leicester University revealed that stillbirth and neonatal death rates were decreasing throughout the UK, with Scotland leading the pack. The stillbirth and neonatal mortality rate in Scotland stands at 4.72 per 1,000 live births, compared to 4.3 in Nordic countries, which are often seen as benchmarks for neonatal survival. For the UK as a whole, the rate for stillbirths and deaths within 28 days of birth in 2015 was 5.61 per 1,000 live births, a decline from 6.04 in 2013. Scotland saw its rate fall from 5.43 to 4.72, making it the lowest among UK nations. Mary Ross-Davie, the director of the Royal College of Midwives in Scotland, described the findings as "good news." She attributed this progress to initiatives like increased dialogue with pregnant women about smoking and monitoring fetal movements, as well as programs for healthcare providers to track baby growth and respond promptly to issues. Ross-Davie noted that Scotland initiated a national stillbirth group in 2013 and established the Maternity Care Quality Improvement Collaborative, positioning the country as a leader in addressing stillbirth rates. She acknowledged that socioeconomic factors, particularly deprivation, play a significant role in these rates and highlighted the importance of continuing efforts to reduce stillbirths and neonatal deaths. Professor Elizabeth Draper, the report's author, reported an 8% decrease in stillbirth rates across the UK, with Scotland starting from a lower base and thus achieving a lower rate. The goal remains to further reduce stillbirths and align the UK’s rates with those of the Nordics.

Strategically positioned along the route of the Great Silk Road, Armenia has been influenced by various cultural forces and empires throughout its history. Following independence from the Soviet Union in 1991, the country found itself entangled in a violent conflict with Azerbaijan over the predominantly Armenian-speaking region of Nagorno-Karabakh. Known for being one of the earliest Christian civilizations, Armenia's first churches were established in the fourth century. Over subsequent centuries, it alternated between Byzantine, Persian, Mongol, and Turkish rule, as well as periods of self-governance. With a population of 3.1 million and an area of 29,743 square kilometers (11,484 square miles), the nation's primary languages are Armenian and Russian, and its main religion is Christianity. Life expectancy stands at 71 years for men and 77 years for women. Key exports include diamonds, machinery, and food products. Serge Sarkisian, the current president, assumed office after winning elections in 2008 and secured a second term in 2013. Prior to his presidency, Sarkisian served as a Soviet soldier, worked in the defense committee of the Nagorno-Karabakh Republic, and held positions as minister of defense, national security chief, and head of the presidential staff. In 2009, he signed a significant agreement to restore diplomatic relations with Turkey, though this improved relationship was short-lived due to opposition from nationalist groups. Armenia's leading medium remains television, with about 25 private stations operating alongside two public networks. Despite the availability of major Russian networks, print media is less relied upon, with small circulation numbers and many titles controlled by influential individuals or political parties. Although a media law bans censorship, journalists have faced legal repercussions for libel and defamation. Key historical milestones include the 1915-1917 massacres and deportations of Armenians in Anatolia, which Armenia views as genocide, a claim disputed by Turkey. In 1918, independent Armenia emerged after the collapse of the Ottoman Empire during World War I. By 1922, Armenia became part of the Soviet Union. The conflict over Nagorno-Karabakh began in 1989 and culminated in a ceasefire brokered by Russia in 1994, though a lasting peace agreement has not been reached. Armenia declared independence from the Soviet Union in 1991.
Nias, a male Malayan tapir, was born to Malacca and Hunter at Port Lympne Reserve near Hythe. With only about 1,500 to 2,000 Malayan tapirs remaining, Nias' birth is a significant milestone. "His arrival is wonderful news for the survival of Malayan tapirs and our skilled keepers," stated director Adrian Harland. "We're particularly proud of our breeding achievements with this species." These tapirs typically inhabit southeast Asia, particularly the forests and rainforests. Their decline is attributed to hunting, illegal logging, and deforestation. Known scientifically as Tapirus indicus, the Malayan tapir is also called the Asian tapir and is the largest of the four tapir species, closely related to horses and rhinoceroses. They are "odd-toed" ungulates, with four toes on the front feet and three on the rear, each ending in a hoof. They have a gestation period of about 13 months, and newborns are born with white spots on dark skin; as they mature, their coloration darkens for better camouflage in their forest habitats. Since 1989, Port Lympne Reserve has successfully bred 11 Malayan tapirs, establishing itself as one of Europe's leading centers for this species.
Following acid attacks in London last month, which targeted delivery riders using bicycles or mopeds, more than 70 Deliveroo riders have since reported avoiding deliveries due to safety concerns. To address these issues, Deliveroo is introducing a new app feature enabling riders to report security concerns and is testing helmet cameras to collect evidence. In the previous month, two of Deliveroo’s riders and three others were injured when acid was thrown at them in north London. As Deliveroo drivers are self-employed, they do not receive sick pay if attacked and must use their personal vehicles for deliveries. The company has enhanced its app to let riders flag safety issues at specific delivery locations, which are then shared with headquarters and local authorities. Additionally, Deliveroo is piloting helmet-mounted cameras in Hackney, East London, a hotspot for rider incidents. The company is also recruiting 50 new staff members focused on rider safety. Jabed Hussein, an UberEats rider who was one of the first victims, described his experience to the BBC’s Victoria Derbyshire program, saying, “It felt like being set on fire; I was screaming on the street.” He added, “This job shouldn’t put your life at risk. Many drivers now stop working by 10 PM, considering it too dangerous afterward. Before, we worked until midnight. Now, I feel like I’m operating in a jungle, anything could happen.” Deliveroo’s managing director, Dan Warne, stated, “Our riders’ safety is our top priority, and we are committed to enhancing our efforts to ensure they feel secure at all times.”

The British Film Institute (BFI) reported a 17% increase in box office takings to £1.24 billion from the previous year. Cinema admissions grew by 9%, with 171.9 million tickets sold, and film production spending reached £1.41 billion, the second-highest amount on record. This marks the first year since 2012 when both admissions and box office revenues increased. In 2015, five films earned over £40 million at the UK box office, compared to just one in 2014. The Force Awakens topped the charts with £114 million, making it the UK's highest-grossing film ever. Spectre, the latest James Bond movie, was second with £94.7 million, followed by Jurassic World with £64.5 million, The Avengers: Age of Ultron with £48.3 million, and Minions in fifth place with £47.7 million. UK independent films secured an 11% market share in 2015, their third-highest on record, with eight films earning over £5 million. The highest-grossing independent film was Tom Hardy’s Legend, which earned £18.4 million, and The Second Best Exotic Marigold Hotel came in second with £16 million. Film production spending in the UK decreased by 6% in 2015, totaling £1.41 billion, down from the record £1.49 billion in 2014. Amanda Nevill, CEO of the BFI, stated, "These numbers indicate a thriving era for British cinema, reinforcing its economic importance, and highlight the success of a diverse array of British films despite challenging times for independent and specialized films." Culture minister Ed Vaizey described the figures as "excellent news."

A Freedom of Information request by the Liberal Democrats revealed that since 2013, 11,000 individuals waited more than 10 days for a specialist appointment. Party leader Kirsty Williams described the situation as "shocking and disappointing." A Welsh Government spokesperson noted that despite an increase in cancer diagnoses, survival rates were improving. The goal is for 95% of patients initially suspected of having cancer to begin definitive treatment within 62 days of diagnosis. In June 2013, Health Minister Mark Drakeford emphasized that ensuring patients received their first hospital appointment within 10 days of referral was crucial to meeting this target. Between October 2013 and September 2015, over 22,000 people were referred by their GPs with suspected breast cancer; 2,249 were diagnosed, and 92.3% of them began treatment within 62 days. Betsi Cadwaladr and Cardiff and Vale health boards did not provide specific details, but Betsi Cadwaladr reported that the average wait for a cancer specialist appointment at Ysbyty Glan Clwyd and Ysbyty Gwynedd was 21 days. Powys Teaching health board refers its patients to neighboring health boards. According to the latest data for the three months ending December, 83.7% of cancer patients started treatment within 62 days. Williams stated, "It's profoundly distressing for individuals who spend so much time worrying about their health and future. It also highlights our need to enhance capacity within cancer services to ensure timely appointments." The Welsh Government spokesperson explained that one-stop diagnostic clinics often occur during the first outpatient appointment for suspected breast cancer cases, allowing immediate treatment. An Aneurin Bevan health board spokesperson added that their waiting times from referral to clinic visits had decreased to 19 days over the past three months, and all patients with suspected malignancies were being seen and treated within the 62-day target set by the Welsh Government.

The Ministry of Justice (MoJ) announced that the Grade II listed, closed-in-2013 prison would be utilized as a venue for the town's 2016 Year of Culture events. This initiative aims to unite local arts groups and position the town as a cultural destination. Alex Brennan from Reading UK organizers described it as a "fabulous" addition to the Year of Culture program. The MoJ stated they would collaborate with a third party to prepare the site for hosting an artistic season in the fall, with no extra costs to taxpayers. Reading East MP Rob Wilson noted that repurposing the prison would help celebrate the town’s cultural and historical importance. Mr. Brennan added, "It holds a central place in Reading's history. It would be a marvellous and rather unusual venue, and I truly hope something comes of it." The prison is linked to several notable figures, including Oscar Wilde, who wrote The Ballad Of Reading Gaol while incarcerated there, and Roderick Maclean, who attempted to assassinate Queen Victoria in 1882. Archaeological investigations are planned before a development plan is established, and Reading Borough Council hopes to acquire the site, having previously criticized the MoJ for its indecision regarding its long-term future. The abandoned jail incurred the MoJ approximately £262,000 in 2014/15 for security and utility expenses.
Sgt Craig Nicholas Jones claimed he was unjustly convicted by magistrates because the lights on two 50mph signs on the A55 at Old Colwyn, Conwy, were not functioning. However, judges at the High Court in London ruled that the signs would still have been visible in the headlights of an unmarked police car. Consequently, the 90-day driving ban previously imposed by magistrates has been reinstated. Sgt Jones was on duty when his vehicle was recorded traveling at 98mph on the road between Llanddulas and Colwyn Bay on April 1, 2009. His legal team argued that since the internal lights of the signs were out, he should have been acquitted at the magistrates' court. They also stated that there was a clear policy not to enforce the speed limit when the signs were not operational. Nevertheless, Mr Justice Charles and Mr Justice Wyn Williams determined that "reasonable people" would conclude that not prosecuting Sgt Jones would undermine the justice system. The judges noted that despite the malfunctioning lights, the signs were correctly placed and adhered to all relevant regulations. They added that the signs were clearly visible from the car's headlights, and established case law confirmed that minor regulatory breaches did not affect the legality of the speed limit. Sgt Jones’s appeal was rejected, and the 90-day driving ban, initially set in December 2009 but temporarily suspended, will now commence on January 31.

Deborah Streatfield from MyBigCareer states that it is "unfair" that students aspiring to study medicine are required to pay for work experience to enhance their university applications. The Chartwell Trust, which manages a private hospital in Essex, offers a one-week program called "Chartwell Work Experience For Medical School Applications." This program promises an "in-depth insight" into hospital and healthcare work, including shadowing staff and receiving "valuable medical experience" to aid Ucas applications. The trust also runs care homes, and the brochure mentions that participants will rotate through these facilities. According to the trust, the placement provides one-on-one meetings with senior management, consultants, and directors to offer insights into healthcare. The fee covers administrative costs and coaching to boost participants' confidence and self-assurance for their future healthcare careers. However, Streatfield argues that such paid work experience acts as a barrier to equal opportunities for less privileged students. MyBigCareer plans to introduce free work experience placements with 43 GP practices in Hackney and the City of London. The Medical Schools Council has noted concerns about the lack of diverse applicants to medicine, with many schools failing to produce any medicine applicants in recent years.

Examples of randomly parked bicycles in Cambridge have been shared as part of the force's #badlyparkedbike campaign to encourage more careful cycling habits. Insp Matt Johnson from Cambridgeshire Police noted that such sights are not uncommon. According to statistics, 58% of residents in Cambridge cycle at least once a month. Johnson commented, "While these photos may seem unbelievable, similar scenarios are frequently observed. Such parking creates obstructions, making it challenging for others to access their bikes, and in some instances, it has led to injuries when people attempt to retrieve their bicycles." He advised, "Whenever possible, use designated bike racks, which are specifically designed to deter theft when paired with a secure lock." Some respondents acknowledged the danger of poor parking, while others pointed out the need for additional bike parking facilities. User Al Storer suggested that the campaign highlights a significant lack of available bike parking spots. Insp Johnson acknowledged the extensive cycle parking infrastructure in the city but emphasized that the issue of abandoned bicycles occupying spaces remains persistent.
The UK's Intellectual Property Office (IPO) supported Swatch's opposition, initially filed in March 2014, regarding the name "iWatch." Swatch argued that "iWatch" was too similar to its existing trademarks for "iSwatch" and "Swatch." By the time Apple launched its smartwatch in 2015, the company had renamed it "Apple Watch." However, if Apple wishes to use "iWatch" in the UK in the future, it will be permitted only for certain products, such as computer software and accessories, but not for computer hardware, monitors, audio/video devices, or cameras. This decision was reached after a hearing in April. Trademark law is territorial, meaning each country requires separate registration. This reality likely influenced Apple's choice not to use "iWatch" globally. Swatch did not provide further comment on the decision.

Daphne, a three-year-old deer, was discovered at Waters' Edge Country Park in Barton on Wednesday. She was too severely injured to save and had to be euthanized, according to park staff. The park, which spans 110 acres, offered a £200 reward for information leading to a prosecution. Daphne frequently stayed near the visitor center after people began feeding her. Staff suggest she may have been attacked by a dog and are advising that dogs should be kept on leads or under close control at all times. Anyone with information regarding the incident is encouraged to contact the visitor center.
For three years, photographer Joanne Coates has documented the lives of Scotland's fishing industry workers, immortalizing the salt-soaked men who spend their days at sea. Raised in a rural part of northern England, Coates found early inspiration in her weekly family trips to coastal towns like Scarborough, Whitby, and Bridlington. "I remember rubber boots, nets, and snippets of seafarers' conversations," she recalls. "Frayed jumpers, big smiles—there was something special about it all." Her photos reveal a world of demanding labor, long hours, and harsh conditions. In 2016, more lives were lost at sea in the first half of the year than the entire previous year, according to the Fishermen's Mission charity, which reports an average of 15 fishermen killed or seriously injured annually. In Orkney, fishing is a way of life for many men. Coates focuses on the Orkney Fishermen's Society, a cooperative founded in 1953 that primarily catches brown crab and lobster. This society collaborates closely with researchers to sustainably manage their fishing grounds, including the home of Europe's largest lobster hatchery, which releases over 100,000 lobsters yearly. Coates' evocative images capture the daily routines of these fishermen, navigating an uncertain future.

At the Battle of the Somme, they acted as stretcher-bearers, organized concerts to boost morale, and even used their bass drum as a communion table. Lance Corporal Joseph Scott from Moore Street and Rifleman George Mills from Pine Street were among those who fell on July 1, with many others losing their lives thereafter. Formed in 1856, the Hamilton Band is one of Northern Ireland's oldest bands. It served as the core of the regimental band for the 10th Battalion of the Royal Inniskilling Fusiliers. After training in Ireland, the battalion moved to England and then to France, where they faced combat and trench warfare for the first time. Post-war, surviving members rebuilt the band despite most instruments being taken to the front. The original drums eventually returned, now displayed in St Columb's Cathedral's Chapter House. "This bass drum was used throughout the First World War and once served as a communion table on the battlefield," noted Ian Bartlett, St Columb's Cathedral's curator. Robert McGonagle, the Hamilton Flute Band's secretary, highlighted the courage of those who joined and ventured into the unknown. "Would such dedication and bravery be replicated today?" Those who gave their lives will be honored at Derry's war memorial, and a special drum service at St Columb's Cathedral will commemorate the band's contribution. A piece of music was commissioned to reflect the band's journey, including their march to the war and actions over the trenches.

Nkurunziza took office in 2005, following a 12-year conflict between ethnic Hutus and Tutsis that claimed 300,000 lives.

Brain Injury Matters noted uncertainties in the figures due to the absence of a dedicated children's brain injury unit. They emphasized the importance of early detection, as initial symptoms might not be obvious and can only become apparent as the brain matures. Janet McCullough recounted how her son, Eric, who was eight when he fell ill, underwent a kidney infection that progressed to encephalitis, causing brain swelling. "He received antibiotics, but his body reacted by attacking his brain," Janet explained. "He complained of a painful head, sensitivity to light, and nausea. He was hospitalized at Causeway Hospital and later at the Royal Victoria Hospital, but when he came out, he was a different child." One year later, the family is working to raise awareness through Brain Injury Matters. Janet shared that her son now tires easily, both physically and mentally, and struggles with concentration. "Once a bright child, he now has dyslexia and requires significant assistance," she said. "Yet, he remains my son—my wonderful boy." Acquired brain injuries have become more common as advancements in medical care have reduced mortality rates. These injuries can result from various causes, including accidents, infections, lack of oxygen, or strokes. They tend to peak in early childhood, between ages four and 15, and are more frequent in males. Fiona McCabe, the CEO of Brain Injury Matters, indicated that the charity assists numerous families. "Based on evidence from other parts of the UK and anecdotal information, we estimate there could be around 1,000 new cases of brain injury in children annually in Northern Ireland," she stated. "Without knowing the exact number of children with brain injuries, resource allocation becomes challenging." Janet highlighted the support her family receives from both Brain Injury Matters and the Child Brain Injury Trust, noting that this assistance helps her other children adapt to their changed brother.

The BBC has seen documents indicating that the parliament's Budgetary Control Committee is questioning allowances given to Markus Engels, an adviser to former EP President Martin Schulz. Engels claimed residence in Brussels in 2012 while living in Berlin, a practice that the SPD party, Schulz's political affiliation, described as "normal" for Brussels. The independent EU anti-fraud office Olaf, which previously investigated Marine Le Pen's expenses, is examining media reports about the employment conditions of certain European Parliament staff members, without naming them. Olaf will assess whether there is sufficient evidence of fraud, corruption, or illegal activity affecting the EU's financial interests before deciding whether to launch an investigation. Schulz served as EP President from 2012 until recently, when he returned to Germany to focus on the September general election. In January 2012, Schulz sought authorization for Engels' long-term mission in Berlin to support his presidency. The Budgetary Control Committee is probing why Brussels was chosen as Engels' place of employment, despite no travel costs occurring, and who made this decision. They are also inquiring into the selection process for Engels' role. A formal response from the committee is anticipated early next month.
Hundreds of devoted fans gathered at Islington's historic Union Chapel to honor the memory of the 69-year-old musician who passed away from cancer on January 10th. The service featured performances by a diverse array of artists, such as the Magic Numbers and former Sex Pistols bassist Glen Matlock, as they played Bowie's songs. Two days prior to his death, Bowie had released his final album, Blackstar. Following his passing, numerous tribute concerts were organized worldwide, and several of his popular tracks re-entered the UK chart. Inside the small north London chapel, Bowie's well-known melodies echoed as an organ played, while a large screen displayed images of the artist throughout his career. The event began with a lively group sing-along of "Starman." Maggi Ronson, sister of late Bowie guitarist and collaborator Mick Ronson, sang "Ziggy Stardust" alongside The Featherz, acknowledging Bowie's significant impact. Guy Pratt, a former bass player for Pink Floyd and Roxy Music, praised Bowie as the "greatest artist" he'd ever known before performing "Life on Mars" with The Feeling. The concert, streamed live on YouTube, spanned five hours, featuring Bowie's hits, clips of the artist's interviews and music videos, and tributes from journalists, authors, and colleagues. Quirky moments included the performance of "Where Are We Now?" on a saw and "Wild Is the Wind" on a harp. Flowers and candles adorned the dimly lit, smoky hall, which also serves as a working church and venue for music and comedy. Fans posted personal tributes and messages on a wall of remembrance, with one message reading, "Thank you for giving my son the freedom to be himself." Another said, "RIP David, the soundtrack to my life." Comedian David Baddiel expressed his disbelief at Bowie's passing, stating, "He is, was, the greatest tunesmith we have." Organizers noted the event was an opportunity for those inspired by Bowie's work to pay their respects, emphasizing it was "for fans by fans." They highlighted how Bowie remained relevant and engaging despite his legendary status. Ticket sales covered the show's costs, and a donation for Macmillan Cancer Support exceeded £2,000.

In March, plans to eliminate one of Wrexham’s fire engines were abandoned by North Wales Fire and Rescue Authority, pending after the May elections. The new authority hasn’t ruled out such cuts as it formulates its three-year budget plan. Deputy Chief Fire Officer Dawn Docx stated that any reductions would be a “last resort.” Although no plans exist to cut front-line services, the Fire Brigades Union (FBU) remains concerned about potential cuts, especially as the authority seeks to address budget constraints. Cerith Griffiths from the FBU expressed worry that eliminating the engine would compel firefighters to violate safety protocols to save lives. According to the service’s rules, two engines are required for house fires, and cutting the second engine would force firefighters to decide between waiting critical minutes or entering a burning building alone, jeopardizing their safety. He noted, “In situations like Grenfell, firefighters disregarded protocols; without that second engine, we shouldn’t enter properties.” Griffiths emphasized, “Firefighters push boundaries to save lives, but waiting half an hour for backup can mean a lot when every second counts.” This could endanger lives. Proposals to reduce one of Wrexham’s two fire engines and 24 firefighter positions to cover a £900,000 funding shortfall by 2020 were withdrawn in March due to public opposition. The station is the only one in north Wales equipped with three engines: two full-time and one part-time. As the new authority prepares its budget, it’s gathering public input before releasing its preliminary financial plan in November. No concrete proposals have been submitted yet, but the authority is exploring ways to save costs without impacting frontline staff. Should the budget gap remain unbridged, the authority might consider reducing the number of engines, leading to longer wait times for backup. Docx clarified, “We’d have to weigh whether to wait for the second engine or proceed,” adding, “This is not something senior officers desire, but if we can’t close the budget gap, this could be an option.”

The ten-child mother, who was Protestant, hailed originally from East Belfast and converted to Catholicism upon marrying Arthur McConville. Following intimidation, the family relocated to West Belfast and settled in the Divis Flats on the Lower Falls Road. Her husband passed away in 1971. She was abducted by the IRA in December 1972, with speculation that she was taken after reportedly assisting an injured British soldier, or that she was an informer, though these claims were later disproven. In a recent BBC documentary, one of her daughters, Agnes, recounted hearing her mother scream as she was taken by several men and women into a van. That was the last time they saw her. Believed to have been held at multiple residences before her execution, her disappearance garnered significant media coverage leading up to Christmas, with her children seeking information about her fate. The community received assurances that she was merely 'lying low,' and her case eventually faded from public attention. For years, the IRA denied involvement in her disappearance. It was the establishment of the Independent Commission for the Location of Victims' Remains in 1999 that reignited interest in her case. This body, initiated by the British and Irish governments, aimed to secure confidential information to aid in locating the remains of those who had vanished during the Troubles. The IRA acknowledged that year that it had killed and secretly buried nine of the Disappeared, including Jean McConville. Her body was discovered in 2003 on Shelling Hill Beach in County Louth, Republic of Ireland, and identified through a bullet wound to the head. Subsequently, the IRA apologized for causing the families of the Disappeared further pain and suffering.
